← 2.3 DTFT2.5 Z-Transform →

2.5 Z-Transform

A unified analysis framework for discrete-time systems

Why does this matter? Because the Z-transform is the all-purpose tool for digital system analysis. Determining filter stability, reading the frequency response from a pole-zero plot, designing IIR filters — all of it relies on the Z-transform. Its role in discrete systems is equivalent to that of the Laplace transform in continuous systems.

Previously... The DFT/FFT in 2.4 is a computational tool. But to analyze discrete system stability and design digital filters, we need a more powerful framework — the Z-transform.

One-sentence summary: The Z-Transform is the "all-purpose tool" for digital systems — stability, frequency response, and filter design all rely on it.

Learning Objectives

  • Define the Z-transform and the Region of Convergence (ROC)
  • Understand that the DFT = Z-transform sampled on the unit circle
  • Use pole-zero analysis to determine stability and frequency response
  • Design simple IIR filters

Pain Point: Core Questions for Digital Filters

You have designed a digital filter with the following difference equation:

$$y[n] = 0.5\,y[n-1] + x[n] - 0.3\,x[n-1]$$
  • Is it stable? (Will the output blow up?)
  • What does its frequency response look like? (Which frequencies are amplified and which are attenuated?)
  • If I want to completely eliminate 1 kHz (a notch), how should I modify it?

These questions are difficult to answer using time-domain difference equations. The Z-transform converts difference equations into algebraic equations, making everything clear.

Historical context: The Z-transform is the discrete counterpart of the Laplace transform. In continuous systems, the Laplace transform converts differential equations into algebraic equations, with the variable $s$ residing in the complex plane. The Z-transform does the same thing but for discrete systems: the variable $z$ also resides in the complex plane, and $z = e^{sT_s}$ maps the $s$-plane to the $z$-plane. Lotfi Zadeh (later famous for fuzzy logic) and John Ragazzini introduced the modern form of the Z-transform in 1952.

Principle: Definition and Region of Convergence

Intuition first: The DTFT evaluates a sequence along the frequency axis ($e^{j\omega}$, the unit circle). The Z-transform extends the evaluation from the unit circle to the entire complex plane. This additional degree of freedom lets us analyze stability, causality, and other properties that the DTFT cannot directly reveal.

Z-Transform Definition

$$X(z) = \sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty}x[n]\,z^{-n}, \quad z \in \mathbb{C}$$

When $z = e^{j\omega}$ (unit circle): $X(e^{j\omega}) = \text{DTFT}$

When $z = e^{j2\pi k/N}$ ($N$ equally spaced points on the unit circle): $X[k] = \text{DFT}$

Region of Convergence (ROC)

The set of $z$ values for which the series $\sum|x[n]||z|^{-n}$ converges. The shape of the ROC determines the properties of the sequence:

Sequence TypeROC ShapeExample
Finite-length (FIR)Entire $z$-plane (possibly excluding $z=0$ or $z=\infty$)$x[n] = \delta[n] - 0.5\delta[n-1]$
Causal right-sided sequenceExterior of a circle: $|z| > r_{\max}$$x[n] = a^n u[n]$, ROC: $|z|>|a|$
Anti-causal left-sided sequenceInterior of a circle: $|z| < r_{\min}$$x[n] = -a^n u[-n-1]$, ROC: $|z|<|a|$

Stability criterion: A causal LTI system is stable $\iff$ the ROC includes the unit circle $\iff$ all poles are inside the unit circle ($|p_i| < 1$). This is the sole criterion for judging digital filter stability.

Pole-Zero Analysis

For a rational transfer function (IIR filter):

$$H(z) = \frac{B(z)}{A(z)} = \frac{b_0 + b_1 z^{-1} + \cdots + b_M z^{-M}}{1 + a_1 z^{-1} + \cdots + a_N z^{-N}} = b_0\frac{\prod_{k=1}^{M}(1 - q_k z^{-1})}{\prod_{k=1}^{N}(1 - p_k z^{-1})}$$

$q_k$: zeros, $p_k$: poles

Frequency response intuition: $H(e^{j\omega})$ is the result of walking around the unit circle and seeing how far each point is from the poles and zeros.

  • A pole near the unit circle amplifies that frequency (resonance). The closer the pole, the sharper the peak.
  • A zero near the unit circle attenuates that frequency (notch). A zero on the unit circle = complete cancellation.
  • A pole outside the unit circle means the system is unstable.
Why do poles cause resonance?

Near $\omega = \theta_p$ (the pole angle):

$$|H(e^{j\omega})| \approx \frac{|b_0|\prod|e^{j\omega}-q_k|}{\prod_{k\neq i}|e^{j\omega}-p_k| \cdot |e^{j\omega}-p_i|}$$

When $\omega \approx \theta_p$, $|e^{j\omega} - p_i| = |e^{j\omega} - |p_i|e^{j\theta_p}| \approx 1 - |p_i|$ (very small).

Therefore $|H| \approx \frac{C}{1-|p_i|}$. As $|p_i| \to 1$, the gain approaches infinity.

The 3-dB bandwidth of the peak $\approx 2(1-|p_i|)$ radians. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Designing Filters from Pole-Zero Plots

  1. Notch filter (eliminate a specific frequency): Place zeros at $z = e^{j\omega_0}$ and $z = e^{-j\omega_0}$, paired with nearby poles $z = r\,e^{\pm j\omega_0}$ ($r < 1$, e.g., $r = 0.95$) to control the notch width.
  2. Resonator (boost a specific frequency): Place poles at $z = r\,e^{\pm j\omega_0}$; the closer $r$ is to 1, the sharper the resonance. $Q$ factor $\approx \frac{\omega_0}{2(1-r)}$.
  3. Stability check: Are all poles $|p_k| < 1$? If any pole is on or outside the unit circle, the system is unstable. Use MATLAB's zplane(b,a) or Python's scipy.signal.tf2zpk.

Application Scenarios

  • IIR filter design: Butterworth, Chebyshev, and Elliptic filters are all designed by placing poles and zeros in the $s$-plane, then mapping to the $z$-plane using the bilinear transform $s = \frac{2}{T_s}\frac{1-z^{-1}}{1+z^{-1}}$. For example, a 5th-order Butterworth low-pass has 5 poles uniformly distributed on the left semicircle of the $s$-plane.
  • Control system stability: The poles of the closed-loop transfer function $H_{cl}(z)$ of a digital PID controller must all be inside the unit circle. If a pole has $|p| = 0.98$ (very close but still inside), the system is stable but will exhibit slowly decaying oscillations.
  • Audio equalizer (EQ): Each band of a parametric EQ consists of a pair of conjugate poles and a pair of conjugate zeros. The center frequency is determined by the pole-zero angle, the $Q$ factor by the radius, and the gain by the distance ratio from zeros to poles.

Interactive: Pole-Zero Plot and Frequency Response

Adjust the pole position (conjugate pair $p = re^{\pm j\theta}$) and observe how the frequency response (= Z-transform evaluated on the unit circle) changes. The closer the radius is to 1, the sharper the resonance peak.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • The ROC must be specified: The same algebraic expression $X(z)$ with different ROCs corresponds to different time-domain sequences. For example, $X(z) = 1/(1-az^{-1})$: ROC $|z|>|a|$ gives the causal exponential $a^n u[n]$; ROC $|z|<|a|$ gives the anti-causal $-a^n u[-n-1]$.
  • FIR filters are always stable: FIR filters have no feedback poles (all poles are at $z=0$), so they are unconditionally stable. This is the biggest advantage of FIR over IIR.
  • Numerical precision: The closer a pole is to the unit circle, the more sensitive the IIR filter is to coefficient quantization. In a 16-bit fixed-point implementation, poles with $|p| > 0.99$ can lead to limit cycle oscillations.

References: [1] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Ch.3-6. [2] Proakis & Manolakis, Digital Signal Processing, Ch.3. [3] Mitra, Digital Signal Processing: A Computer-Based Approach.

Quick Check

Q1: How can you tell from a pole-zero plot whether a frequency is amplified or attenuated?

Show answer

A pole near the unit circle at a certain frequency amplifies that frequency (resonance); a zero near the unit circle attenuates it (notch).

Q2: What is the condition for an IIR filter to be stable?

Show answer

All poles must be inside the unit circle (|z|<1), which is equivalent to the ROC including the unit circle.

← 2.4 DFT/FFT2.6 Sampling Theorem →

2.6 Sampling Theorem

Rigorous derivation of the Shannon theorem from the Poisson summation formula

Why does this matter? Because sampling is the very first step from analog to digital, and aliasing caused by an incorrect sampling rate is an irreversible disaster. Why does a CD use 44.1 kHz? Why does vibration analysis require a 2.56x factor? Why does 5G use oversampling? All of these are direct applications of the Nyquist theorem.

Previously... Up to now we assumed we already have a discrete sequence x[n]. But x[n] is sampled from the analog signal x(t) — how high must the sampling rate be to avoid losing information?

One-sentence summary: If the sampling rate is not high enough, high frequencies masquerade as low frequencies — this is called aliasing, and it is irreversible.

Learning Objectives

  • Derive sampling = spectral periodization (Poisson summation formula)
  • Derive the Nyquist condition $f_s \geq 2f_{\max}$ from periodization
  • Derive the sinc reconstruction (Whittaker-Shannon interpolation) formula
  • Understand anti-aliasing filter design in practical systems

Pain Point: A Recording Engineer's Nightmare

Suppose you record a sound containing a 5000 Hz tone at a sampling rate of $f_s = 8000$ Hz. On playback you hear not 5000 Hz but 3000 Hz (= $8000 - 5000$).

  • 5000 Hz exceeds the Nyquist frequency $f_s/2 = 4000$ Hz
  • It has been "folded" (aliased) to $f_s - 5000 = 3000$ Hz
  • Irreversible: you cannot distinguish between 3000 Hz and 5000 Hz from the recorded data

This is why every ADC must have an anti-aliasing filter (AAF) in front of it. The sampling theorem tells you where to set the cutoff frequency of this filter.

Historical context: Harry Nyquist first stated in his 1928 paper Certain Topics in Telegraph Transmission Theory that sampling at rate $f_s$ can represent at most a signal of bandwidth $f_s/2$. Claude Shannon provided the complete mathematical proof and the sinc reconstruction formula in his foundational 1949 information theory paper. In Russia, V. A. Kotelnikov independently obtained the same result in 1933. For this reason, the theorem is sometimes also called the Nyquist-Shannon-Kotelnikov sampling theorem.

Principle: Rigorous Derivation

Intuition first: Sampling is like using a comb to "stamp" the frequency axis — every $f_s$ apart, a copy of the original spectrum is placed. If the original spectrum is too wide, adjacent copies overlap (aliasing), just like stamps placed too closely together cause the patterns to blur.

From Poisson Summation to the Shannon Theorem

Step 1: Sampling = multiplication by an impulse train

$$x_s(t) = x(t)\cdot\sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty}\delta(t - nT_s) = \sum_n x(nT_s)\,\delta(t-nT_s)$$

Step 2: Frequency domain

The FT of an impulse train is another impulse train: $\mathcal{F}\{\sum_n\delta(t-nT_s)\} = \frac{2\pi}{T_s}\sum_k\delta(\omega-k\omega_s)$, $\omega_s = 2\pi f_s$.

Time-domain multiplication = frequency-domain convolution:

$$X_s(\omega) = \frac{1}{2\pi}X(\omega) * \frac{2\pi}{T_s}\sum_k\delta(\omega-k\omega_s) = \frac{1}{T_s}\sum_{k=-\infty}^{\infty}X(\omega - k\omega_s)$$

Sampling causes periodic repetition of the spectrum, with spacing $\omega_s = 2\pi f_s$.

Step 3: Nyquist condition

If $X(\omega) = 0$ for $|\omega| > \omega_{\max}$ (band-limited signal) and $\omega_s > 2\omega_{\max}$, then adjacent copies do not overlap. The original spectrum can be recovered using an ideal low-pass filter with gain $T_s$ and cutoff frequency $\omega_s/2$:

$$\boxed{f_s \geq 2f_{\max} \quad \text{(Nyquist Rate)}}$$

Step 4: sinc reconstruction (Whittaker-Shannon Interpolation)

The impulse response of an ideal low-pass filter is a sinc function. Therefore:

$$x(t) = \sum_{n=-\infty}^{\infty}x(nT_s)\,\text{sinc}\!\left(\frac{t-nT_s}{T_s}\right)$$

where $\text{sinc}(u) = \frac{\sin(\pi u)}{\pi u}$. Each sample point "grows" a sinc waveform, and the superposition of all sincs precisely reconstructs the original continuous signal. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Sampling Rate Design in Practical Systems

In theory, $f_s \geq 2f_{\max}$ is sufficient. But in practice a higher rate is needed because:

  1. Anti-aliasing filters are not ideal: An ideal low-pass filter (brick-wall) is not realizable. Practical analog LPFs have a transition band, requiring additional margin.
  2. Rule of thumb: $f_s \geq 2.56 \times f_{\max}$ (vibration analysis industry standard), or the more conservative $f_s \geq 3{-}4 \times f_{\max}$.
  3. The anti-aliasing filter is placed before the ADC (in the analog domain), with its cutoff frequency set at or slightly below $f_s/2$.
Application$f_{\max}$$f_s$ (actual)RatioNotes
CD audio20 kHz44.1 kHz2.2xHuman hearing upper limit: 20 kHz
Professional audio20 kHz96 kHz4.8xSimplifies AAF design
Vibration analysis$f_{max}$$2.56 \times f_{max}$2.56xISO/IEC standard
5G baseband100 MHz245.76 MHz2.46x3GPP standard sampling rate
Sigma-Delta ADC$f_b$$64{-}256 \times f_b$64-256xOversampling trades for bit depth

Application Scenarios

  • Audio CD (44.1 kHz): The highest frequency perceived by the human ear is approximately 20 kHz. $44100/20000 = 2.205$. Why 44.1 and not 40? Because margin is needed for the anti-aliasing filter's transition band. The historical reason for 44100 is related to the NTSC video format.
  • Mechanical vibration analysis (2.56x): Monitoring fault frequencies of turbine bearings. If the highest frequency of interest is 10 kHz, sampling rate $= 25.6$ kHz, using an 8th-order Butterworth AAF with cutoff at 10 kHz. $2.56 = 2 \times 1.28$, where the 1.28 margin ensures AAF attenuation $> 60$ dB at the Nyquist frequency.
  • Oversampling ADC: Sigma-Delta ADCs sample at an extremely high rate (e.g., $256 \times f_b$), then use a digital decimation filter to downsample to the target rate. The benefit: the anti-aliasing filter can be very simple (a first-order RC suffices), because the transition band width $\approx 255 \times f_b$.

Interactive: Aliasing Demonstration

An 80 Hz sine wave. Adjust the sampling rate $f_s$. When $f_s < 160$ Hz (Nyquist), observe the aliasing phenomenon — the sampled signal appears to be at a different frequency.

ALIASING!
Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • "A high enough sampling rate eliminates the need for an anti-aliasing filter" — Wrong. Any signal plus ambient noise has theoretically infinite bandwidth. Without an AAF, high-frequency noise will alias into the signal band.
  • "Sinc reconstruction can perfectly recover the signal" — In theory, yes, but the sinc function is infinitely long and cannot be realized. Practical systems use finite-length approximations (such as the Lanczos kernel or polynomial interpolation).
  • Bandpass sampling: If the signal is bandpass (e.g., an RF signal centered at $f_c \pm B/2$), the required sampling rate is only $f_s \geq 2B$ (not $2f_c$). However, the choice of $f_s$ must avoid overlap at the boundaries of spectral copies, requiring more careful calculation.
  • Aliasing is irreversible: Once aliasing occurs, no post-processing (digital filtering, AI, etc.) can recover the original signal. Prevention is the only remedy.

References: [1] Shannon, Communication in the Presence of Noise, Proc. IRE, 1949. [2] Nyquist, Certain Topics in Telegraph Transmission Theory, Trans. AIEE, 1928. [3] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Ch.4.

Worked Example

A signal contains 100 Hz, 250 Hz, and 500 Hz components. (a) What is the minimum sampling rate? (b) If fs=800 Hz, is there aliasing? (c) If fs=900 Hz, where does the 500 Hz component alias to?

Show solution

(a) fs >= 2 x 500 = 1000 Hz

(b) fs=800 < 1000, so 500 Hz will alias. 250 Hz is unaffected (800 > 500).

(c) 500 Hz aliases to |500 - 900| = 400 Hz

Quick Check

Q1: Why is the CD sampling rate 44.1 kHz?

Show answer

The human ear can hear up to ~20 kHz. Nyquist requires >= 40 kHz. 44.1 kHz provides about 10% margin for the anti-aliasing filter's transition band.

Q2: If you forget to add an anti-aliasing filter before sampling, can you fix it afterward?

Show answer

No. Aliasing is irreversible — high frequencies have already disguised themselves as low frequencies, and there is no way to distinguish the true low frequencies from the aliased imposters.

← 2.5 Z-Transform2.7 Transform Relationships Overview →

2.7 Transform Relationships Overview

The complete relationship chain among FS / CTFT / DTFT / DFT / Z-Transform

Why does this matter? Because FS, CTFT, DTFT, DFT, and the Z-transform are not five independent tools — they are five perspectives of the same story. Once you understand the relationships among them, you will never get lost on the question of "which transform should I use."

Previously... We have now learned 5 transforms (FS, CTFT, DTFT, DFT, Z). They appear to be different tools, but in fact they have precise mathematical relationships — understanding this relationship map will keep you from confusing them.

One-sentence summary: FS, CTFT, DTFT, DFT, Z-transform — not five independent tools, but five perspectives of the same story.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the derivation relationships among the five transforms
  • Master the duality principles: "sampling leads to periodization" and "truncation leads to discretization"
  • Choose the correct transform tool based on signal characteristics

Relationship Chain: Five Branches of the Same Tree

Intuition first: All Fourier transforms do the same thing — decompose a signal into frequency components. The differences arise from whether the signal is continuous/discrete, periodic/non-periodic. Two core operations connect them:

Sampling leads to frequency-domain periodization
Truncation / Periodization leads to frequency-domain discretization

Transform Relationship Diagram

FS
Continuous periodic → Discrete		 $\xrightarrow{T \to \infty}$
De-periodization		 CTFT
Continuous non-periodic → Continuous
$\downarrow$ Sampling
Spectral periodization
DTFT
Discrete non-periodic → Continuous periodic		 $\xleftarrow{z = e^{j\omega}}$
Unit circle evaluation		 Z-Transform
Discrete → $z$-plane
$\downarrow$ Truncation to $N$ points
Spectral discretization
DFT
Discrete periodic → Discrete periodic

Complete Comparison Table

TransformTime DomainFrequency DomainConnecting OperationFormula
FSContinuous, periodic $T$Discrete ($c_n$)—$c_n = \frac{1}{T}\int_0^T f(t)e^{-jn\omega_0 t}dt$
CTFTContinuous, non-periodicContinuous$T\to\infty$ (FS → CTFT)$F(\omega) = \int f(t)e^{-j\omega t}dt$
DTFTDiscrete, non-periodicContinuous, $2\pi$-periodicSampling (CTFT → DTFT)$X(e^{j\omega}) = \sum x[n]e^{-j\omega n}$
DFTDiscrete, $N$-periodicDiscrete, $N$-periodicTruncation to $N$ points (DTFT → DFT)$X[k] = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1}x[n]e^{-j2\pi kn/N}$
Z-TransformDiscreteFunction on the $z$-plane$z=e^{j\omega}$ → DTFT$X(z) = \sum x[n]z^{-n}$

Core Insight: Duality Principles

Sampling ↔ Periodization: Sampling in the time domain (multiplication by an impulse train) causes periodization in the frequency domain (spectral repetition). The converse also holds: periodization in the time domain causes discretization in the frequency domain (yielding discrete coefficients $c_n$).

Truncation ↔ Discretization: Truncation in the time domain (multiplication by a finite window) is equivalent to convolution in the frequency domain (convolution with the FT of the window, causing leakage). At the same time, a finite-length $N$ sequence's DTFT can be fully represented by $N$ equally spaced samples — this is the DFT.

The DFT is the result of "double operations": both sampling (time-domain discretization) and truncation (time-domain finiteness), so both the time domain and frequency domain are discrete and periodic. This is why the DFT is the only computable version — because computers can only handle a finite number of discrete values.

How to Use: Choosing the Right Transform Tool

Your Signal / NeedUseReason
Continuous periodic signal (e.g., 60 Hz power)FSDiscrete harmonic structure, THD analysis
Continuous transient signal (theoretical analysis)CTFTProperty derivation, filter theory
Theoretical spectrum of a discrete signalDTFTFIR/IIR frequency response analysis
Discrete finite-length data (actual computation)DFT/FFTThe only computable version
System stability / pole-zero analysisZ-TransformROC for stability, poles for resonance
Continuous system $s$-domain analysisLaplaceContinuous counterpart of the Z-transform

Practical rules of thumb:

  • Theoretical derivation → CTFT / DTFT / Z (pen-and-paper tools)
  • Writing code for spectral computation → DFT/FFT (the only computable version)
  • Analyzing filter stability → Pole-zero plot of the Z-transform
  • Understanding the physical meaning of FFT results → Refer back to DTFT and CTFT theory

"Translation" Examples Between Transforms

The following demonstrates how the same physical problem is translated between different transforms:

Scenario: Designing a digital low-pass filter

  1. CTFT: Define the ideal low-pass $H(\omega) = \text{rect}(\omega/2\omega_c)$; inverse FT yields $h(t) = \text{sinc}$
  2. Sampling: $h[n] = h(nT_s) = \text{sinc}(nT_s \omega_c/\pi)$ → enter the discrete world (DTFT)
  3. Truncation: $h_w[n] = h[n] \cdot w[n]$ (window), length $M$ → finite-length FIR (DFT)
  4. Z-transform: $H(z) = \sum_{n=0}^{M-1}h_w[n]z^{-n}$ (all zeros, no poles → FIR is always stable)
  5. FFT: Use an $N$-point FFT to compute $H(e^{j2\pi k/N})$ to verify the frequency response

Common Confusions

  • DTFT $\neq$ DFT: The DTFT is a continuous frequency function (not directly computable); the DFT is its $N$-point sampling.
  • Z-transform $\neq$ DTFT: The DTFT is a special case of the Z-transform on the unit circle. The ROC information in the Z-transform is "invisible" in the DTFT.
  • FS $\neq$ DFT: The FS is a continuous-time theory; the DFT is for discrete finite-length computation. However, taking the DFT of a periodic signal over an integer number of periods yields a result equivalent to the FS coefficients multiplied by $N$.

References: [1] Oppenheim & Willsky, Signals and Systems, Ch.3-5. [2] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Ch.2-8. [3] Haykin & Van Veen, Signals and Systems.

← 2.6 Sampling Theorem3.1 Periodogram and Windowing →

3.1 Periodogram & Window Functions

The root cause of spectral leakage and the engineering tradeoffs of windowing

Why does this matter? Performing an FFT without windowing is like wearing badly astigmatic glasses to view a spectrum -- sidelobe leakage will completely drown out weak signals. Proper windowing is the first step toward credible spectral analysis.

Previously... Part II gave us the transform tools. But directly feeding a signal into the FFT to compute a spectrum (periodogram) produces results severely distorted by truncation effects. Window functions are the first step to solving this problem.

Learning Objectives

  • Derive that truncation = frequency-domain convolution, and understand the cause of leakage
  • Compare performance metrics of Rectangular / Hann / Hamming / Blackman / Kaiser windows
  • Select the optimal window based on resolution vs. dynamic range requirements
  • Master the statistical properties of the periodogram as a PSD estimator

One-Sentence Summary

A periodogram is simply "feed the signal into an FFT and square the result" -- simple but crude. Window functions are the key to making it less crude.

The Problem: "Ghost Artifacts" in the Spectrum

You analyze a signal with FFT. It contains only a single 100 Hz sine wave, yet the spectrum sprouts a bunch of ghost artifacts at 80, 90, 110, 120 Hz and beyond -- this is spectral leakage.

A worse scenario: you want to detect a weak 1.05 kHz signal next to a strong 1 kHz signal (e.g., harmonic distortion analysis), but the sidelobe leakage completely buries the weak signal. This is not a hardware problem -- it is a mathematical inevitability.

Origin

Arthur Schuster (1898) first proposed the concept of the periodogram for analyzing periodicity in sunspot activity. He directly performed Fourier analysis on observational data, computing the "intensity" at each frequency -- a simple, intuitive idea, but with poor statistical properties (not fully understood until the mid-20th century).

Systematic study of window functions came with Fredric J. Harris (1978) and his classic paper "On the Use of Windows for Harmonic Analysis with the Discrete Fourier Transform". Harris systematically compared the frequency-domain characteristics of over 20 window functions and established engineering criteria for window selection. This paper remains one of the most cited references in the field (Google Scholar citations exceeding 10,000).

Jim Kaiser at Bell Labs developed the Kaiser window based on Bessel functions. Its unique feature is that a single parameter $\beta$ continuously adjusts the tradeoff between main-lobe width and sidelobe attenuation -- transforming window selection from "picking one from a set of fixed windows" to "turning a knob to your desired balance point."

Principle: Why Does Truncation Cause Leakage?

Intuition: You only observe a finite-duration segment of the signal. Mathematically, this is equivalent to multiplying an infinitely long signal by a rectangular function (1 within the observation window, 0 elsewhere). Multiplication in time = convolution in frequency. The spectrum of a rectangular function is a sinc function (with infinitely many sidelobes), so the originally clean spectral lines get "smeared" by the sinc sidelobes -- that is leakage.

Truncation = Multiply by rectangular window = Convolve with sinc in frequency

$$x_w[n] = x[n] \cdot w[n] \;\longleftrightarrow\; X_w(e^{j\omega}) = \frac{1}{2\pi}\,X(e^{j\omega}) * W(e^{j\omega})$$

The DTFT of the rectangular window is the Dirichlet kernel (discrete sinc):

$$W_{\text{rect}}(e^{j\omega}) = e^{-j\omega(N-1)/2}\,\frac{\sin(N\omega/2)}{\sin(\omega/2)}$$

Its first sidelobe is only -13 dB below the main lobe. This means: if a strong signal is present, the energy it leaks into neighboring frequencies is only 13 dB below itself -- completely drowning out any nearby signal weaker than -13 dB relative.

Expand: Why can other windows improve sidelobes?

All windows share the same core design philosophy: sacrifice main-lobe width in exchange for sidelobe attenuation.

The problem with the rectangular window is its abrupt truncation to zero at the edges, causing Gibbs phenomenon in the frequency domain (slow sidelobe decay). If the window function tapers smoothly to zero at the edges (like the cosine shape of the Hann window), the frequency-domain sidelobes decay rapidly.

Mathematically, the Hann window can be expressed as a linear combination of three rectangular windows:

$$w_{\text{Hann}}[n] = 0.5\,w_{\text{rect}}[n] - 0.25\,w_{\text{rect}}[n]\,e^{j2\pi n/(N-1)} - 0.25\,w_{\text{rect}}[n]\,e^{-j2\pi n/(N-1)}$$

Therefore, the Hann window's DTFT is the superposition of three shifted Dirichlet kernels. In the sidelobe region, these three terms approximately cancel each other (destructive interference), causing sidelobes to drop off rapidly.

The cost is that the main lobe widens from 2 bins to 4 bins -- the minimum resolvable frequency separation doubles.

More generally, the higher the order of continuous derivatives at the window edges, the faster the sidelobe decay rate:

$$\text{At edges: } w^{(k)}(0) = w^{(k)}(N-1) = 0 \text{ for } k = 0, 1, \ldots, m \implies \text{sidelobes} \sim O(\omega^{-(m+2)})$$

$\blacksquare$

Periodogram: Definition and Statistical Properties

The simplest spectral estimate: directly take the squared magnitude of the windowed DFT.

$$\hat{S}(\omega) = \frac{1}{NU}\left|\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}w[n]\,x[n]\,e^{-j\omega n}\right|^2, \quad U = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}|w[n]|^2$$
Expand: Proof that the periodogram is an inconsistent estimator

Expected value of the periodogram:

$$E[\hat{S}(\omega)] = \frac{1}{2\pi}S(\omega) * |W(\omega)|^2$$

This is the convolution of the true PSD $S(\omega)$ with the window power spectrum -- biased, but as $N \to \infty$, $|W|^2$ approaches a delta function and bias $\to$ 0.

However, the variance is the problem. It can be shown (using Bartlett's formula) that:

$$\text{Var}[\hat{S}(\omega)] \approx S^2(\omega) \quad (\text{does NOT decrease with } N\text{!})$$

The relative standard deviation is $\approx 100\%$, regardless of how large $N$ is. Increasing $N$ only gives you finer frequency grid points with equally wild random fluctuations.

This is why the periodogram is an "inconsistent estimator" -- Welch's method or the Multitaper method is needed to reduce variance. $\;\blacksquare$

Window Function Formulas

Rectangular: $w[n] = 1, \quad 0 \leq n \leq N-1$

Hann: $w[n] = 0.5\!\left(1 - \cos\frac{2\pi n}{N-1}\right)$

Hamming: $w[n] = 0.54 - 0.46\cos\frac{2\pi n}{N-1}$

Blackman: $w[n] = 0.42 - 0.5\cos\frac{2\pi n}{N-1} + 0.08\cos\frac{4\pi n}{N-1}$

Blackman-Harris (4-term): $w[n] = 0.35875 - 0.48829\cos\frac{2\pi n}{N-1} + 0.14128\cos\frac{4\pi n}{N-1} - 0.01168\cos\frac{6\pi n}{N-1}$

Flat-top: Coefficients designed to flatten the main-lobe top, sacrificing frequency resolution for amplitude accuracy ($< 0.01$ dB error)

Kaiser: $w[n] = \frac{I_0\!\left(\beta\sqrt{1 - \left(\frac{2n}{N-1} - 1\right)^2}\right)}{I_0(\beta)}$, where $I_0$ is the modified Bessel function

Five-Window Comparison Table

WindowMain-Lobe Width (bins)Highest Sidelobe (dB)Sidelobe Decay RateENBW (bins)Typical Use
Rectangular2-13-6 dB/oct1.00Transient analysis, frequency resolution priority
Hann4-31-18 dB/oct1.50General-purpose default, audio analysis
Hamming4-42-6 dB/oct1.36Speech analysis, FIR filter design
Blackman6-58-18 dB/oct1.73High dynamic range, radar sidelobe suppression
Kaiser ($\beta$=6)~5-46Adjustable~1.5Adjustable tradeoff, filter design
Flat-top~10-44-6 dB/oct3.77Calibration, precise amplitude measurement

ENBW (Equivalent Noise Bandwidth): Represents how much noise power the window lets through. ENBW = 1.0 is ideal (only the rectangular window achieves this); other windows with ENBW > 1 amplify noise power. When measuring PSD, ENBW correction is required.

How to Use: Window Selection Decision Tree

What is your requirement?

  • Need to resolve two closely spaced frequencies? → Rectangular or Kaiser (small $\beta$, e.g., 2--4)
  • Need to see a weak signal next to a strong one (high dynamic range)? → Blackman or Kaiser (large $\beta$, e.g., 10--14) or Blackman-Harris
  • Need precise amplitude measurement of frequency components? → Flat-top
  • Unsure / general-purpose scenario? → Hann (almost always a safe choice)
  • Want continuously adjustable tradeoff? → Kaiser (adjust $\beta$ from 0 to 20 for continuous coverage of all tradeoffs)

Kaiser $\beta$ Rules of Thumb: $\beta = 0$ → rectangular; $\beta \approx 5$ → approximately Hamming; $\beta \approx 6$ → approximately Hann; $\beta \approx 8.5$ → approximately Blackman; $\beta > 10$ → dynamic range exceeding Blackman.

Applications

  1. THD (Total Harmonic Distortion) Analysis: Measuring harmonic distortion of an audio amplifier with a 1 kHz fundamental, needing to see 2nd and 3rd harmonics 90 dB below the fundamental. Use Hann or Flat-top window (Flat-top amplitude error < 0.01 dB; prefer Flat-top if frequency resolution is sufficient). Industry standard: AES17 specifies Flat-top window for THD+N measurement.
  2. Audio Spectrum Analysis (DAW / Mixer Console): Real-time display of a music signal's spectrum. Typically uses Hann window, NFFT = 4096--8192 (at 44.1 kHz sampling rate, frequency resolution ~5--10 Hz). Hann sidelobes are low enough (-31 dB) to avoid serious display artifacts.
  3. Radar Sidelobe Suppression: In radar returns, a strong target's (e.g., large ship) sidelobes can mask nearby weak targets (e.g., small speedboat). Use Blackman-Harris 4-term window (sidelobes -92 dB) or Dolph-Chebyshev window (equiripple sidelobe design). The cost is a wider main lobe (range resolution drops ~50%), but worthwhile in scenarios demanding extremely high dynamic range.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Forgetting to window = rectangular window = -13 dB sidelobes: This is the most common mistake. Many beginners use np.fft.fft(x) directly without windowing, then wonder why the spectrum looks so "dirty." As long as the signal is not truncated at an exact integer number of periods, there will be leakage.
  • Flat-top window: accurate amplitude but poor frequency resolution: Main-lobe width ~10 bins, 2.5x wider than Hann. Two closely spaced frequencies will merge. Only suitable when frequency components are known and sufficiently spaced (e.g., calibration measurements).
  • Window functions alter the effective data length: Edge data points are down-weighted by the window function. Effective data length $\approx$ ENBW $\times$ $N / f_s$ seconds. Overlap processing can partially compensate for this loss.
  • Coherent gain correction: After windowing, you must divide by the window's mean value (coherent gain = $\frac{1}{N}\sum w[n]$) to correctly read the amplitude of a single frequency component. Forgetting this correction will make amplitude readings too low.

When NOT to Use the Periodogram?

  • Need a stable PSD estimate: Periodogram variance does not decrease with data length → use Welch's method (Section 3.2) or Multitaper
  • Extremely short data (tens of points) with high resolution needed: FFT resolution = $f_s/N$ is insufficient → use AR parametric model (Section 3.3)
  • Need to resolve extremely close sinusoids (super-resolution): Even with windowing, the FFT resolution limit cannot be surpassed → use MUSIC / ESPRIT (Section 3.4)
  • Only interested in a narrow frequency band's details: Full-range FFT wastes computation → use Chirp-Z Transform (Section 3.5)

Interactive: Window Spectrum Comparison

Select a window function and observe its time-domain shape and frequency-domain response (dB scale). Note the tradeoff between main-lobe width and sidelobe height.

References: [1] Schuster, On the Investigation of Hidden Periodicities with Application to a Supposed 26 Day Period of Meteorological Phenomena, Terr. Magn., 1898. [2] Harris, On the Use of Windows for Harmonic Analysis with the DFT, Proc. IEEE, 1978. [3] Kaiser & Schafer, On the Use of the I₀-Sinh Window for Spectrum Analysis, IEEE Trans. ASSP, 1980. [4] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Ch.10.

Worked Example

You need to analyze two sinusoids: 100 Hz (0 dB) and 108 Hz (-35 dB). Sampling rate 1000 Hz, observation duration 0.256 s (256 points). Can you resolve them without windowing? With a Hann window? With a Blackman window?

Show solution

$\Delta f$ = 1000/256 = 3.91 Hz. The frequency separation of 8 Hz > $\Delta f$, so they are theoretically resolvable.

But the weak signal is only -35 dB:

(1) Rectangular window sidelobes -13 dB → leakage from 100 Hz at 108 Hz is ~-13 dB, far stronger than the -35 dB true signal → not visible

(2) Hann window sidelobes -31 dB → leakage -31 dB, still stronger than -35 dB → marginal

(3) Blackman window sidelobes -58 dB → leakage -58 dB, far below -35 dB → clearly visible

Quick Check

Q1: You need to detect a weak signal next to a strong one (40 dB difference). What window should you use?

Show answer

Blackman window (sidelobes -58 dB) or Kaiser (large $\beta$). Hann sidelobes are only -31 dB -- not enough.

Q2: What does performing an FFT without windowing equate to?

Show answer

It equates to using a rectangular window, with sidelobes at only -13 dB -- almost guaranteed to produce severe spectral leakage.

← 2.7 Transform Relationship Overview3.2 Welch & Multitaper →

3.2 Welch's Method & Multitaper Spectral Estimation

Two major approaches to reducing PSD estimation variance

Why does this matter? A single FFT produces a spectrum that looks different every time (high variance), making it unsuitable for setting alarm thresholds or performing statistical comparisons. Welch's method stabilizes the result enough for engineering decisions.

Previously... Section 3.1's windowing solved the leakage problem, but the periodogram still has another fatal flaw: excessive variance (different every time you compute it). How do we stabilize the spectral estimate?

Learning Objectives

  • Understand Welch's method: bias-variance tradeoff of segment-window-average
  • Master the parameter selection process for segment length, overlap ratio, and window
  • Learn about DPSS (Slepian) sequences and the Multitaper method
  • Compare the applicable scenarios of Welch vs. Multitaper

One-Sentence Summary

The core idea of Welch's method is dead simple: split a long data record into multiple segments, compute the spectrum of each, then average them to stabilize the result.

The Problem: An Unstable Spectrum

You compute the PSD of a vibration signal using a periodogram, and the result looks different every time -- wildly fluctuating, resembling noise rather than a clean spectrum. You cannot use such an unstable result to set alarm thresholds for machine monitoring, nor can you reliably compare two measurements.

This is because the periodogram is an inconsistent estimator -- no matter how long the data, the relative fluctuation stays around 100%. You need a way to "smooth out" these fluctuations.

Origin

M.S. Bartlett (1948) first proposed the idea of segment averaging: split the data into $K$ non-overlapping segments, compute the periodogram of each, then average. This straightforwardly reduces variance to $\approx 1/K$.

Peter Welch (1967) at IBM made two key improvements to Bartlett's method: (1) allowing overlap between segments, squeezing more segments from the same data length; (2) applying a window function to each segment (instead of a rectangular window) to reduce leakage. This method became the de facto standard for PSD computation in engineering due to its simplicity and effectiveness. Python's scipy.signal.welch() and MATLAB's pwelch() are both based on this approach.

David Thomson (1982) at Bell Labs proposed a completely different approach -- the Multitaper method: instead of segmenting, use multiple orthogonal windows (DPSS / Slepian sequences) to perform multiple windowed FFTs on the same data, then average. This avoids sacrificing frequency resolution (since no segmenting occurs) and is the gold standard for short-data PSD estimation.

Principle: Welch's Method

Intuition: A single photo may have noise, but averaging $K$ photos yields a clean image. Welch's method does the same for spectra -- split the data into segments, compute a "spectral photo" for each, then average.

Welch PSD Estimate

$$\hat{S}_W(f) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{i=0}^{K-1}\frac{1}{LU}\left|\sum_{n=0}^{L-1}w[n]\,x[n+iD]\,e^{-j2\pi fn/f_s}\right|^2$$

$L$: segment length, $D$: hop size ($D = L - \text{overlap}$), $K$: number of segments, $U = \frac{1}{L}\sum|w[n]|^2$ (window power normalization)

Core Tradeoff (Bias-Variance Tradeoff):

  • More segments $K$ → lower variance ($\approx 1/K$) → more stable spectrum
  • But for a fixed total data length $N$, more $K$ → shorter segments ($L$ smaller) → worse frequency resolution $\Delta f = f_s/L$
  • This is unavoidable: frequency resolution $\times$ stability = constant (determined by total data length $N$)
Expand: Derivation of Welch's equivalent degrees of freedom

Each segment periodogram approximately follows a $\chi^2_2$ distribution (2 degrees of freedom). After averaging $K$ segments:

$$\hat{S}_W(f) \sim \frac{S(f)}{K_{\text{eff}}}\,\chi^2_{2K_{\text{eff}}}$$

where $K_{\text{eff}}$ is the effective number of independent segments. If segments do not overlap (Bartlett), $K_{\text{eff}} = K$. With 50% overlap + Hann window:

$$K_{\text{eff}} \approx \frac{K}{1 + 2\sum_{k=1}^{K-1}(1-k/K)\rho_k^2}$$

where $\rho_k$ is the correlation coefficient of windowed data between the $k$-th pair of adjacent segments. For Hann window with 50% overlap, $\rho_1 \approx 0.167$; more distant segments are nearly uncorrelated.

Empirical conclusion: Hann + 50% overlap yields approximately 1.6 times the equivalent degrees of freedom compared to the non-overlapping version -- squeezing 60% more statistical independence from the same data.

Normalized standard deviation (relative error): $\epsilon = 1/\sqrt{K_{\text{eff}}}$. Engineering rule of thumb: $\epsilon < 0.1$ (i.e., $K_{\text{eff}} > 100$) is considered "stable." $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Four-Step Parameter Selection

Step 1: Determine frequency resolution $\Delta f$

Segment length $L = f_s / \Delta f$. Example: $f_s = 10\,\text{kHz}$, need $\Delta f = 1\,\text{Hz}$ → $L = 10000$ points.

Step 2: Choose overlap ratio

Hann window: 50% overlap ($D = L/2$). Hamming window: 67% overlap ($D = L/3$). Rule of thumb: overlap ratio = $1 - 1/\alpha$, where $\alpha$ is the ENBW.

Step 3: Choose window

Usually Hann. If higher dynamic range is needed, switch to Blackman.

Step 4: Compute number of segments and stability

$K = \lfloor(N - L)/(L - \text{overlap}) + 1\rfloor$. Equivalent degrees of freedom $\nu \approx 2K \times (\text{overlap correction})$. Normalized error $\epsilon \approx 1/\sqrt{K_{\text{eff}}}$.

Concrete Example: Vibration Monitoring

  • Accelerometer sampling rate $f_s = 10\,\text{kHz}$
  • Desired frequency resolution $\Delta f = 1\,\text{Hz}$ → $L = 10000$ points (1 second)
  • Hann window, 50% overlap → $D = 5000$
  • Collect 10 seconds of data ($N = 100000$)
  • Number of segments $K = (100000 - 10000)/5000 + 1 = 19$ segments
  • Equivalent degrees of freedom $\nu \approx 2 \times 19 \times 0.85 \approx 32$ (normalized error $\approx 18\%$)
  • If more stability is needed ($\epsilon < 10\%$), collect 30 seconds of data → $K \approx 59$, $\epsilon \approx 10\%$

Multitaper Method (Thomson, 1982)

Intuition: Welch's method shortens the data to get more segments for averaging, sacrificing resolution. Can we use all the data to preserve resolution and still average multiple estimates? Yes -- by applying different windows to the same data for each FFT. But these windows must be orthogonal, otherwise the results are not independent and averaging has no effect.

DPSS (Discrete Prolate Spheroidal Sequences / Slepian sequences) are exactly such an orthogonal window family: they are sequences with maximal energy concentration within a given bandwidth $NW/N$. The first $K \approx 2NW$ DPSS are nearly perfectly orthogonal, with energy almost entirely concentrated in the target bandwidth.

$$\hat{S}_{MT}(f) = \frac{1}{K}\sum_{k=0}^{K-1}\left|\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}v_n^{(k)}\,x[n]\,e^{-j2\pi fn/f_s}\right|^2$$

$\{v^{(k)}\}_{k=0}^{K-1}$: first $K$ DPSS, $NW$: half-bandwidth parameter (commonly $NW = 3$ or 4)

PropertyWelchMultitaper
Variance reduction methodSegment averagingMulti-window averaging (no segmenting)
Frequency resolution$f_s/L$ ($L$ = segment length < $N$)$2NW \cdot f_s/N$ ($\approx f_s/N$ level)
Equivalent degrees of freedom$\approx 2K$$\approx 2K$ ($K \approx 2NW$)
Best scenarioLong dataShort data (preserves full resolution)
Computational cost$K$ FFTs ($L$-point)$K$ FFTs ($N$-point) + DPSS computation
Implementationscipy.signal.welch()spectrum.pmtm() / nitime

Gold Standard: Multitaper does not require segmenting (preserving full frequency resolution) yet still reduces variance. For short data (hundreds to thousands of points) PSD estimation, it is the acknowledged optimal method. The cost: DPSS must be precomputed (but only once), and $K$ is limited to $\approx 2NW$ (typically 5--8), unlike Welch which can have tens or even hundreds of segments.

Applications

  1. Vibration PSD Trend Monitoring (Welch): A wind turbine gearbox acquires a 60-second acceleration segment every hour ($f_s = 25.6\,\text{kHz}$), using Welch's method ($L = 25600$, 50% overlap, Hann) to compute a stable PSD. Daily comparison of energy in specific PSD frequency bands -- a rising trend triggers early fault warning.
  2. Communication System Noise Floor Measurement (Welch): Measuring the noise power spectral density $N_0$ of an RF receiver. Requires a very stable PSD estimate ($\epsilon < 5\%$), typically using long acquisitions + Welch's method with $K > 400$ segments.
  3. Neuroscience LFP/EEG Power Spectrum (Multitaper): Brain signal trials are typically only 1--2 seconds long. Using Multitaper ($NW = 4$, $K = 7$ tapers) preserves $\sim 2\,\text{Hz}$ resolution while yielding stable PSD estimates. This is the standard practice in the neuroscience community.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Segments too short → frequency blurring: If $\Delta f = f_s/L = 100\,\text{Hz}$, but the two frequencies you want to observe differ by only 50 Hz, they will merge together. Always verify that $L$ provides sufficient $\Delta f$ for your needs.
  • Too few segments → still unstable: $K = 3$ segments give only 6 degrees of freedom; PSD estimates remain highly variable. Practical minimum: $K \geq 8$ (16 degrees of freedom) before results become useful.
  • Too much overlap → segments are not independent: 90% overlap appears to give many segments, but adjacent segments share almost all data, greatly reducing the averaging effect. Hann + 50% is the optimal balance point.
  • Multitaper $NW$ selection: $NW$ too large → poor resolution (equivalent bandwidth $= 2NW \cdot f_s/N$); $NW$ too small → DPSS quality degrades (high-order tapers leak energy). Typically $NW = 3$ or 4.

When NOT to Use?

  • Signal is non-stationary: Welch assumes statistical properties are the same within each segment. If the signal's frequency changes over time (e.g., chirp), Welch averages spectra from different time instants → use STFT / spectrogram (Section 5.1)
  • Only need to detect discrete sinusoidal frequencies (not continuous PSD): → use MUSIC / ESPRIT (Section 3.4) for more precise results
  • Extremely short data with known signal model (e.g., speech): → use AR parametric model (Section 3.3)

References: [1] Bartlett, Smoothing Periodograms from Time-Series with Continuous Spectra, Nature, 1948. [2] Welch, The Use of FFT for the Estimation of Power Spectra, IEEE Trans. Audio Electroacoustics, 1967. [3] Thomson, Spectrum Estimation and Harmonic Analysis, Proc. IEEE, 1982. [4] Percival & Walden, Spectral Analysis for Physical Applications, Cambridge, 1993.

Worked Example

Vibration monitoring: fs=10 kHz, desired PSD with $\Delta f$=2 Hz. Using Hann window with 50% overlap. (a) Segment length? (b) How many segments from 10 seconds of data? (c) Equivalent degrees of freedom?

Show solution

(a) L = fs/$\Delta f$ = 10000/2 = 5000 points

(b) hop = 5000 $\times$ 0.5 = 2500, K = floor((100000 - 5000)/2500) + 1 = 39 segments

(c) Hann 50% overlap effective segments $\approx$ 39 $\times$ 0.67 $\approx$ 26, DOF $\approx$ 52

Quick Check

Q1: With Welch segment length L=1000 and sampling rate fs=10 kHz, what is the frequency resolution?

Show answer

$\Delta f$ = fs/L = 10000/1000 = 10 Hz.

Q2: If the number of segments doubles (K → 2K), what happens to the PSD estimation variance?

Show answer

Approximately halved (~1/K), assuming the segments are approximately independent.

Same noisy signal (containing two sinusoids) -- compare the periodogram's "jagged" appearance vs. Welch's "smooth" result. Each click regenerates noise.
