3.3 Parametric Spectral Estimation (AR Model)

Replacing direct FFT with model fitting -- high-resolution spectra from short data

Why does this matter? When data is very short (only tens of samples), FFT frequency resolution is too coarse. The AR model can squeeze higher resolution from short data than FFT -- especially important in speech analysis (20 ms per frame) and biomedical signals.

Previously... Section 3.2's Welch method reduces variance by segment averaging but sacrifices frequency resolution. If the data is very short, is there a way to avoid sacrificing resolution?

Learning Objectives

  • Establish the equivalence between the AR(p) model and Linear Prediction
  • Derive the Yule-Walker equations and the Levinson-Durbin recursion
  • Understand the lattice structure of the Burg algorithm
  • Master AR order selection using AIC/BIC criteria
  • Compare the resolution of AR spectra vs. the periodogram

One-Sentence Summary

Instead of computing the FFT directly, assume the signal is generated by a model (AR model) and use the model to infer the spectrum -- yielding smooth, high-resolution results even from short data.

The Problem: The Resolution Bottleneck of Short Data

Your data has only tens of samples (e.g., a 20 ms speech frame at 8 kHz sampling rate yields only 160 points). FFT frequency resolution $\Delta f = f_s/N = 8000/160 = 50\,\text{Hz}$. If two speech formants are at 500 Hz and 530 Hz, separated by only 30 Hz -- well below the 50 Hz resolution -- the FFT shows only a single blurred peak, completely unresolved.

You cannot collect longer data (speech is non-stationary; beyond 20--30 ms the statistics change), and zero-padding is only interpolation, not truly increased resolution. You need a method that can "squeeze" more spectral information from short data.

Origin

The AR model story traces back to G. Udny Yule (1927) and Gilbert Walker (1931), who built autoregressive model estimation theory (Yule-Walker equations) to analyze the quasi-periodicity of sunspots and the periodic patterns of Indian monsoons.

Norman Levinson (1947) and later James Durbin (1960) developed an efficient recursive solution (Levinson-Durbin recursion), reducing computational complexity from $O(p^3)$ (direct linear system solve) to $O(p^2)$.

The person who truly brought AR models into spectral estimation was John Parker Burg (1967), who proposed the Maximum Entropy Method (MEM) in his Stanford doctoral dissertation. Burg's insight: given finite autocorrelation data, among all consistent PSDs, the one with "maximum information entropy" is precisely the PSD corresponding to the AR model. This gave AR spectral estimation an information-theoretic foundation. Burg's doctoral advisor was Robert White, and his research was motivated by short-data spectral analysis in geophysics -- seismic exploration data is often very short, and FFT resolution is insufficient.

In the speech processing field, the AR model is widely known as LPC (Linear Predictive Coding). Itakura (1968) and Atal & Hanauer (1971) applied the AR model to speech analysis and coding, ushering in the era of digital voice communication. Early GSM mobile voice coding (RPE-LTP) and CELP coding are built on the AR model at their core.

Principle: The AR(p) Model

Intuition: The AR model assumes that each signal value can be predicted by a linear combination of the past $p$ values plus white noise. If the prediction is good, the residual is white noise. Viewed in reverse, the linear predictor is an "all-pole filter" through which white noise produces the observed signal. Each pair of conjugate poles creates a peak in the spectrum.

AR(p) Difference Equation

$$x[n] = -\sum_{k=1}^{p}a_k\,x[n-k] + e[n], \quad e[n] \sim \text{WN}(0, \sigma^2)$$

Equivalent form: $A(z)\,X(z) = E(z)$, where $A(z) = 1 + a_1 z^{-1} + \cdots + a_p z^{-p}$

PSD of the AR model:

$$S_{AR}(f) = \frac{\sigma^2}{\left|A(e^{j2\pi f/f_s})\right|^2} = \frac{\sigma^2}{\left|1 + \sum_{k=1}^{p}a_k\,e^{-j2\pi fk/f_s}\right|^2}$$

Since the denominator is the squared magnitude of a polynomial, $S_{AR}(f)$ has only peaks (corresponding to poles near the unit circle), no zeros (valleys). Each pair of conjugate poles $r\,e^{\pm j\theta}$ produces a peak at $f = \theta f_s/(2\pi)$; the closer the pole radius $r$ is to 1, the sharper the peak.

Yule-Walker Equations

Show full derivation

Multiply both sides of the AR equation by $x^*[n-m]$ and take the expectation:

$$E[x[n]\,x^*[n-m]] = -\sum_{k=1}^{p}a_k\,E[x[n-k]\,x^*[n-m]] + E[e[n]\,x^*[n-m]]$$

Left side = $r_{xx}[m]$ (autocorrelation function). First term on the right = $-\sum a_k\,r_{xx}[m-k]$.

Second term on the right: since $e[n]$ is only correlated with $x[n], x[n-1], \ldots$ (not with future values):

  • $m = 0$: $E[e[n]\,x^*[n]] = \sigma^2$ ($e[n]$ is part of $x[n]$)
  • $m \geq 1$: $E[e[n]\,x^*[n-m]] = 0$ ($e[n]$ is uncorrelated with past $x$)

For $m = 1, 2, \ldots, p$, we obtain the Yule-Walker linear system:

$$\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}r[0]&r[-1]&\cdots&r[1-p]\\r[1]&r[0]&\cdots&r[2-p]\\\vdots&&\ddots&\vdots\\r[p-1]&\cdots&&r[0]\end{bmatrix}}_{\mathbf{R}\;(\text{Toeplitz})}\begin{bmatrix}a_1\\a_2\\\vdots\\a_p\end{bmatrix} = -\begin{bmatrix}r[1]\\r[2]\\\vdots\\r[p]\end{bmatrix}$$

For $m = 0$: $\sigma^2 = r[0] + \sum_{k=1}^{p}a_k\,r[-k]$ (white noise power).

$\mathbf{R}$ is a Toeplitz positive definite matrix (since it is an autocorrelation matrix) and can be solved in $O(p^2)$ using the Levinson-Durbin recursion (instead of the general $O(p^3)$ for linear systems). $\;\blacksquare$

Levinson-Durbin Recursion

Expand: Algorithm steps

Recursively build from AR(1) up to AR(p):

Initialization ($m=0$): $\sigma_0^2 = r[0]$

Recursion ($m = 1, 2, \ldots, p$):

$$k_m = -\frac{r[m] + \sum_{i=1}^{m-1}a_i^{(m-1)}\,r[m-i]}{\sigma_{m-1}^2} \quad \text{(reflection coefficient)}$$ $$a_m^{(m)} = k_m$$ $$a_i^{(m)} = a_i^{(m-1)} + k_m\,a_{m-i}^{(m-1)}, \quad i = 1, \ldots, m-1$$ $$\sigma_m^2 = (1 - |k_m|^2)\,\sigma_{m-1}^2$$

Stability guarantee: If $|k_m| < 1$ holds for all $m$ (guaranteed by the autocorrelation method), then the AR model is stable (all poles inside the unit circle).

Each step's $\sigma_m^2$ is the prediction error power of AR($m$) -- decreasing with $m$. When adding one more order barely reduces $\sigma_m^2$, that is the indicator for optimal order. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Four-Step Process

Step 1: Choose AR order $p$

Rule of thumb: $p \approx 2 \times$ (expected number of spectral peaks). For example, speech has 4--5 formants → $p \approx 10$, but adding glottal wave and radiation effects → typically $p = 10$--$14$ (at 8 kHz sampling rate).

Information criteria: AIC = $N\ln\sigma_p^2 + 2p$; BIC = $N\ln\sigma_p^2 + p\ln N$. Choose $p$ that minimizes AIC/BIC. BIC penalizes higher orders more heavily, favoring lower $p$.

Step 2: Compute autocorrelation $r[0], r[1], \ldots, r[p]$

$r[k] = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{n=0}^{N-1-k}x[n+k]\,x^*[n]$ (biased estimator, but guarantees the Toeplitz matrix is positive definite).

Step 3: Solve Yule-Walker (Levinson-Durbin) for $\{a_k\}$ and $\sigma^2$

Or use the Burg algorithm (does not require precomputing autocorrelation; directly estimates reflection coefficients from data with better statistical properties).

Step 4: Compute PSD

Evaluate $S_{AR}(f) = \sigma^2/|A(e^{j2\pi f/f_s})|^2$ on a dense frequency grid.

Concrete Example: Speech Formant Analysis

  • Sampling rate $f_s = 8\,\text{kHz}$, speech frame length 20 ms → $N = 160$ points
  • FFT resolution = $8000/160 = 50\,\text{Hz}$ (too coarse!)
  • Choose AR(12): expected 4 formants + 2 extra poles (glottal + radiation) = 12
  • Levinson-Durbin solve → 12 AR coefficients
  • Compute PSD → smooth spectral envelope clearly showing $F_1 \approx 500\,\text{Hz}$, $F_2 \approx 1500\,\text{Hz}$, $F_3 \approx 2500\,\text{Hz}$ formants
  • This is the core of LPC (Linear Predictive Coding)!

Applications

  1. Speech Analysis and Coding (LPC): LPC = AR model. GSM mobile voice coding (RPE-LTP, 13 kbps) and CELP coding (e.g., AMR, G.729) are built on AR(10)--AR(16) models at their core. Vocoders use the AR model to separate glottal excitation from vocal tract resonance, forming the basis of speech synthesis and voice transformation technology.
  2. High-Resolution Frequency Estimation from Short Data: Seismic exploration reflection wave analysis with data windows of only 50--100 samples. The AR model can resolve frequency differences between multiple reflection layers when FFT resolution is insufficient. Practical case: 100 points @ 1 kHz data, FFT $\Delta f = 10\,\text{Hz}$, AR(20) successfully resolved two peaks separated by 5 Hz.
  3. Heart Rate Variability (HRV) Frequency Domain Analysis: ECG R-R interval series typically have only 300--500 data points (5-minute short-term HRV). AR(16)--AR(20) models can clearly resolve the LF (0.04--0.15 Hz) and HF (0.15--0.4 Hz) components, providing smoother and more stable results than FFT. The LF/HF ratio is an important clinical indicator of autonomic nervous system function.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • AR only has peaks, no valleys: An all-pole model can inherently only describe spectra with peaks. If the true spectrum has deep valleys (zeros), the AR model requires a very high order to approximate them, and the results are poor. Consider ARMA models (with both poles and zeros) in such cases.
  • Order too low → missed peaks: AR(4) can have at most 2 spectral peaks. If there are actually 3 peaks, the third will be completely missed.
  • Order too high → spurious peaks: AR(30) on 160 data points will overfit, producing nonexistent false peaks. Always use AIC/BIC as a check.
  • Worse than periodogram for broadband noise: The AR model assumes the spectrum is smooth (determined by a few poles). For broadband flat noise floors, it actually performs worse than a direct periodogram.
  • Non-stationary signals: AR assumes stationarity. For non-stationary signals, perform AR analysis in short windowed segments, or use time-varying AR (e.g., Kalman filter + AR).

When NOT to Use?

  • Data is long and you just need a general PSD: Directly use Welch's method (Section 3.2) -- simpler, more robust, no order selection needed
  • Spectrum has prominent zeros (deep valleys): Consider ARMA model (but estimation is more complex and convergence is worse)
  • Need super-resolution for sinusoidal frequencies: AR model resolution is still limited by signal SNR → use MUSIC / ESPRIT (Section 3.4)
  • Signal model is unclear: AR's advantage relies on "correct model assumption." If unsure whether the signal fits an AR description, non-parametric methods (Welch / Multitaper) are safer

Interactive: AR Spectrum vs. FFT

Signal containing two sinusoids (100 Hz + 105 Hz) + noise, with only 64 samples. Compare the frequency resolution of the periodogram vs. the AR model. Adjust AR order to observe the effect: too low misses peaks, too high creates spurious peaks.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

References: [1] Burg, Maximum Entropy Spectral Analysis, Ph.D. dissertation, Stanford, 1975. [2] Kay & Marple, Spectrum Analysis — A Modern Perspective, Proc. IEEE, 1981. [3] Kay, Modern Spectral Estimation: Theory and Application, Prentice Hall, 1988. [4] Stoica & Moses, Spectral Analysis of Signals, Pearson, 2005. [5] Makhoul, Linear Prediction: A Tutorial Review, Proc. IEEE, 1975.

Quick Check

Q1: What happens if the AR model order is too low? Too high?

Show answer

Too low: real spectral peaks are missed. Too high: spurious peaks appear (overfitting). Typically use AIC/BIC to choose the order.

Q2: Why can't the AR model represent spectral "valleys"?

Show answer

Because AR is an all-pole model. Poles can only produce peaks; without zeros, valleys cannot be created. An ARMA model is needed for that.

← 3.2 Welch / Multitaper3.4 MUSIC / ESPRIT →

3.4 MUSIC & ESPRIT -- Subspace Frequency Estimation

Frequency estimation methods that surpass the Fourier resolution limit

Why does this matter? In certain scenarios (radar target resolution, array antenna localization), you need to resolve frequencies or angles spaced closer than a single FFT bin. Subspace methods are currently the only mainstream technique capable of super-resolution estimation.

Previously... Section 3.3's AR model offers better resolution than FFT, but is still limited by model assumptions. Is there a fundamentally different approach that can surpass the Fourier frequency resolution limit?

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the decomposition into Signal Subspace and Noise Subspace
  • Derive the principle of the MUSIC pseudospectrum
  • Learn about ESPRIT's Rotational Invariance
  • Master signal number estimation (MDL/AIC) and limitations of subspace methods

One-Sentence Summary

MUSIC can resolve two frequencies spaced closer than a single FFT bin -- it is not a better FFT, but a completely different approach: separating the signal space from the noise space.

The Problem: Two Frequencies That FFT Cannot Separate

You have two radar targets too close together, with Doppler frequencies of 100 Hz and 103 Hz respectively. Sampling rate 1 kHz, and you captured only 64 data points. One FFT frequency bin = $f_s/N = 1000/64 = 15.6\,\text{Hz}$, while the two frequencies are only 3 Hz apart -- the FFT shows just a single broad peak, completely unable to determine whether there is one target or two.

Zero-padding to 1024 points? That only interpolates the frequency axis with finer bins, but the sinc main lobe width does not change -- the two targets remain blurred within the same main lobe.

AR model? It helps, but at moderate SNR it can easily produce bias or spurious peaks. You need a fundamentally different method.

Origin

Ralph O. Schmidt (1979) at ESL Inc. (a defense electronics company) developed the MUSIC (Multiple Signal Classification) algorithm. His original motivation was Direction of Arrival (DOA) estimation in radar and electronic warfare: multiple electromagnetic waves arriving at an antenna array from different directions -- how to estimate each wave's angle of arrival with high precision? Traditional beamforming methods are limited by the antenna array aperture. Schmidt's breakthrough was exploiting the subspace structure of the signal autocorrelation matrix, bypassing traditional resolution limits.

The MUSIC paper (IEEE Trans. AP, 1986) became one of the most cited papers in signal processing (Google Scholar > 12,000 citations). Schmidt received the IEEE 2000 Society Award for this work.

Richard Roy and Thomas Kailath (1986) at Stanford proposed ESPRIT (Estimation of Signal Parameters via Rotational Invariance Techniques). ESPRIT exploits the rotational invariance between two sub-arrays within the array, requiring no pseudospectrum search (which MUSIC needs to scan the frequency axis) and directly obtaining frequency estimates from matrix eigenvalues. It is computationally faster and does not require precise array calibration.

Together, these two methods are known as subspace methods and are core tools for frequency/direction estimation in modern radar, communications, sonar, and similar systems.

Principle: The Subspace Intuition

Core intuition: Imagine you are in an $M$-dimensional space. The received data = signal + noise. If there are $p$ sinusoidal signals, they "live" in a $p$-dimensional subspace (signal subspace). Noise is spread across the entire $M$-dimensional space.

Eigenvalue decomposition of the autocorrelation matrix separates these two spaces: $p$ large eigenvalues with their eigenvectors span the signal subspace; the remaining $M-p$ small eigenvalues ($\approx \sigma^2$, the noise power) with their eigenvectors span the noise subspace.

Key fact: The signal's steering vector is necessarily orthogonal to the noise subspace. So when you take a test vector $\mathbf{a}(\omega)$ and compute its inner product with the noise subspace, at the correct frequencies the inner product is zero -- taking the reciprocal yields infinity, a sharp peak.

Mathematical Derivation

Assume we observe $p$ complex sinusoids plus white noise. Construct the $M \times M$ autocorrelation matrix:

$$\mathbf{R} = E[\mathbf{x}\mathbf{x}^H] = \mathbf{A}\mathbf{S}\mathbf{A}^H + \sigma^2\mathbf{I}$$

$\mathbf{A} = [\mathbf{a}(\omega_1), \ldots, \mathbf{a}(\omega_p)]$: steering matrix, $\mathbf{S}$: signal covariance matrix

The steering vector:

$$\mathbf{a}(\omega) = \begin{bmatrix}1 \\ e^{j\omega} \\ e^{j2\omega} \\ \vdots \\ e^{j(M-1)\omega}\end{bmatrix}$$
Expand: MUSIC pseudospectrum derivation

Eigenvalue decomposition of $\mathbf{R}$:

$$\mathbf{R} = \sum_{i=1}^{M}\lambda_i\,\mathbf{e}_i\mathbf{e}_i^H = \underbrace{\sum_{i=1}^{p}(\lambda_i^s + \sigma^2)\,\mathbf{e}_i\mathbf{e}_i^H}_{\text{signal subspace}} + \underbrace{\sigma^2\sum_{i=p+1}^{M}\mathbf{e}_i\mathbf{e}_i^H}_{\text{noise subspace}}$$

where $\lambda_1 \geq \cdots \geq \lambda_p > \lambda_{p+1} = \cdots = \lambda_M = \sigma^2$.

Let $\mathbf{E}_n = [\mathbf{e}_{p+1}, \ldots, \mathbf{e}_M]$ be the eigenvector matrix of the noise subspace.

Key property: $\mathbf{a}(\omega_i) \perp \mathbf{E}_n$ for all $i = 1, \ldots, p$.

Proof: Since $\mathbf{a}(\omega_i)$ is a column of $\mathbf{A}$, it lies in the signal subspace $\text{span}(\mathbf{e}_1, \ldots, \mathbf{e}_p)$, and the signal subspace is orthogonal to the noise subspace.

Therefore, define the MUSIC pseudospectrum:

$$P_{\text{MUSIC}}(\omega) = \frac{1}{\mathbf{a}^H(\omega)\,\mathbf{E}_n\mathbf{E}_n^H\,\mathbf{a}(\omega)}$$

At $\omega = \omega_i$, the denominator $\mathbf{a}^H\mathbf{E}_n\mathbf{E}_n^H\mathbf{a} = \|\mathbf{E}_n^H\mathbf{a}\|^2 \to 0$, so $P_{\text{MUSIC}} \to \infty$ -- producing a sharp peak.

Note: $P_{\text{MUSIC}}$ is not a true power spectral density but an indicator function ("pseudospectrum"). Peak positions are meaningful (= frequency estimates), but peak heights have no power interpretation. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Five-Step Process

Step 1: Build the autocorrelation matrix $\mathbf{R}$ (size $M \times M$)

From data $x[0], \ldots, x[N-1]$, construct a Hankel matrix and estimate $\hat{\mathbf{R}} = \frac{1}{N-M+1}\sum_{n=0}^{N-M}\mathbf{x}_n\mathbf{x}_n^H$, where $\mathbf{x}_n = [x[n], x[n+1], \ldots, x[n+M-1]]^T$.

Choosing $M$: $M$ must be greater than the signal count $p$. Rule of thumb: $M \approx N/3$ to $N/2$. $M$ too small → matrix too small, poor resolution; $M$ too large → too few snapshots to estimate $\hat{\mathbf{R}}$ accurately.

Step 2: Eigenvalue decomposition

$\hat{\mathbf{R}} = \mathbf{E}\boldsymbol{\Lambda}\mathbf{E}^H$. Observe the eigenvalues: the first $p$ are significantly larger than the rest.

Step 3: Estimate the number of signals $p$

Look for the "cliff drop" in eigenvalues: $\lambda_1 \geq \cdots \geq \lambda_p \gg \lambda_{p+1} \approx \cdots \approx \lambda_M$. Or use MDL (Minimum Description Length) / AIC criteria for automatic determination:

$\text{MDL}(k) = -(N-M+1)(M-k)\ln\frac{\prod_{i=k+1}^{M}\lambda_i^{1/(M-k)}}{\frac{1}{M-k}\sum_{i=k+1}^{M}\lambda_i} + \frac{1}{2}k(2M-k)\ln(N-M+1)$

Choose the $k$ that minimizes MDL as $\hat{p}$.

Step 4: Construct noise subspace and scan pseudospectrum

$\mathbf{E}_n = [\mathbf{e}_{p+1}, \ldots, \mathbf{e}_M]$; densely sample along the frequency axis to compute $P_{\text{MUSIC}}(\omega)$.

Step 5: Find peaks → frequency estimates

Peak positions of $P_{\text{MUSIC}}$ = estimated frequencies. Parabolic interpolation can further refine the estimates.

Concrete Example: Two closely spaced sinusoids

  • Signal: $x[n] = \sin(2\pi \cdot 100\,n/f_s) + \sin(2\pi \cdot 103\,n/f_s) + \text{noise}$
  • Sampling rate $f_s = 1000\,\text{Hz}$, only $N = 64$ data points
  • FFT bin = $1000/64 = 15.6\,\text{Hz}$ → completely unresolvable (3 Hz << 15.6 Hz)
  • MUSIC: choose $M = 16$, estimate $p = 2$
  • Eigenvalue decomposition → 2 large eigenvalues ($\gg \sigma^2$) → 14 small eigenvalues ($\approx \sigma^2$)
  • Scan pseudospectrum → two clear sharp peaks at 100 Hz and 103 Hz
  • Successfully resolved! Resolution improvement of $15.6/3 \approx 5\times$

ESPRIT: A More Efficient Alternative

Intuition: MUSIC requires scanning the entire frequency axis to search for peaks, which is computationally expensive. ESPRIT exploits a clever observation -- if you take the first $M-1$ rows and the last $M-1$ rows of the matrix as two "sub-arrays," the relationship between them is a rotation (phase shift), and the rotation amount = $e^{j\omega}$, which is directly the frequency!

ESPRIT Rotational Invariance

$$\mathbf{E}_{s2} = \mathbf{E}_{s1}\,\mathbf{\Phi}, \quad \mathbf{\Phi} = \text{diag}(e^{j\omega_1}, e^{j\omega_2}, \ldots, e^{j\omega_p})$$

$\mathbf{E}_{s1}$, $\mathbf{E}_{s2}$: projections of the signal subspace onto the two sub-arrays

Implementation steps:

  1. Eigendecomposition to obtain signal subspace $\mathbf{E}_s$ (same as MUSIC)
  2. Take $\mathbf{E}_{s1}$ = $\mathbf{E}_s$ with last row removed, $\mathbf{E}_{s2}$ = $\mathbf{E}_s$ with first row removed
  3. Solve $\mathbf{\Phi} = \mathbf{E}_{s1}^{\dagger}\mathbf{E}_{s2}$ (least squares / Total Least Squares)
  4. Eigenvalues of $\mathbf{\Phi}$: $\lambda_i = e^{j\omega_i}$ → $\omega_i = \angle\lambda_i$ → frequency $f_i = \omega_i f_s / (2\pi)$
PropertyMUSICESPRIT
OutputPseudospectrum (peak search required)Frequency values directly
Computational cost$O(M^3) + O(M^2 \cdot N_{\text{scan}})$$O(M^3)$ (no search needed)
Array calibrationRequires precise calibrationLess sensitive
ResolutionSlightly higher (can use full noise subspace)Slightly lower but still far exceeds FFT
Additional informationPseudospectrum provides visualizationOnly numerical frequency values

Applications

  1. Radar DOA Estimation: A phased-array radar's 8--64 antenna elements receive signals; MUSIC estimates precise azimuth angles of multiple targets. In practice, with a 64-element ULA (uniform linear array), MUSIC can resolve two targets with angular separation < 1 degree at SNR = 10 dB (traditional beamforming resolution is ~7 degrees).
  2. Wireless Communication AoA Localization: 5G base stations use massive MIMO antenna arrays, leveraging ESPRIT/MUSIC to estimate user device Angle of Arrival (AoA). Combined with multi-station data, this enables indoor positioning (accuracy < 1 m). ESPRIT is preferred in real-time systems due to its computational efficiency.
  3. Closely Spaced Modal Identification in Vibration Analysis: Mechanical structures (e.g., aircraft wings, bridges) have multiple vibration modes at natural frequencies, some very closely spaced (< 1 Hz difference). MUSIC can resolve these closely spaced modes from short accelerometer data segments, supporting structural health monitoring.
  4. Multiple Pitch Estimation in Music Signals: In a piano chord, multiple notes' fundamental frequencies differ by less than a semitone (~6%), each with its own harmonics. MUSIC can precisely estimate each note's fundamental frequency in a chord, serving as a tool for Automatic Music Transcription.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Must know or estimate the number of signals $p$: This is the "Achilles' heel" of subspace methods. If $p$ is estimated incorrectly, results are wrong. Overestimating $p$ → spurious peaks; underestimating $p$ → missed signals. The MDL criterion is reliable at higher SNR but tends to fail at low SNR.
  • Correlated signals (coherent sources) cause failure: If two signals are perfectly correlated (e.g., reflected waves in multipath), $\mathbf{S}$ is rank-deficient, the signal subspace dimension is less than $p$, and MUSIC will "leak" some signals into the noise subspace. Solution: spatial smoothing -- sacrificing some array aperture to restore the rank of $\mathbf{S}$.
  • Computational cost $O(M^3)$: Eigendecomposition complexity. Already expensive at $M = 64$. Large arrays ($M > 100$) require fast subspace tracking algorithms (e.g., PAST, GROUSE).
  • Performance degrades sharply at low SNR: Subspace methods exhibit a "threshold effect" -- when SNR falls below a certain threshold (typically 5--10 dB), performance degrades dramatically, with large estimation bias or completely wrong results. This is because noise eigenvalues and signal eigenvalues begin to overlap, causing subspace separation to fail.
  • Only suitable for the "few sinusoids + white noise" model: If the signal is broadband (e.g., speech) or the noise is colored (non-white), the standard MUSIC/ESPRIT assumptions are violated and results are unreliable. Colored noise requires pre-whitening.

When NOT to Use?

  • Need a complete PSD (not discrete frequencies): MUSIC only estimates discrete frequency components, not a continuous PSD → use Welch or AR model
  • Many unknown signals: More than $M/2$ signals cannot be handled (a fundamental limitation of subspace methods). In practice, $p > 5$--$8$ is already very challenging
  • Real-time processing with large matrices: $O(M^3)$ eigendecomposition may be too slow for embedded systems → consider ESPRIT (faster) or subspace tracking algorithms
  • Need a robust "works anywhere" method: Subspace methods are sensitive to model assumptions (white noise, known signal count, uncorrelated signals) → use Welch / Multitaper for safety

Interactive: MUSIC vs. FFT

Two very closely spaced sinusoids (center frequency 100 Hz) -- adjust frequency separation and SNR. When $\Delta f$ is much smaller than the FFT bin width, FFT sees only one peak, but MUSIC can resolve two.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

FFT bin width = 15.6 Hz. When $\Delta f < 15.6$ Hz, FFT in principle cannot resolve them. Lower the SNR to observe the threshold effect.

References: [1] Schmidt, Multiple Emitter Location and Signal Parameter Estimation, IEEE Trans. AP, 1986 (original report 1979). [2] Roy & Kailath, ESPRIT — Estimation of Signal Parameters via Rotational Invariance Techniques, IEEE Trans. ASSP, 1989. [3] Stoica & Moses, Spectral Analysis of Signals, Ch.4, Pearson, 2005. [4] Van Trees, Optimum Array Processing, Part IV of Detection, Estimation, and Modulation Theory, Wiley, 2002.

Quick Check

Q1: What information does MUSIC need to work?

Show answer

It needs to know (or estimate) the number of signals M, in order to distinguish the signal subspace from the noise subspace. Typically estimated using MDL or AIC criteria.

Q2: What happens to MUSIC if two signals are perfectly correlated (coherent)?

Show answer

It fails -- correlated signals cause the autocorrelation matrix rank to drop, preventing correct subspace separation. Spatial smoothing preprocessing is needed.

← 3.3 AR Model3.5 Chirp-Z Transform →

3.5 Chirp-Z Transform (CZT)

A spectral magnifying glass -- computing the DFT along any path in the Z-plane

Why does this matter? Sometimes you only care about a narrow frequency band's details (e.g., precisely measuring power grid frequency offset). CZT lets you focus computational resources on that band, like a spectral magnifying glass.

Previously... Section 3.4's MUSIC can do super-resolution estimation, but is computationally expensive. If you just want to "zoom in" on a specific frequency band's details, there is a lighter-weight tool --

Learning Objectives

  • Define CZT: a generalized DFT sampling along a spiral in the Z-plane
  • Derive Bluestein's identity and the $O(N\log N)$ computation method
  • Understand CZT's "frequency zoom-in" capability and how it differs from zero-padding
  • Distinguish between "denser frequency sampling" and "higher frequency resolution"

One-Sentence Summary

CZT lets you compute only the frequency range you care about, like a magnifying glass for the spectrum.

The Problem: I Only Care About a Small Frequency Range

You are monitoring power system frequency and only care about 49.9--50.1 Hz, looking for tiny frequency deviations. Sampling rate $f_s = 1\,\text{kHz}$, and you collected 1 second of data ($N = 1000$). FFT gives you a full-range 0--500 Hz spectrum with each bin = 1 Hz. Near 50 Hz there are only one or two points -- far too few to see a 0.01 Hz frequency deviation.

You could zero-pad to $N = 100000$ (100-second equivalent), making the bin width 0.01 Hz, but that means computing a 100K-point FFT where 99.96% of the results (0--49.9 Hz and 50.1--500 Hz) are unnecessary.

Is there a way to compute only the 49.9--50.1 Hz range with a dense 0.01 Hz spacing?

Origin

Lawrence Rabiner, Ronald Schafer, and Charles Rader (1969) at Bell Labs proposed the Chirp-Z Transform. Their key insight was: the DFT is actually equally spaced sampling of the Z-transform on the unit circle. If the sampling points are moved to an arbitrary spiral in the Z-plane, you get a more flexible frequency analysis tool.

The paper title directly states the method's core: "The Chirp z-Transform Algorithm." "Chirp" refers to the linear chirp signal used in the algorithm, because the kernel function in Bluestein's identity (which converts the DFT into convolution) is exactly a chirp.

Rader's other important contribution was discovering that prime-length DFTs can be converted to convolution (Rader's algorithm, 1968), while the CZT's Bluestein method is more general -- it converts DFTs of any length to convolution (no need for the length to be a power of 2 or a prime).

Principle

Intuition: The DFT is $N$ equally spaced sampling points on the unit circle of the Z-transform. CZT generalizes these to $M$ equally spaced sampling points on any spiral in the Z-plane. If the spiral covers only the small arc (frequency range) you are interested in, you get arbitrarily dense spectral sampling within that range -- and $M$ can be much larger or much smaller than $N$.

CZT Definition

$$X(z_k) = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1}x[n]\,z_k^{-n}, \quad z_k = A\,W^{-k}, \; k = 0, 1, \ldots, M-1$$

$A = A_0\,e^{j\theta_0}$ (starting point), $W = W_0\,e^{j\phi_0}$ (step size) define the spiral path in the Z-plane

DFT is a special case of CZT: $A = 1$, $W = e^{-j2\pi/N}$, $M = N$ ($N$ equally spaced points on the unit circle).

Frequency zoom-in: Simply set $A = e^{j2\pi f_1/f_s}$ (start frequency), $W = e^{-j2\pi(f_2-f_1)/(Mf_s)}$ (step frequency), and the $M$-point CZT computes only the $M$-point spectrum within $[f_1, f_2]$.

Expand: Bluestein's identity and convolution implementation

Direct CZT computation is $O(NM)$, but Bluestein's identity converts it to convolution:

Key: $kn = \frac{1}{2}[k^2 + n^2 - (k-n)^2]$. Therefore:

$$W^{kn} = W^{k^2/2}\,W^{n^2/2}\,W^{-(k-n)^2/2}$$

Substituting into the CZT definition:

$$X(z_k) = W^{k^2/2}\sum_{n=0}^{N-1}\underbrace{\left[x[n]\,A^{-n}\,W^{n^2/2}\right]}_{g[n]}\,\underbrace{W^{-(k-n)^2/2}}_{h[k-n]}$$

The bracketed summation is a linear convolution of $g[n]$ and $h[n] = W^{-n^2/2}$!

Therefore, CZT can be computed with three FFTs:

  1. Compute $g[n] = x[n]\,A^{-n}\,W^{n^2/2}$, $n = 0, \ldots, N-1$
  2. Compute $h[n] = W^{-n^2/2}$, $n = -(N-1), \ldots, M-1$
  3. FFT convolution: $y = g * h$ (zero-pad to $\geq N+M-1$, FFT → pointwise multiply → IFFT)
  4. $X(z_k) = W^{k^2/2}\,y[k]$, $k = 0, \ldots, M-1$

Total complexity: $O((N+M)\log(N+M))$, much faster than direct $O(NM)$. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use

Three parameters are all you need

  • Start frequency $f_1$: lower limit of your frequency band of interest
  • End frequency $f_2$: upper limit of your frequency band of interest
  • Number of points $M$: how many frequency points within $[f_1, f_2]$

Frequency spacing = $(f_2 - f_1)/M$. You can make this spacing arbitrarily small -- but remember, this does not increase the true frequency resolution.

// CZT Zoom-in Example (Python) from scipy.signal import czt, czt_points import numpy as np fs = 1000 # Sampling rate 1 kHz t = np.arange(0, 1, 1/fs) # 1 second of data x = np.sin(2*np.pi*50.03*t) # 50.03 Hz sine wave # CZT zoom into 49.9 ~ 50.1 Hz f1, f2, M = 49.9, 50.1, 1000 z_points = czt_points(M, w=np.exp(-1j*2*np.pi*(f2-f1)/(M*fs)), a=np.exp(1j*2*np.pi*f1/fs)) X = czt(x, M, w=np.exp(-1j*2*np.pi*(f2-f1)/(M*fs)), a=np.exp(1j*2*np.pi*f1/fs)) # Result: 1000 points between 49.9~50.1 Hz # Frequency spacing = 0.0002 Hz, clearly showing the 50.03 Hz peak

Applications

  1. Power System Frequency Monitoring: The nominal grid frequency is 50/60 Hz, but actual frequency fluctuates between 49.95--50.05 Hz. Monitoring this tiny deviation is critical for grid stability. CZT can achieve 0.001 Hz frequency measurement precision on 1-second data (direct FFT would require 1000 seconds of data for the same bin density). IEC 61000-4-30 Class A power quality analyzers use CZT-like techniques in practice.
  2. Musical Instrument Tuner: A4 = 440 Hz, A4# = 466.16 Hz, semitone difference = 26.16 Hz (~6%). Tuning requires precision < 1 cent (0.058%, i.e., 0.26 Hz). For 44.1 kHz sampled 0.1-second audio ($N = 4410$), FFT bin = 10 Hz -- completely insufficient. CZT zoom to 430--450 Hz, $M = 2000$ points → spacing 0.01 Hz → easily achieving 0.1 cent precision.
  3. Precision Vibration Analysis: Rotating machinery (e.g., turbine generator, speed 3000 RPM = 50 Hz) shaft vibration. Need to precisely track the amplitude and phase variations of 1X (50 Hz) and 2X (100 Hz) with small load/temperature changes. CZT focuses on two narrow bands: 49--51 Hz and 99--101 Hz, more flexible than order tracking.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • CZT does NOT increase true frequency resolution: This is the most important and most commonly misunderstood point. Frequency resolution is still limited by observation time $T$: $\Delta f_{\text{resolution}} \approx 1/T$. CZT only provides denser frequency sampling (like reading a ruler with finer marks), but cannot resolve two frequencies spaced less than $1/T$ apart. This is similar to zero-padding, but CZT can do it only in the band you care about, more flexibly and with less computation.
  • Leave margin when choosing the $[f_1, f_2]$ range: If the zoom range is too narrow, the target frequency's main lobe may be truncated, causing sidelobe artifacts. Typically leave 2--3 main-lobe widths of margin on each side.
  • Windowing is still required: CZT does not eliminate spectral leakage. Data must still be windowed before CZT.
  • Numerical stability when $|W_0| \neq 1$ (spiral rather than arc): If the sampling path deviates too far from the unit circle, $z_k^{-n}$ grows or decays exponentially, causing numerical issues. Sampling on the unit circle ($|A_0| = |W_0| = 1$) is usually safest.

When NOT to Use?

  • Need the full-range spectrum: If you need the complete spectrum from 0 to $f_s/2$, a direct FFT is faster and simpler
  • Need to truly increase resolution (resolve closely spaced frequencies): CZT is a magnifying glass, not a microscope → use MUSIC / ESPRIT (Section 3.4) or collect longer data
  • Both $M$ and $N$ are large and similar in size: CZT's three FFTs plus preprocessing are slower than a single direct FFT → only worthwhile when the zoom range is significantly smaller than the full range

References: [1] Rabiner, Schafer & Rader, The Chirp z-Transform Algorithm, IEEE Trans. Audio Electroacoustics, 1969. [2] Bluestein, A Linear Filtering Approach to the Computation of Discrete Fourier Transform, IEEE Trans. AU, 1970. [3] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Section 9.6.

← 3.4 MUSIC / ESPRIT4.1 Hilbert Transform →

4.1 Hilbert Transform

The mathematical foundation for constructing the analytic signal -- extracting amplitude envelope and instantaneous phase from a real signal

Why does this matter? Envelope detection is the core operation in communications demodulation (AM) and mechanical fault diagnosis (bearings). The Hilbert transform is the cleanest, most mathematically rigorous method for extracting envelopes.

Previously... Part III taught various spectral estimation methods. But some applications need more than just a spectrum -- they need the "envelope," how the signal's amplitude varies over time. The Hilbert transform is the mathematical tool for extracting envelopes.

Learning Objectives

  • Define the Hilbert transform and its frequency-domain representation $-j\,\text{sgn}(\omega)$
  • Understand the physical meaning of the analytic signal: eliminating negative frequency redundancy
  • Master the three-step FFT-based Hilbert transform implementation
  • Recognize the limitations and correct usage of the Hilbert transform

One-Sentence Summary

The Hilbert transform turns a real signal into a complex signal (analytic signal), allowing you to extract the amplitude envelope and instantaneous frequency.

The Problem: How Do You Extract What Is "Hidden Inside the Carrier"?

Scenario 1: AM radio. A station modulates voice (20--4000 Hz) onto a 1 MHz carrier for broadcast. How does the receiver "peel off" the voice from the carrier? The voice is the carrier's amplitude envelope, but how do you extract an envelope from a real signal?

Scenario 2: Bearing fault detection. A bearing outer race has a small defect, and each time a rolling element passes the defect it produces an impact that excites the bearing housing's high-frequency resonance (2--5 kHz). The time-domain waveform shows a series of impact pulses "modulated" by 2--5 kHz resonance. You need the impact repetition frequency (BPFO, ~87 Hz), but it is buried in the high-frequency resonance. You need to extract the envelope first, then FFT the envelope to find the BPFO.

In both scenarios, envelope detection is the core operation, and the Hilbert transform is the cleanest envelope detection tool.

Origin

David Hilbert (1905) introduced a special class of singular integral transforms while studying complex function theory and integral equations. This purely mathematical tool had no engineering connection at the time.

Dennis Gabor (1946) was the key figure in bringing the Hilbert transform into signal processing. In his landmark paper "Theory of Communication" (published in the Journal of the IEE), Gabor proposed the concept of the "analytic signal": pairing a real signal $x(t)$ with its Hilbert transform $\hat{x}(t)$ as the imaginary part to form the complex signal $z(t) = x(t) + j\hat{x}(t)$.

Gabor's motivation was communication theory -- he wanted to provide rigorous mathematical definitions for a signal's "instantaneous frequency" and "instantaneous amplitude." The analytic signal provides this framework. Incidentally, the same paper also introduced what would later be called the Gabor transform (a special case of the short-time Fourier transform).

Gabor later received the 1971 Nobel Prize in Physics for his invention of holography.

Principle

Intuition: A real signal's spectrum is conjugate symmetric ($X(-\omega) = X^*(\omega)$); positive and negative frequencies carry exactly the same information. The analytic signal removes the negative frequencies and doubles the positive ones -- no information is lost, but the representation is more concise. After removing negative frequencies, the signal becomes complex, and you can directly use magnitude and phase angle to extract the envelope and instantaneous phase.

Hilbert Transform (Time-Domain Definition)

$$\hat{x}(t) = \mathcal{H}\{x(t)\} = \frac{1}{\pi}\,\text{p.v.}\!\int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\frac{x(\tau)}{t - \tau}\,d\tau = x(t) * \frac{1}{\pi t}$$

p.v. = Cauchy principal value (avoiding the singularity at $\tau = t$)

Hilbert Transform (Frequency-Domain Representation)

$$\hat{X}(\omega) = -j\,\text{sgn}(\omega)\cdot X(\omega) = \begin{cases}-jX(\omega), & \omega > 0 \\ 0, & \omega = 0 \\ jX(\omega), & \omega < 0\end{cases}$$

Effect: Positive frequency components are phase-shifted by $-90°$, negative frequency components by $+90°$, amplitude unchanged. It is an allpass phase shifter.

Expand: Why does $-j\,\text{sgn}(\omega)$ equal a 90-degree phase shift?

$-j = e^{-j\pi/2}$; multiplying by $-j$ subtracts 90 degrees from the phase.

For positive frequency components $X(\omega)$ ($\omega > 0$): $\hat{X}(\omega) = -jX(\omega)$, phase shift $-90°$.

For negative frequency components ($\omega < 0$): $\text{sgn}(\omega) = -1$, so $\hat{X}(\omega) = jX(\omega)$, phase shift $+90°$.

Hilbert transform of $\cos(\omega_0 t)$:

$$\mathcal{H}\{\cos(\omega_0 t)\} = \sin(\omega_0 t)$$

Since each positive frequency component of $\cos$ is phase-shifted by $-90°$, $\cos(\omega_0 t - 90°) = \sin(\omega_0 t)$. $\;\blacksquare$

Analytic Signal

Definition

$$z(t) = x(t) + j\,\hat{x}(t)$$

Spectrum of the analytic signal:

$$Z(\omega) = \begin{cases}2X(\omega), & \omega > 0 \\ X(0), & \omega = 0 \\ 0, & \omega < 0\end{cases}$$

Only positive frequencies -- negative frequencies are completely eliminated. This is what "analytic" means: in complex function theory, an analytic function's Fourier transform exists only in the half-plane.

Concrete example: $x(t) = A\cos(\omega_0 t + \phi)$
$\hat{x}(t) = A\sin(\omega_0 t + \phi)$
$z(t) = Ae^{j(\omega_0 t + \phi)}$
$|z(t)| = A$ (constant envelope), $\angle z(t) = \omega_0 t + \phi$ (linear phase).

FFT-Based Hilbert Transform: Three Steps

This is the most commonly used practical implementation (and how MATLAB's hilbert() and SciPy's scipy.signal.hilbert() work internally):

// Hilbert Transform via FFT — returns analytic signal // Input: real signal x (length N) // Output: { re: real part, im: imaginary part } (analytic signal) function hilbert(x) { const N = np2(x.length); // pad to power of 2 const re = zp(x, N); // zero-pad const im = new Array(N).fill(0); const F = fft(re, im); // Step 1: FFT // Step 2: zero out negative frequencies, double positive frequencies // F[0] (DC) and F[N/2] (Nyquist) remain unchanged for (let k = 1; k < N/2; k++) { F.re[k] *= 2; F.im[k] *= 2; } for (let k = N/2 + 1; k < N; k++) { F.re[k] = 0; F.im[k] = 0; } // Step 3: IFFT → obtain analytic signal // IFFT = conjugate → FFT → conjugate → /N const c_re = F.re.slice(); const c_im = F.im.map(v => -v); const R = fft(c_re, c_im); return { re: R.re.map(v => v / N), // real part = original signal x(t) im: R.im.map(v => -v / N) // imaginary part = Hilbert transform x̂(t) }; } // Envelope detection: |z(t)| = sqrt(re² + im²) function envelope(x) { const z = hilbert(x); return z.re.map((r, i) => Math.sqrt(r * r + z.im[i] * z.im[i]) ); }

How to Use

Envelope Detection Workflow

  1. (Critical!) Bandpass filter: First restrict the signal to the narrow frequency band of interest. Computing the Hilbert envelope without filtering first usually gives a physically meaningless result.
  2. Compute the Hilbert transform / analytic signal: Use the FFT three-step method above.
  3. Take the magnitude = envelope: $A(t) = |z(t)| = \sqrt{x^2(t) + \hat{x}^2(t)}$

Concrete Example: AM Demodulation

$x(t) = [1 + 0.8\cos(2\pi \cdot 5\,t)] \cdot \cos(2\pi \cdot 1000\,t)$

Carrier 1000 Hz, modulation wave 5 Hz (modulation depth 80%)

  • Directly compute the Hilbert envelope $|z(t)|$
  • The envelope perfectly recovers $1 + 0.8\cos(2\pi \cdot 5\,t)$ -- the modulation waveform
  • This is the principle of AM envelope detection!

Applications

  1. Communications Demodulation (AM / SSB): AM envelope detection as described above. SSB (single-sideband modulation) uses the Hilbert transform to eliminate half the bandwidth: $x_{\text{SSB}}(t) = x(t)\cos(\omega_c t) \mp \hat{x}(t)\sin(\omega_c t)$ (upper/lower sideband). SSB bandwidth is half that of AM and is the standard modulation method for amateur radio and some military communications.
  2. Bearing Fault Envelope Spectrum Analysis: Accelerometer measures bearing vibration → bandpass filter (focus on 2--5 kHz resonance band) → Hilbert envelope → envelope FFT → find BPFO = 87.3 Hz and its 2X (174.6 Hz), 3X (261.9 Hz) harmonics in the envelope spectrum. ISO 13373-3 and major vibration analysis software (e.g., SKF Microlog, B&K Pulse) use this as the standard procedure.
  3. Speech Fundamental Frequency Tracking: Bandpass filter the speech signal to the fundamental frequency range (80--400 Hz) → Hilbert envelope → autocorrelation of the envelope → first non-zero peak = fundamental period $T_0$ → fundamental frequency $F_0 = 1/T_0$.
  4. Seismic Wave Analysis: The seismic signal's envelope (instantaneous amplitude) is used to determine P-wave and S-wave arrival times and estimate source distance.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Hilbert envelope of a broadband signal has no physical meaning: If the signal is not narrowband (i.e., cannot be written as $x(t) \approx A(t)\cos[\omega_c t + \phi(t)]$), the envelope $|z(t)|$ will fluctuate wildly and be difficult to interpret. You must bandpass filter first to make the signal narrowband before taking the Hilbert envelope. This is the most important usage rule.
  • Edge effect: FFT performs circular convolution; artifacts appear at the signal's beginning and end. Solutions: (a) extend data at both ends (mirror or zero-pad), then trim the middle portion after computation; (b) use overlap-add segmented processing.
  • Discrete-time approximation: The continuous Hilbert transform is non-causal ($1/(\pi t)$ has non-zero values for $t < 0$). The FFT implementation is a finite-length approximation. When signal bandwidth approaches the Nyquist frequency, approximation quality degrades.
  • DC component issue: If the signal has a DC offset, the Hilbert transform's DC component remains in the real part but not the imaginary part, causing bias in envelope estimation. Remove the mean before computing the Hilbert transform.

When NOT to Use?

  • Signal has multiple components with different carriers: The Hilbert envelope will be a mixed envelope of all components, inseparable → bandpass filter first to separate components, or use EMD/HHT (Section 5.6)
  • Need time-frequency analysis (not just an envelope): Hilbert only gives a one-dimensional envelope and instantaneous frequency, not a complete time-frequency distribution → use STFT (Section 5.1) or CWT (Section 5.4)
  • Envelope detection in broadband noise: Filter first, then Hilbert. Without filtering first, the envelope detector will track the random noise envelope, yielding no useful information

Interactive: Envelope Detection

AM modulated signal $x(t) = [1 + m\cos(2\pi f_m t)]\cos(2\pi \cdot 1000\,t)$. The Hilbert envelope (orange) perfectly tracks the modulation waveform. Adjust the modulation frequency $f_m$ to observe the effect.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

References: [1] Gabor, Theory of Communication, J. IEE, 1946. [2] Hahn, Hilbert Transforms in Signal Processing, Artech House, 1996. [3] Marple, Computing the Discrete-Time Analytic Signal via FFT, IEEE Trans. SP, 1999. [4] Feldman, Hilbert Transform Applications in Mechanical Vibration, Wiley, 2011.

Quick Check

Q1: What does the Hilbert transform do in the frequency domain?

Show answer

Positive frequencies are phase-shifted by -90 degrees, negative frequencies by +90 degrees, amplitude unchanged. Equivalent to multiplying by $-j \cdot \text{sgn}(\omega)$.

Q2: Why must you bandpass filter before computing the Hilbert envelope?

Show answer

Because a broadband signal's envelope has no physical meaning. After bandpass filtering, the signal becomes narrowband, and the envelope can correctly reflect modulation characteristics.

← 3.5 Chirp-Z Transform4.2 Envelope & Instantaneous Frequency →

4.2 Envelope & Instantaneous Frequency

Extracting time-varying amplitude and frequency from the analytic signal -- a core tool for mechanical fault diagnosis

Why does this matter? For bearing faults, gear defects, and other rotating machinery problems, fault signatures are not directly visible in the spectrum -- they are hidden in the "envelope" of high-frequency resonance. Envelope spectrum analysis is the standard tool for industrial predictive maintenance.

Previously... Section 4.1 introduced the mathematics of the Hilbert transform. Now let's look at its most important application: extracting the envelope and instantaneous frequency from the analytic signal -- the key technique for bearing fault diagnosis.

Learning Objectives

  • Derive envelope and instantaneous frequency from the polar representation of the analytic signal
  • Understand the limitation that instantaneous frequency is physically meaningful only for narrowband signals
  • Master the complete bearing fault envelope spectrum analysis workflow (6 steps)
  • Compare the Hilbert envelope with traditional rectification + low-pass filtering methods

One-Sentence Summary

The analytic signal's magnitude is the envelope, and the derivative of its phase is the instantaneous frequency -- allowing you to track how a signal's amplitude and frequency change over time.

The Problem

"I want to know the instantaneous frequency of this chirp signal at every moment." A linear chirp sweeps from 100 Hz to 1000 Hz, but a standard FFT only tells you "the signal contains 100--1000 Hz components" -- it does not tell you which time corresponds to which frequency.

"What is the repetition frequency of the bearing impacts?" Accelerometer-measured vibration looks like a mass of high-frequency noise; the impact pattern is invisible to the naked eye. But if you can extract the envelope, the impact pattern emerges, and you can use the envelope spectrum to find the characteristic frequency.

Origin

The concept of instantaneous frequency emerged naturally within Gabor's (1946) framework: the time derivative of the analytic signal's phase $\frac{1}{2\pi}\frac{d\phi}{dt}$ is the instantaneous frequency. However, whether "instantaneous frequency" is physically meaningful was the subject of prolonged academic debate.

Ville (1948) rigorously proved that for narrowband signals, Gabor's instantaneous frequency equals the conditional expectation value (first moment of frequency) of the Wigner-Ville distribution, thus having a clear physical meaning. But for broadband signals, instantaneous frequency can be negative or even infinite -- losing any intuitive meaning as a frequency.

Envelope spectrum analysis as a mechanical fault diagnostic tool was systematically developed by Robert B. Randall and others during the 1980s--1990s, becoming the international standard method for rotating machinery condition monitoring (ISO 13373, ISO 10816 series).

Principle

Writing the analytic signal in polar form:

$z(t) = A(t)\,e^{j\phi(t)}$

$$A(t) = |z(t)| = \sqrt{x^2(t) + \hat{x}^2(t)} \quad \text{(instantaneous amplitude / envelope)}$$ $$\phi(t) = \arg[z(t)] = \arctan\frac{\hat{x}(t)}{x(t)} \quad \text{(instantaneous phase)}$$ $$f_i(t) = \frac{1}{2\pi}\frac{d\phi}{dt} \quad \text{(instantaneous frequency)}$$

Intuition: Think of the signal as a rotating vector (phasor). At each instant, the vector's length = envelope $A(t)$, the vector's angle = phase $\phi(t)$, the vector's rotation speed = instantaneous angular frequency $2\pi f_i(t)$.

Expand: Computing instantaneous frequency in discrete time

In discrete time, phase $\phi[n] = \arctan(\hat{x}[n]/x[n])$. Instantaneous frequency is approximated by differencing:

$$f_i[n] = \frac{f_s}{2\pi}\,\Delta\phi[n] = \frac{f_s}{2\pi}\left(\phi[n] - \phi[n-1]\right)$$

Note: $\arctan$ output is in $(-\pi, \pi]$; the phase difference between adjacent samples may "jump" $\pm 2\pi$ (phase wrapping). You must first perform phase unwrapping before differencing:

$$\Delta\phi_{\text{unwrapped}}[n] = \text{wrap}(\phi[n] - \phi[n-1]) = \Delta\phi[n] - 2\pi\,\text{round}\!\left(\frac{\Delta\phi[n]}{2\pi}\right)$$

Or a more robust method -- compute directly from the analytic signal's real and imaginary parts:

$$f_i[n] = \frac{f_s}{2\pi}\,\text{Im}\!\left(\frac{z[n]\,z^*[n-1]}{|z[n]\,z^*[n-1]|}\right) \cdot \frac{1}{\Delta t}$$

$\blacksquare$

How to Use: Complete Bearing Fault Envelope Spectrum Analysis Workflow

This is one of the most important spectral analysis techniques in rotating machinery condition monitoring. Below is the complete 6-step industrial workflow with specific numerical values:

Step 1: Acquire accelerometer data

Sampling rate $f_s = 25.6\,\text{kHz}$ (common vibration analysis sampling rate, covering up to 10 kHz). Acquire 2 seconds of data → $N = 51200$ points.

Step 2: Bandpass filter (focus on resonance band)

This is the most critical step. Bearing impacts excite structural resonance, with energy concentrated in a high-frequency band (typically 2--10 kHz, depending on the bearing and structure).

Design a bandpass filter: center frequency 3.5 kHz, bandwidth 2--5 kHz. Use a 4th-order Butterworth bandpass filter. Spectral kurtosis can be used to automatically select the optimal filter band (Antoni 2006).

Step 3: Hilbert envelope detection

$A[n] = |z[n]| = \sqrt{x_{\text{filtered}}^2[n] + \hat{x}_{\text{filtered}}^2[n]}$

Result: a series of pulses, each corresponding to one rolling element passing the defect. Pulse spacing = $1/\text{BPFO}$.

Step 4: Low-pass filter the envelope (remove carrier residuals)

The envelope may still contain some high-frequency residuals (carrier remnants). Use a low-pass filter with cutoff frequency $\approx 500\,\text{Hz}$ (well above the expected fault frequency, but well below the carrier frequency).

Step 5: Envelope FFT (envelope spectrum)

Compute the FFT of the low-pass filtered envelope (with Hann window). Frequency resolution $\Delta f = f_s/N = 25600/51200 = 0.5\,\text{Hz}$ (sufficient to resolve fault frequency harmonics).

Step 6: Search for characteristic frequencies in the envelope spectrum

Bearing fault characteristic frequencies (using SKF 6205 bearing at 1797 RPM as an example):

Fault TypeCharacteristic FrequencyValue (Hz)Envelope Spectrum Pattern
Outer race (BPFO)$n_b f_r(1-d/D)/2$87.387.3, 174.6, 261.9 Hz
Inner race (BPFI)$n_b f_r(1+d/D)/2$162.2162.2 Hz $\pm f_r$ sidebands
Rolling element (BSF)$Df_r(1-(d/D)^2)/(2d)$69.6$2\times$ BSF + sidebands

$n_b$=9 (number of rolling elements), $f_r$=29.95 Hz (rotational speed), $d/D$=0.3348 (rolling element diameter / pitch diameter)

Interpretation guide: Seeing BPFO and its 2X, 3X harmonics in the envelope spectrum → outer race fault. Seeing BPFI with $\pm f_r$ sidebands → inner race fault (the inner race rotates with the shaft, and the fault moves in and out of the load zone, producing modulation). The number and relative heights of harmonics indicate fault severity.

Applications

  1. Rotating Machinery Condition Monitoring: Wind turbine gearbox and generator bearings (bearing replacement cost per turbine: $150,000--$300,000 USD + downtime losses). Automated hourly envelope spectrum analysis with BPFO/BPFI energy trend tracking. Early warning can reduce downtime from weeks to planned days. Widely adopted globally in wind farms (e.g., Bruel & Kjaer Vibro, SKF Enlight).
  2. Speech Pitch Tracking (Fundamental Frequency Tracking): Bandpass filter the speech signal to 50--500 Hz → Hilbert envelope → autocorrelation of the envelope or envelope spectrum → fundamental frequency $F_0$. Instantaneous frequency is more direct: bandpass to near the fundamental → instantaneous frequency $f_i(t)$ gives the real-time intonation variation. Used for prosody analysis in speech synthesis and singing pitch detection.
  3. Ultrasonic Nondestructive Testing (NDT): Ultrasonic pulses (center frequency 5 MHz) reflect within materials. Envelope detection extracts echo amplitude as a function of depth (A-scan display). Envelope peak position = defect depth; peak magnitude $\propto$ defect reflectance. Standard inspection method in aerospace, nuclear, and petrochemical industries.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Instantaneous frequency is physically meaningful only for narrowband signals: This limitation bears repeating. If the signal bandwidth is comparable to the center frequency (i.e., $BW \approx f_c$), instantaneous frequency will exhibit rapid fluctuations, even negative values, completely devoid of any "frequency" meaning. Rule of thumb: $BW / f_c < 0.3$ for instantaneous frequency to be reliable.
  • Computing envelope without bandpass filtering first → meaningless result: If the signal contains multiple frequency band components (e.g., bearing vibration with shaft frequency, meshing frequency, and resonance frequency simultaneously), the Hilbert envelope will be a mixture of all components, unable to isolate the fault signature you need.
  • Phase unwrapping difficulty: In low-SNR regions or moments when the envelope is near zero, phase estimation fluctuates wildly, causing pulse-like false values (spikes) in instantaneous frequency. Countermeasures: (a) low-pass filter the instantaneous frequency; (b) trust instantaneous frequency only during time segments where the envelope is sufficiently large.
  • Hilbert envelope vs. rectification + low-pass: Traditional "rectification + low-pass filtering" can also perform envelope detection, but the Hilbert method's advantage is that it does not require choosing a low-pass cutoff frequency (the envelope is produced automatically) and yields more accurate envelope estimates for narrowband signals. Disadvantage: the FFT method requires the entire data segment (non-causal) and is not suitable for real-time processing.

When NOT to Use?

  • Need a complete time-frequency distribution (not just an envelope curve): Use STFT spectrogram (Section 5.1) or CWT scalogram (Section 5.4)
  • Signal is highly non-stationary with multiple time-varying components: Use EMD / HHT (Section 5.6), which adaptively decomposes into multiple IMFs, each of which can then have its Hilbert envelope and instantaneous frequency extracted
  • Real-time (causal) envelope detection: The Hilbert FFT method is non-causal (requires the entire data segment). Real-time systems can use FIR Hilbert filters (with delay) or simple rectification + low-pass filtering

References: [1] Gabor, Theory of Communication, J. IEE, 1946. [2] Randall & Antoni, Rolling Element Bearing Diagnostics — A Tutorial, Mech. Sys. Sig. Proc., 2011. [3] Antoni, The Spectral Kurtosis: A Useful Tool for Characterising Non-Stationary Signals, Mech. Sys. Sig. Proc., 2006. [4] Boashash, Estimating and Interpreting the Instantaneous Frequency of a Signal, Proc. IEEE, 1992.

Worked Example

CNC spindle bearing SKF 6205 (n=9, d=7.94 mm, D=38.5 mm), speed 3600 RPM. (a) Calculate BPFO. (b) How to choose the bandpass filter band? (c) What frequency resolution does the envelope spectrum need?

Show solution

(a) fr=60 Hz, BPFO = (9/2) $\times$ 60 $\times$ (1 - 7.94/38.5) = 214.2 Hz

(b) Choose a high-frequency resonance band, typically 2--8 kHz (determined by the actual measured frequency response function)

(c) Need to see BPFO $\approx$ 214 Hz and its 2x = 428 Hz, so $\Delta f$ should be at least < 5 Hz → observation time $\geq$ 0.2 seconds

← 4.1 Hilbert Transform4.3 Cepstrum Analysis →

4.3 Cepstrum Analysis

The spectrum of the spectrum -- discovering hidden periodic structures in the "quefrency" domain

Why does this matter? When the spectrum contains periodic patterns (harmonic families, sideband families, echoes), the cepstrum reveals that periodicity at a glance -- it is the classic tool for speech pitch detection and gearbox analysis.

Previously... Section 4.2 used envelope analysis to find periodic impacts in the time domain. But sometimes the periodicity is not in the time domain but in the frequency domain -- the spectrum contains a set of equally spaced peaks (harmonic families, sideband families). The cepstrum is the tool for analyzing "periodicity in the spectrum."

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the cepstrum definition: real cepstrum vs. complex cepstrum
  • Establish the correspondence between quefrency-axis peaks and periodic spectral structures
  • Master cepstrum applications in speech pitch detection and gearbox fault analysis
  • Recognize numerical issues arising from logarithm computation and phase unwrapping

One-Sentence Summary

Cepstrum = spectrum of the spectrum. When the spectrum contains periodic patterns (such as equally spaced sidebands or harmonic families), the cepstrum helps you find that periodicity.

The Problem: Periodic Patterns in the Spectrum

Scenario 1: Gearbox fault. Gear meshing vibration produces a spectrum centered on the Gear Mesh Frequency (GMF) with a whole family of equally spaced sidebands, spaced at the shaft rotational frequency. For example, GMF = 600 Hz, rotational speed = 30 Hz; you see peaks at 510, 540, 570, 600, 630, 660, 690 Hz -- the human eye can see they are equally spaced (30 Hz apart), but automatically detecting "what is the spacing of these peaks" algorithmically is not straightforward.

Scenario 2: Speech pitch detection. A speech signal's spectrum has a set of harmonics: $F_0, 2F_0, 3F_0, \ldots$, where $F_0$ is the fundamental frequency (male $\approx$ 100 Hz, female $\approx$ 200 Hz). These harmonics form an equally spaced pattern in the spectrum with spacing = $F_0$. You need a method to automatically find this equally spaced interval.

The cepstrum is designed precisely for this: it takes the Fourier transform of the spectrum once more, converting "periodic patterns in the spectrum" into "peaks in the cepstrum."

Origin

B.P. Bogert, M.J.R. Healy, and the renowned statistician John W. Tukey (1963) at Bell Labs proposed the cepstrum during a seismic wave analysis study. Their original motivation was detecting echoes in seismic signals: seismic waves reflecting through geological layers produce delayed copies. In the spectrum, this appears as periodic ripple, where the ripple "frequency" = reciprocal of the echo delay.

In his characteristic humorous style, Tukey reversed the letters of all related terminology:

OriginalReversedMeaning
spectrumcepstrumSpectrum of the spectrum
frequencyquefrencyCepstrum horizontal axis (units: time)
harmonicsrahmonics"Harmonics" in the cepstrum
filteringlifteringFiltering operations in the cepstral domain

The paper title itself is full of Tukey's style: "The Quefrency Alanysis of Time Series for Echoes: Cepstrum, Pseudo-Autocovariance, Cross-Cepstrum, and Saphe Cracking". Yes, even "alanysis" is a deliberate reversal of "analysis."

The cepstrum later found its broadest applications in speech processing and mechanical fault diagnosis. In speech processing, it forms the basis of MFCC (Mel-Frequency Cepstral Coefficients) -- one of the most important features in speech recognition.

Principle

Intuition: If the spectrum has equally spaced peaks (spacing $\Delta f$), the spectrum has a "frequency" = $\Delta f$. Taking the FFT of the spectrum one more time will find this "frequency" -- it appears as a peak at quefrency = $1/\Delta f$.

Real / Power Cepstrum

$$c[n] = \text{IFFT}\left\{\log\left|X[k]\right|\right\} = \text{IFFT}\left\{\log\left|\text{FFT}\{x[n]\}\right|\right\}$$

Complex Cepstrum

$$\hat{c}[n] = \text{IFFT}\left\{\log X[k]\right\} = \text{IFFT}\left\{\log|X[k]| + j\,\angle X[k]\right\}$$

Meaning of the quefrency axis:

  • The unit of quefrency is time (seconds or sample counts)
  • A peak at quefrency $= \tau$ → the spectrum has a periodic structure with spacing $= 1/\tau$ Hz
  • Peak height $\propto$ strength of the periodic structure
Expand: Why take the log?

The cepstrum's design has a deep reason: homomorphic deconvolution.

Many signals can be modeled as the convolution of two components:

$$x[n] = s[n] * h[n] \quad \Longleftrightarrow \quad X[k] = S[k] \cdot H[k]$$

Taking the logarithm:

$$\log|X[k]| = \log|S[k]| + \log|H[k]|$$

Convolution becomes multiplication in the frequency domain; taking the log turns it into addition. Then taking the IFFT:

$$c_x[n] = c_s[n] + c_h[n]$$

If $c_s$ and $c_h$ occupy different regions in the quefrency domain, liftering (windowing/filtering in the cepstral domain) can separate them.

Speech example: Speech $x = e * v$ (glottal excitation $e$ convolved with vocal tract impulse response $v$).

  • Vocal tract $v$'s cepstrum is concentrated at low quefrency ($< 3$ ms) → spectral envelope (formants)
  • Excitation $e$'s cepstrum has peaks at high quefrency ($= T_0 \approx 5$--$10$ ms) → fundamental frequency

Use a low-time lifter to keep only low quefrency → retains only the vocal tract response → formants can be extracted.

Find peaks at high quefrency → fundamental frequency estimation. $\;\blacksquare$

How to Use: Three Steps

Step 1: FFT → magnitude → log → IFFT → real cepstrum

$c[n] = \text{IFFT}\{\log|X[k]|\}$. Note that $|X[k]|$ may have zero values → add a small constant $\varepsilon$ to avoid $\log(0)$: $\log(|X[k]| + \varepsilon)$.

Step 2: Search for peaks on the quefrency axis

Ignore the DC/low-frequency components near quefrency $\approx 0$. Search for peaks within a reasonable quefrency range (based on expected periodic structures).

Step 3: Peak position $\tau$ → corresponding spectral periodic structure spacing $1/\tau$

Confirm results via the peak's "rahmonics" ($2\tau, 3\tau, \ldots$).

// Real Cepstrum Computation (JavaScript) function realCepstrum(x) { const N = np2(x.length); const re = zp(x, N); const im = new Array(N).fill(0); const F = fft(re, im); // log|X[k]| const eps = 1e-12; const logMag = F.re.map((r, k) => { const mag = Math.sqrt(r * r + F.im[k] * F.im[k]); return Math.log(mag + eps); }); // IFFT of logMag (real input → use FFT trick) const imZ = new Array(N).fill(0); const C = fft(logMag, imZ); // FFT of log|X| // IFFT = conjugate, FFT, conjugate, /N return C.re.map(v => v / N); // real cepstrum }

Concrete Example: Gearbox Fault Detection

  • Gearbox: number of teeth $z = 20$, speed $N_r = 1800\,\text{RPM} = 30\,\text{Hz}$
  • Gear Mesh Frequency GMF = $z \times f_r = 20 \times 30 = 600\,\text{Hz}$
  • If the gear has a local defect → spectrum shows equally spaced sidebands around 600 Hz: $\ldots, 540, 570, 600, 630, 660, \ldots$ Hz
  • Sideband spacing = $30\,\text{Hz}$ (= rotational frequency)
  • Cepstrum shows a clear peak at quefrency $= 1/30 = 33.3\,\text{ms}$
  • As well as smaller peaks at $66.7\,\text{ms}$ (2X) and $100\,\text{ms}$ (3X) (rahmonics)
  • Conclusion: the spectrum has a periodic structure with 30 Hz spacing → pointing to a gear fault modulated at the rotational frequency

Applications

  1. Speech Pitch Detection: A speech signal's spectrum has a harmonic series $F_0, 2F_0, \ldots, nF_0$. The cepstrum shows a peak at quefrency $= 1/F_0$. Male $F_0 \approx 100\,\text{Hz}$ → peak at 10 ms; female $F_0 \approx 200\,\text{Hz}$ → peak at 5 ms. This is one of the classic pitch detection methods (alongside autocorrelation), widely used in speech coding (G.729) and music analysis. MFCC features are a direct descendant of cepstral analysis.
  2. Gearbox Fault Sideband Analysis: As in the example above. The cepstrum can automatically extract sideband spacing from complex spectra without manually identifying spectral peak patterns. Industrial software such as B&K PULSE and SKF @ptitude include built-in cepstrum analysis. ISO 13373-9 specifically standardizes cepstrum application in gearbox diagnosis.
  3. Echo Detection and Removal: If the signal is $y[n] = x[n] + \alpha x[n-D]$ (original signal plus a delayed echo at delay $D$), the spectrum $|Y| = |X|\cdot|1 + \alpha e^{-j\omega D}|$. $\log|Y| = \log|X| + \log|1 + \alpha e^{-j\omega D}|$. The second term is periodic with period $2\pi/D$ → the cepstrum shows a peak at quefrency $= D$, precisely locating the echo delay. Used in audio post-production and telephone echo cancellation.
  4. Mechanical Fault Transfer Path Isolation: The vibration signal $X = S \cdot H$ (excitation source $S$ $\times$ transfer path $H$). In the cepstrum, $c_x = c_s + c_h$. The excitation source's periodic characteristics (e.g., bearing fault frequency) appear at specific quefrencies; the transfer path's effect (structural resonance envelope) is concentrated at low quefrency. Liftering can separate them, making fault signatures clearer.

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • $\log(0)$ problem: If $|X[k]| = 0$ (spectral zero), $\log(0) = -\infty$. In practice, add a small constant: $\log(|X[k]| + \varepsilon)$, where $\varepsilon \approx 10^{-10}$ to $10^{-12}$. Too large an $\varepsilon$ will distort the cepstrum shape.
  • Complex cepstrum phase unwrapping is extremely tricky: The complex cepstrum requires $\log X = \log|X| + j\angle X$, where $\angle X$ must be continuous (unwrapped phase). But the discrete spectrum's phase is defined only in $(-\pi, \pi]$, and unwrapping algorithms frequently fail in the presence of noise. In practice, usually only the real cepstrum (power cepstrum) is used, avoiding the phase issue entirely.
  • Frequency resolution vs. quefrency resolution tradeoff: Cepstrum quefrency resolution = $1/f_s$ (one sampling interval). To resolve two quefrency peaks that are very close (i.e., two nearly identical spectral periodicities), very high frequency resolution (long FFT) is needed, which in turn requires very long data.
  • Real cepstrum is an even function: $c[n] = c[-n]$ (since taking log yields a real value, the IFFT result is symmetric). Only the $n > 0$ portion needs to be examined.
  • Not suitable for non-periodic spectral features: The cepstrum excels at detecting "equally spaced patterns in the spectrum." If the fault spectrum is not equally spaced sidebands but a broadband elevation or single peak shift, the cepstrum is less useful than directly examining the spectrum.

When NOT to Use?

  • No periodic pattern in the spectrum: The cepstrum's strength is detecting "periodicity in the spectrum." If your analysis target is single frequency peaks or broadband noise characteristics, examining the spectrum or PSD directly is more effective
  • Need precise power/energy measurements: The logarithm operation destroys linear power relationships. If precise spectral energy values are needed → use Welch PSD (Section 3.2)
  • Analyzing bearing faults (not gearbox): Bearing faults are typically diagnosed more directly using envelope spectrum (Section 4.2). The cepstrum is better suited for gearboxes (since gear sidebands are a classic equally spaced pattern)
  • Real-time speech pitch tracking: The cepstrum method requires FFT of the entire speech segment, introducing significant latency. Real-time systems more commonly use autocorrelation or the YIN algorithm

References: [1] Bogert, Healy & Tukey, The Quefrency Alanysis of Time Series for Echoes: Cepstrum, Pseudo-Autocovariance, Cross-Cepstrum, and Saphe Cracking, Proc. Symposium on Time Series Analysis, 1963. [2] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, Ch.13 (Homomorphic Signal Processing). [3] Randall, Vibration-based Condition Monitoring: Industrial, Automotive and Aerospace Applications, Wiley, 2011. [4] Noll, Cepstrum Pitch Determination, JASA, 1967.

Interactive: Cepstrum Echo Detection

Original signal plus a delayed echo at delay D. The cepstrum shows a peak at quefrency = D.
