← 4.2 Envelope & Instantaneous Frequency5.1 STFT →

5.1 Short-Time Fourier Transform (STFT)

The cornerstone of time-frequency analysis — sliding a window and performing FFT as you go

Why does this matter? Because most real-world signals have frequencies that change over time. FFT only tells you "which frequencies are present" but not "when they appear." STFT is the most fundamental time-frequency analysis tool and serves as the benchmark for all advanced methods.

Previously... The analysis methods in Part IV assumed that a signal's frequency characteristics do not change over time. But real signals are usually non-stationary. STFT lets you see both "when" and "what frequency" simultaneously.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the core idea of STFT: windowed segmentation + FFT, bringing time information into frequency analysis
  • Master the selection logic for three key parameters: window length, overlap ratio, and NFFT
  • Interpret typical patterns in a Spectrogram
  • Understand the fundamental limitation of the Heisenberg uncertainty principle on STFT resolution

One-Sentence Summary

STFT is "sliding a window and performing FFT as you go" — it lets you see how frequencies change over time. Cut a long signal into many short segments, perform FFT on each segment, then arrange the results along the time axis to obtain a Spectrogram.

Pain Point: FFT Loses "Time"

Standard FFT tells you "which frequency components are in the signal," but gives you no information about when these components appear. For time-varying signals, this is a critical flaw:

  • Speech: When transitioning from vowel /a/ to /i/, the formant frequencies change dramatically within 50 ms. FFT simply mixes all frequencies together
  • Engine vibration: When accelerating from idle at 800 rpm to 6000 rpm, the dominant vibration frequency rises continuously. FFT only tells you "frequencies from 800 to 6000 rpm are all present"
  • Seismic waves: P-waves (longitudinal, high-frequency arrive first) and S-waves (transverse, low-frequency arrive later) require simultaneous analysis of arrival time and frequency characteristics
  • Music: The onset and offset times of each note in a melody, pitch changes, vibrato — all require simultaneous time + frequency analysis

Fundamental problem: The basis functions of the Fourier transform, $e^{j\omega t}$, extend to $\pm\infty$ in time, so it inherently cannot provide time localization. We need a method to "localize" the signal to finite time segments before analysis.

Origin

Dennis Gabor (1946) first proposed using a Gaussian-windowed STFT (which he called "logons") to analyze communication signals in his seminal paper Theory of Communication. Gabor's core insight was that the information in a signal lies not only in frequency but also in time — we need a joint time-frequency representation.

J. B. Allen (1977) systematized the theory of discrete STFT in his IEEE paper Short Term Spectral Analysis, Synthesis, and Modification by Discrete Fourier Transform, establishing a complete framework including window function selection and Overlap-Add reconstruction, laying the foundation for modern digital speech processing.

Principle

Intuition: Imagine you are viewing a recording of a concert. Instead of listening to the entire song at once and then analyzing the frequencies (that's FFT), you take a "snapshot" at regular intervals — recording which frequencies are present and how loud they are at that moment. Arranging all snapshots in a row gives you the Spectrogram.

Procedure:

  1. Choose a window function $w[n]$ (e.g., Hann window) of length $L$ samples
  2. Slide the window to position $m$ in the signal, extracting the local segment $x[n] \cdot w[n-m]$
  3. Perform FFT on this local segment to obtain the local spectrum at position $m$
  4. Slide the window by $H$ samples (Hop Size), and repeat steps 2-3
  5. Arrange all local spectra along the time axis to form the Spectrogram

Discrete STFT Definition

$$\text{STFT}\{x\}[m, k] = \sum_{n=0}^{L-1} x[n + mH]\, w[n]\, e^{-j2\pi kn/N_{\text{FFT}}}$$

$m$: time frame index, $k$: frequency bin index, $H$: Hop Size, $L$: window length, $N_{\text{FFT}}$: FFT length

Spectrogram

$$S[m,k] = |\text{STFT}\{x\}[m,k]|^2$$

Taking the squared magnitude yields the power spectral density as a function of time

Show full derivation: Continuous STFT definition and properties

Continuous STFT:

$$\text{STFT}\{x\}(t,\omega) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(\tau)\, w(\tau - t)\, e^{-j\omega\tau}\, d\tau$$

This can be interpreted as the inner product of $x(\tau)$ and $w(\tau - t)e^{j\omega\tau}$ — i.e., the "component magnitude" of the signal near time $t$ and frequency $\omega$.

Inverse transform (reconstruction):

$$x(t) = \frac{1}{2\pi \|w\|^2} \int_{-\infty}^{\infty}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \text{STFT}\{x\}(\tau,\omega)\, w(t-\tau)\, e^{j\omega t}\, d\omega\, d\tau$$

The prerequisite is that the window function $w(t) \neq 0$ (non-zero window), ensuring perfect reconstruction.

Energy conservation (Parseval-like):

$$\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} |x(t)|^2\, dt = \frac{1}{2\pi \|w\|^2} \int\!\!\int |\text{STFT}\{x\}(t,\omega)|^2\, dt\, d\omega$$

Heisenberg Uncertainty Principle

The time resolution $\Delta t$ and frequency resolution $\Delta f$ of STFT cannot both be arbitrarily good simultaneously:

$$\Delta t \cdot \Delta f \geq \frac{1}{4\pi}$$

Longer window $\to$ smaller $\Delta f$ (frequency is seen more clearly) $\to$ but larger $\Delta t$ (time becomes more blurry), and vice versa. This is not a technical limitation but a fundamental mathematical limit. STFT uses the same window across the entire time-frequency plane $\to$ time-frequency resolution is the same everywhere $\to$ this is a fixed rectangular tiling.

How to Use: Practical Parameter Selection Guide

Step 1: Determine your required frequency resolution $\Delta f$

Frequency resolution is determined by window length: $\Delta f \approx f_s / L$ ($L$ = window length in samples).

Formula: If you want $\Delta f = 4$ Hz with sampling rate $f_s = 1000$ Hz $\to$ window length $L = f_s / \Delta f = 1000/4 = 250$ samples.

Step 2: Choose the window function

Window FunctionMain Lobe WidthSidelobe AttenuationSuitable For
RectangularNarrowest ($2f_s/L$)-13 dB (worst)Transient analysis, known signals with no leakage
Hann$4f_s/L$-31 dBGeneral-purpose first choice
Hamming$4f_s/L$-43 dBSpeech analysis
Blackman-Harris$8f_s/L$-92 dBHigh dynamic range requirements
Gaussian ($\alpha$=2.5)AdjustableNo sidelobes (theoretically)Gabor analysis, minimum time-frequency area

Step 3: Determine the overlap ratio

Overlap ratio = $(L - H)/L \times 100\%$, where $H$ = Hop Size.

  • 50% (Hann window): The minimum overlap that satisfies the COLA (Constant Overlap-Add) condition, guaranteeing perfect reconstruction
  • 75%: Smoother time axis, better time resolution, recommended for most cases
  • 87.5%: For very fine time tracking (e.g., pitch tracking)

Time resolution: $\Delta t = H / f_s$ (time interval between consecutive frames).

Step 4: Choose NFFT

NFFT $\geq$ window length $L$, rounded up to the next power of 2 (for maximum FFT efficiency). If NFFT $>$ L $\to$ zero-padding $\to$ denser frequency axis (interpolation effect, but does not increase true frequency resolution).

Scenario 1: Speech Analysis

ParameterValueRationale
$f_s$16 kHzStandard sampling rate for telephony/speech recognition
Window length $L$512 samples = 32 msCovers 2-3 fundamental frequency periods (male $F_0 \approx 100$ Hz $\to$ 10 ms/period)
Overlap75% $\to$ $H$ = 128 samplesSmooth tracking of formant changes
NFFT512Already a power of 2
$\Delta f$$16000/512 = 31.25$ HzSufficient to distinguish adjacent formants
$\Delta t$$128/16000 = 8$ msSufficient to track rapid speech changes

Scenario 2: Mechanical Vibration Analysis

ParameterValueRationale
$f_s$25.6 kHzCommon for vibration analysis (frequency range up to 10 kHz)
Window length $L$4096 samples = 160 msFine frequency resolution needed to distinguish closely spaced gear meshing frequencies
Overlap75% $\to$ $H$ = 1024 samplesTrack speed variations
NFFT4096Already a power of 2
$\Delta f$$25600/4096 = 6.25$ HzCan distinguish harmonics spaced 10 Hz apart
$\Delta t$$1024/25600 = 40$ msSufficient for tracking moderate speed changes

Interactive: Spectrogram and the Window Length Trade-off

Select different signals and window lengths to observe how the Spectrogram changes. Longer windows yield a clearer frequency axis but a blurrier time axis, and vice versa.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. Speech recognition front-end (Mel-Spectrogram): The input to modern ASR systems (e.g., Whisper) is STFT $\to$ Mel filter bank $\to$ log. Typical settings: 25 ms window, 10 ms hop, 80 Mel bins. This produces 100 frames/second $\times$ 80-dimensional feature matrices
  2. Music Information Retrieval (MIR): Analyzing chord progressions, melody lines, and rhythmic structure in songs. Uses longer windows (2048-4096 samples @44.1 kHz = 46-93 ms) for sufficient frequency resolution to distinguish semitones
  3. Vibration order tracking: During engine acceleration, diagonal lines on the STFT spectrogram represent frequency trajectories of various orders. Engineers use this to find resonance points (sudden amplitude increases at certain RPMs)
  4. Seismic wave analysis: Spectrograms can distinguish P-waves (arrive first, high frequency) from S-waves (arrive later, low frequency), aiding in seismic wave characterization
  5. EEG event-related spectral analysis: Analyzing brainwave frequency changes following specific stimuli (e.g., alpha wave suppression, gamma wave enhancement), with window lengths of 0.5-2 seconds

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Window too long $\to$ time blurring: If the signal's frequency changes dramatically within 10 ms but you use a 100 ms window, the change gets "averaged out" and appears as a blurry blob on the Spectrogram
  • Window too short $\to$ frequency blurring: A 32-sample window @1 kHz $\to$ $\Delta f = 31$ Hz, which cannot distinguish between 440 Hz and 460 Hz tones
  • There is no "perfect" window length: The Heisenberg uncertainty principle guarantees you cannot simultaneously achieve arbitrarily good time and frequency resolution. This is a law of physics, not a technical deficiency
  • Spectrogram discards phase: $|STFT|^2$ throws away phase information. When phase is needed (e.g., signal reconstruction, Griffin-Lim algorithm), the full STFT must be retained
  • Output size calculation: A 10-second music clip at $f_s = 44.1$ kHz, window length 2048, hop 512, NFFT 2048 $\to$ time frames $\approx 860$, frequency bins = 1025 $\to$ approximately 880,000 complex values. Memory requirements for long signals can be substantial

When Not to Use STFT? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Need multi-resolution (frequency detail at low freq, time detail at high freq)STFT's fixed window cannot adaptCWT (Wavelet Transform) $\to$ Section 5.4
Analyzing extremely short transients (< a few cycles)Frequency becomes meaningless when window is not short enoughWVD $\to$ Section 5.2 or Matching Pursuit
Nonlinear, non-stationary complex signalsSinusoidal basis is not suitableEMD / HHT $\to$ Section 5.6
Only need to track a few specific frequenciesComputing the full STFT spectrum is wastefulGoertzel algorithm or Chirp-Z Transform

References: [1] Gabor, D., Theory of Communication, J. IEE, 93(26):429-457, 1946. [2] Allen, J.B., Short Term Spectral Analysis, Synthesis, and Modification by Discrete Fourier Transform, IEEE Trans. ASSP, 25(3):235-238, 1977. [3] Oppenheim & Schafer, Discrete-Time Signal Processing, 3rd ed., Ch.10. [4] Griffin & Lim, Signal Estimation from Modified Short-Time Fourier Transform, IEEE Trans. ASSP, 1984.

Quick Check

Q1: For speech analysis using a 32 ms window (fs=16 kHz $\to$ 512 samples), what is the frequency resolution?

Show answer

$\Delta f$ = fs/N = 16000/512 = 31.25 Hz. Sufficient to distinguish speech formants (spaced ~500-1000 Hz apart).

Q2: What happens if the STFT window is too short? Too long?

Show answer

Too short $\to$ frequency blurring (cannot see frequencies clearly); too long $\to$ time blurring (cannot see rapid changes). There is no perfect window length.

← 4.3 Cepstral Analysis5.2 Wigner-Ville Distribution →

5.2 Wigner-Ville Distribution (WVD)

The theoretically highest-resolution time-frequency distribution — but at the cost of "ghost images"

Why does this matter? Because STFT's time-frequency resolution is limited by the uncertainty principle. WVD breaks through this limitation — at the cost of cross-terms. Understanding WVD is the foundation for understanding all quadratic time-frequency distributions.

Previously... The STFT from Section 5.1 is limited by the uncertainty principle: a fixed window length means fixed time-frequency resolution. WVD attempts to break through this limitation — at the cost of cross-terms.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the definition of WVD and its relationship to local autocorrelation functions
  • Prove that WVD satisfies perfect marginal properties, free from Heisenberg limitations
  • Understand the cause, location, and magnitude of cross-terms
  • Master the smoothing strategies of Pseudo-WVD and Smoothed-WVD

One-Sentence Summary

WVD is theoretically the highest-resolution time-frequency distribution — but it produces "ghost images" (cross-terms) in unexpected places. Like a funhouse mirror: the subject is seen very clearly, but strange phantom reflections appear alongside it.

Pain Point: Can We Break Through Heisenberg?

STFT's time-frequency resolution is limited by the Heisenberg inequality $\Delta t \cdot \Delta f \geq 1/(4\pi)$ — the window length is fixed, and time and frequency resolution trade off against each other.

Is there a way to break through this limitation and simultaneously achieve perfect time resolution and frequency resolution? WVD's answer is: yes, but at a cost.

Origin

Eugene Wigner (1932) proposed this distribution in quantum mechanics to describe the "quasi-probability distribution" of quantum states in phase space (position-momentum). Wigner noted that it can take negative values — impossible in classical probability — reflecting the non-classical nature of quantum mechanics.

Jean Ville (1948) independently introduced the same mathematical formulation into signal processing, using it to analyze the instantaneous frequency and power of signals. Hence it is called the Wigner-Ville distribution.

Interestingly, Wigner later received the 1963 Nobel Prize in Physics — but not for the WVD; rather for his contributions to the theory of atomic nuclei and elementary particles.

Principle

Intuition: At each time $t$, compute the "local autocorrelation" of the signal (centered at $t$, looking $\tau/2$ forward and backward), then Fourier transform over the lag $\tau$ to obtain the "local spectrum" at that instant.

Wigner-Ville Distribution

$$W_x(t,\omega) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x\!\left(t + \frac{\tau}{2}\right)\, x^*\!\left(t - \frac{\tau}{2}\right)\, e^{-j\omega\tau}\, d\tau$$

Interpretation: $x(t+\tau/2) \cdot x^*(t-\tau/2)$ is the "instantaneous autocorrelation function" centered at $t$. Taking the FT over $\tau$ is analogous to the Wiener-Khinchin theorem: the FT of autocorrelation = power spectrum. Except here it is local and time-varying.

Perfect Marginal Properties

WVD satisfies the following remarkable properties:

$$\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} W_x(t,\omega)\, \frac{d\omega}{2\pi} = |x(t)|^2 \quad \text{(time marginal = instantaneous power)}$$ $$\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} W_x(t,\omega)\, dt = |X(\omega)|^2 \quad \text{(frequency marginal = power spectral density)}$$ $$\int\!\!\int W_x(t,\omega)\, dt\, \frac{d\omega}{2\pi} = \|x\|^2 = E_x \quad \text{(total energy)}$$

This means WVD is an "ideal" energy distribution — projections along time and frequency precisely equal the instantaneous power and power spectrum, respectively. No Heisenberg inequality limitation.

Show full derivation: Proof of the time marginal property

Compute $\int W_x(t,\omega)\, d\omega/(2\pi)$:

$$\int \frac{d\omega}{2\pi} \int x\!\left(t+\frac{\tau}{2}\right) x^*\!\left(t-\frac{\tau}{2}\right) e^{-j\omega\tau}\, d\tau$$

Swap the order of integration:

$$= \int x\!\left(t+\frac{\tau}{2}\right) x^*\!\left(t-\frac{\tau}{2}\right) \underbrace{\left[\int \frac{e^{-j\omega\tau}}{2\pi}\, d\omega\right]}_{\delta(\tau)}\, d\tau$$

Using the sifting property of $\delta(\tau)$, set $\tau = 0$:

$$= x(t)\, x^*(t) = |x(t)|^2 \quad \blacksquare$$
Show full derivation: Exact WVD result for a single-component chirp

Consider a linear chirp $x(t) = e^{j(\omega_0 t + \beta t^2/2)}$ (instantaneous frequency $\omega_i(t) = \omega_0 + \beta t$).

Substituting into WVD:

$$x\!\left(t+\frac{\tau}{2}\right) x^*\!\left(t-\frac{\tau}{2}\right) = e^{j(\omega_0 + \beta t)\tau}$$

Therefore:

$$W_x(t,\omega) = \int e^{j(\omega_0+\beta t)\tau}\, e^{-j\omega\tau}\, d\tau = 2\pi\,\delta(\omega - \omega_0 - \beta t)$$

The WVD is precisely concentrated on the instantaneous frequency line $\omega_i(t) = \omega_0 + \beta t$ in the time-frequency plane — perfect time-frequency localization with no blurring. $\blacksquare$

The Fatal Flaw: Cross-Terms

For a multi-component signal $x = x_1 + x_2$:

$$W_{x_1+x_2}(t,\omega) = \underbrace{W_{x_1}(t,\omega)}_{\text{auto-term}} + \underbrace{W_{x_2}(t,\omega)}_{\text{auto-term}} + \underbrace{2\,\text{Re}\left[W_{x_1,x_2}(t,\omega)\right]}_{\text{cross-term}}$$

where the cross-term $W_{x_1,x_2}(t,\omega) = \int x_1(t+\tau/2)\, x_2^*(t-\tau/2)\, e^{-j\omega\tau}\, d\tau$.

Three alarming properties of cross-terms:

  • Location: Appears at the midpoint of the time-frequency positions of $x_1$ and $x_2$ (averaging both time and frequency)
  • Magnitude: Can be as large as the auto-terms, or even larger
  • Oscillation: Cross-terms oscillate rapidly (in the direction of the time-frequency difference between the two components), with frequency proportional to the time-frequency distance between the two components

For a signal with $N$ components, there are $N$ auto-terms but $N(N-1)/2$ cross-terms — when $N$ is large, cross-terms completely overwhelm the auto-terms!

How to Use

  1. Compute the analytic signal: First apply the Hilbert transform to $x(t)$ to obtain the analytic signal $z(t) = x(t) + j\hat{x}(t)$. This eliminates cross-terms between positive and negative frequencies (which would otherwise appear near zero frequency and are quite annoying)
  2. Compute the discrete WVD:
    % MATLAB / Octave conceptual code N = length(z); WV = zeros(N, N); for n = 1:N for tau = -(N-1):(N-1) n1 = n + round(tau/2); n2 = n - round(tau/2); if n1 >= 1 && n1 <= N && n2 >= 1 && n2 <= N WV(n, :) = WV(n, :) + z(n1)*conj(z(n2)) * exp(-1j*2*pi*(0:N-1)*tau/N); end end end

    Note: computational complexity is $O(N^2)$, far greater than STFT's $O(N \log N)$.

  3. Smooth with a kernel function to suppress cross-terms:
    • Pseudo-WVD (PWVD): Apply a window only in the $\tau$ direction $\to$ suppresses cross-term components far from $\tau=0$
    • Smoothed Pseudo-WVD (SPWVD): Apply windows in both $t$ and $\tau$ directions $\to$ stronger cross-term suppression, but greater resolution loss
  4. Visualization: Plot $W_x(t,\omega)$. Note that WVD can take negative values — this is not an error, but its non-classical property (analogous to quasi-probability distributions in quantum mechanics)

Applications

  1. Precise time-frequency analysis of single-component chirp signals: For linear frequency-modulated (LFM chirp) pulses in radar returns, WVD achieves the theoretical limit of precision. For example: a 10 MHz bandwidth, 100 $\mu$s pulse-width radar chirp — WVD perfectly recovers its time-frequency slope $\beta = 10^{11}$ Hz/s
  2. Radar ambiguity function: The ambiguity function $A(\tau, \nu)$ is exactly the 2D Fourier transform of the WVD. Thus, WVD properties directly correspond to the range-velocity resolution capability of radar waveforms
  3. Quantum optics / Quantum information: The Wigner function remains the primary tool for describing quantum states of optical fields (e.g., squeezed states, Fock states, cat states)

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Nearly unusable for multi-component signals: 3 components produce 3 cross-terms; 10 components produce 45 cross-terms. After smoothing, the resolution advantage is largely diminished
  • Computational complexity $O(N^2)$: Much greater than STFT's $O(N \log N)$. At N = 10000, WVD requires approximately 750 times more computation than STFT
  • Can take negative values: Cannot be directly interpreted as an "energy distribution" — although the marginals are correct, local negative values make physical interpretation difficult
  • Discretization difficulties: 2x oversampling is required to avoid aliasing in the discrete WVD

When Not to Use WVD? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Multi-component signalsToo many cross-termsSmoothed Pseudo-WVD or Choi-Williams $\to$ 5.3 Cohen's Class
Need fast computation$O(N^2)$ too slowSTFT ($O(N\log N)$)
Nonlinear non-stationary signalsQuadratic distribution assumptions are not flexible enoughEMD / HHT $\to$ Section 5.6
Need cross-term-free high resolutionWVD's cross-terms cannot be eliminatedSST $\to$ Section 5.7

References: [1] Wigner, E., On the Quantum Correction for Thermodynamic Equilibrium, Phys. Rev., 40:749-759, 1932. [2] Ville, J., Theorie et Applications de la Notion de Signal Analytique, Cables et Transmission, 2(1):61-74, 1948. [3] Claasen, T.A.C.M. & Mecklenbräuker, W.F.G., The Wigner Distribution, Philips J. Res., 1980. [4] Cohen, L., Time-Frequency Analysis, Prentice Hall, 1995.

← 5.1 STFT5.3 Cohen's Class →

5.3 Cohen's Class: Generalized Quadratic Time-Frequency Distributions

A unified framework — all quadratic time-frequency distributions are smoothed versions of WVD

Why does this matter? Because STFT and WVD are just two extremes of time-frequency analysis. Cohen's Class provides a unified mathematical framework that reveals how all quadratic time-frequency distributions are different choices of "how to smooth WVD."

Previously... The WVD from Section 5.2 has cross-term problems. The STFT from Section 5.1 has no cross-terms but poor resolution. Cohen's Class provides a unified framework for finding the optimal trade-off between the two.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the unified formula of Cohen's Class: the kernel function $\Phi(\theta,\tau)$ completely determines the distribution's properties
  • Recognize the trade-off between kernel functions and auto-term preservation / cross-term suppression
  • Compare specific distributions such as WVD, Spectrogram, and Choi-Williams
  • Select an appropriate kernel function based on signal characteristics

One-Sentence Summary

Cohen's Class is a unified framework: the STFT Spectrogram, WVD, and everything in between — the only difference is "which kernel function is used to smooth the WVD." Choosing a kernel is like turning a dial on a radio: one end gives the highest resolution but with cross-terms (WVD), the other end gives no cross-terms but is blurry (Spectrogram).

Pain Point: Can We Find a Compromise Between STFT and WVD?

We now face two extremes:

  • STFT Spectrogram: No cross-terms ✓ but resolution limited by Heisenberg ✗
  • WVD: Resolution not limited ✓ but severe cross-terms for multi-component signals ✗

Is there a distribution that lies between the two — retaining most of the resolution advantage while effectively suppressing cross-terms? Cohen's Class provides a systematic answer.

Origin

Leon Cohen (1966, 1989) proposed this unified theory. His core insight was that all "reasonable" quadratic time-frequency distributions (i.e., those satisfying time-shift and frequency-shift covariance) can be described by a single mathematical framework, differing only in the choice of a 2D kernel function $\Phi(\theta, \tau)$.

His 1989 survey paper in Proceedings of the IEEE, Time-Frequency Distributions — A Review, became one of the most cited papers in the time-frequency analysis field (over 5000 citations).

Principle

Intuition: Imagine the WVD as a high-resolution photo with lots of noise (cross-terms). Cohen's Class applies different blur filters to this photo — the stronger the filter, the less noise, but the less detail. Different kernel functions = different blur filters.

Cohen's Class Unified Formula

$$C_x(t,\omega) = \frac{1}{4\pi^2}\int\!\!\int\!\!\int e^{-j\theta t - j\tau\omega + j\theta u}\, \Phi(\theta,\tau)\, x\!\left(u+\frac{\tau}{2}\right) x^*\!\left(u-\frac{\tau}{2}\right)\, du\, d\tau\, d\theta$$

$\Phi(\theta,\tau)$: kernel function, completely determines the distribution's properties

Equivalently, this can be written as a 2D convolution of WVD with the kernel function:

$$C_x(t,\omega) = \iint \phi(t-t', \omega-\omega')\, W_x(t',\omega')\, dt'\, d\omega'$$

where $\phi(t,\omega)$ is the 2D Fourier transform of $\Phi(\theta,\tau)$

Implication: All Cohen's Class distributions are some form of 2D smoothing of the WVD. The kernel function determines in which directions and how much smoothing is applied on the time-frequency plane.

Show full derivation: Relationship between kernel properties and marginal conditions

Theorem: A Cohen's Class distribution $C_x$ satisfies the marginal properties (i.e., $\int C_x\, d\omega = |x(t)|^2$ and $\int C_x\, dt = |X(\omega)|^2$) if and only if:

$$\Phi(\theta, 0) = 1 \;\;\forall\theta \quad \text{and} \quad \Phi(0, \tau) = 1 \;\;\forall\tau$$

Proof (time marginal):

$$\int C_x(t,\omega)\, \frac{d\omega}{2\pi} = \frac{1}{2\pi}\int\!\!\int\!\!\int\!\!\int e^{-j\theta t - j\tau\omega + j\theta u}\, \Phi\, K_x(u,\tau)\, du\, d\tau\, d\theta\, d\omega$$

Integrating over $\omega$ first: $\int e^{-j\tau\omega}\, d\omega/(2\pi) = \delta(\tau)$, setting $\tau = 0$:

$$= \frac{1}{2\pi}\int\!\!\int e^{-j\theta t + j\theta u}\, \Phi(\theta, 0)\, |x(u)|^2\, du\, d\theta$$

If $\Phi(\theta, 0) = 1$: $= \int |x(u)|^2\, \delta(t-u)\, du = |x(t)|^2 \quad \blacksquare$

Note: Most practical kernel functions do not simultaneously satisfy both marginal conditions. Sacrificing marginal properties is the cost of suppressing cross-terms.

Comparison of Common Kernel Functions

Kernel $\Phi(\theta,\tau)$Distribution NameAuto-term PreservationCross-term SuppressionMarginal Properties
$\Phi = 1$WVDPerfectNonePerfect
$\Phi = e^{-\theta^2\tau^2/\sigma}$Choi-Williams (CWD)GoodSuppresses cross-terms far from originSatisfied
$\Phi = e^{-|\theta\tau|^\alpha}$Zhao-Atlas-Marks (ZAM)GoodSuppresses off-axis cross-termsSatisfied
$\Phi = h(\tau)$ (depends only on $\tau$)Pseudo-WVDModerateSmoothing in $\tau$ directionFrequency marginal ✓ Time marginal ✗
$|H(\omega)|^2$ (Spectrogram)SpectrogramMost blurryComplete suppressionNot satisfied

The Spectrogram is also a member of Cohen's Class! It can be shown that $|\text{STFT}|^2$ is equivalent to smoothing the signal's WVD with the WVD of the window function as the kernel:

$S_x(t,\omega) = \iint W_h(t'-t, \omega'-\omega)\, W_x(t',\omega')\, dt'\, d\omega'$

where $W_h$ is the WVD of the window function $h$. This is why the Spectrogram has no cross-terms — it uses the "heaviest" smoothing.

How to Use

  1. Analyze your signal characteristics: Number of components? Time-frequency spacing? SNR?
  2. Choose a kernel function:
    • Single component $\to$ WVD ($\Phi = 1$), no cross-term issue
    • Few components, well-separated $\to$ Choi-Williams ($\sigma = 1$~$10$), usually the best starting point for compromise
    • Many components or closely spaced $\to$ Spectrogram (complete cross-term suppression, accepting resolution loss)
  3. Tune parameters: For Choi-Williams, larger $\sigma$ $\to$ less smoothing $\to$ closer to WVD; smaller $\sigma$ $\to$ more smoothing $\to$ closer to Spectrogram
  4. Visualize and verify: Check for residual cross-terms (oscillations appearing where no component should exist)

Practical advice: Start with the Spectrogram to quickly observe the signal's general time-frequency structure and confirm the number and location of components. Then use Choi-Williams to improve resolution, starting from $\sigma = 1$ and gradually increasing until cross-terms begin to appear.

Applications

  1. Engine acceleration analysis: The Choi-Williams distribution can clearly show how gear meshing frequencies rise linearly with RPM, while suppressing cross-terms between different orders. Typical parameter $\sigma = 5$, analyzing a 0-6000 RPM acceleration process
  2. Speech transition analysis: The transition from consonant to vowel (e.g., /ba/ $\to$ /a/) involves rapid formant migration. CWD more precisely localizes the time and frequency trajectories of the transition than the Spectrogram
  3. Underwater acoustics: Multipath propagation in underwater channels causes overlapping of multiple time-frequency components. Cohen's Class distributions help separate the arrival times and Doppler shifts of different paths

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • There is no "best" kernel function: Different signals suit different kernels. Automatic kernel selection remains an open research problem
  • Marginal properties are usually sacrificed: Most practical kernel functions do not satisfy perfect marginal conditions, leading to errors in time or frequency projections
  • Computational cost: Typical Cohen's Class computation is $O(N^2)$ or greater (triple integral), much slower than STFT
  • Can take negative values: Like WVD, Cohen's Class distributions can generally take negative values (unless the kernel is designed to be positive definite, as with the Spectrogram)

When Not to Use? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Signal is multiple pure sinusoids + noiseQuadratic distributions are not the best toolMUSIC / ESPRIT (parametric methods are more direct)
Need real-time processing$O(N^2)$ too slowSTFT ($O(N\log N)$), trading resolution for speed
Highly nonlinear signalQuadratic distribution assumptions (stationarity approximation) do not holdEMD / HHT $\to$ Section 5.6
Need multi-scale analysisCohen's Class has fixed resolutionCWT $\to$ Section 5.4

References: [1] Cohen, L., Generalized Phase-Space Distribution Functions, J. Math. Phys., 7(5):781-786, 1966. [2] Cohen, L., Time-Frequency Distributions — A Review, Proc. IEEE, 77(7):941-981, 1989. [3] Choi, H. & Williams, W., Improved Time-Frequency Representation of Multicomponent Signals, IEEE Trans. ASSP, 37(6):862-871, 1989. [4] Hlawatsch, F. & Boudreaux-Bartels, G.F., Linear and Quadratic Time-Frequency Signal Representations, IEEE SP Magazine, 1992.

← 5.2 Wigner-Ville5.4 Continuous Wavelet Transform (CWT) →

5.4 Continuous Wavelet Transform (CWT)

Multi-resolution time-frequency analysis — automatically adapting: frequency detail at low frequencies, time detail at high frequencies

Why does this matter? Because STFT has a fixed window length, but you often need to simultaneously resolve low frequencies and localize high-frequency transients. The multi-resolution property of wavelets automatically solves this contradiction — this is why it is extensively used in seismology, finance, and neuroscience.

Previously... Cohen's Class in Section 5.3 still uses a fixed analysis window. CWT uses scalable wavelets — automatically using long windows for low frequencies and short windows for high frequencies — breaking free from the fixed-window limitation.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand how CWT achieves multi-resolution analysis through scale dilation
  • Grasp the physical meaning of the admissibility condition
  • Compare the characteristics and use cases of Morlet and Mexican Hat wavelets
  • Be able to convert between scale $a$ and frequency $f$
  • Understand the boundary effects of the Cone of Influence

One-Sentence Summary

CWT is an evolution of STFT — it uses long windows for low frequencies to see frequency clearly, and short windows for high frequencies to see time clearly, automatically adapting. Like a zoom lens on a telescope: it automatically magnifies when looking at distant objects (low frequencies) and zooms out when looking at nearby objects (high frequencies).

Pain Point: STFT's Fixed-Window Dilemma

STFT's window length is fixed. But many real signals require different resolutions at different frequencies:

  • Seismic waves: Low-frequency components ($< 1$ Hz surface waves) need very long windows ($> 5$ seconds) to resolve frequency clearly, while simultaneously high-frequency components ($> 10$ Hz body waves) need short windows ($< 100$ ms) to precisely localize arrival times. STFT cannot achieve both
  • Music: The low notes C2 (65 Hz) and C#2 (69 Hz) differ by only 4 Hz, requiring a long window to distinguish; but percussive sounds in the high register need millisecond-level time localization
  • Biomedical signals: The QRS complex of an ECG lasts 60-100 ms (high frequency), but the T-wave lasts 200-400 ms (low frequency), with very different analysis requirements

STFT's fundamental limitation: Fixed window = fixed time-frequency rectangular tiles. All frequencies use tiles of the same size, unable to simultaneously satisfy the different requirements of low and high frequencies. CWT's solution: let the tile dimensions automatically adjust with frequency.

Origin

Jean Morlet (1982), a French geophysicist, first proposed the concept of wavelet analysis while analyzing seismic waves. He found that Fourier analysis performed poorly on seismic waves (seismic signals are brief and non-stationary), so he conceived the idea of using "wavelets" of different widths to match components at different frequencies.

Alex Grossmann & Jean Morlet (1984) jointly published the rigorous mathematical framework in SIAM, defining the continuous wavelet transform and the admissibility condition. Grossmann was a theoretical physicist who provided a solid mathematical foundation for Morlet's intuition.

Ingrid Daubechies (1988) further established the rigorous framework for wavelet theory, constructing orthogonal wavelet bases with compact support, opening the era of discrete wavelets ($\to$ Section 5.5).

Principle

Intuition: Choose a "mother wavelet" $\psi(t)$ (a brief oscillatory waveform). To analyze low frequencies $\to$ stretch the mother wavelet (increase scale $a$) $\to$ better low-frequency matching, automatically longer window. To analyze high frequencies $\to$ compress the mother wavelet (decrease scale $a$) $\to$ better high-frequency matching, automatically shorter window.

Continuous Wavelet Transform (CWT)

$$W_x(a, b) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{|a|}}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} x(t)\, \psi^*\!\left(\frac{t-b}{a}\right)\, dt$$

$a$: Scale, controls the wavelet width ($a$ large $\to$ stretched $\to$ low frequency)

$b$: Translation, controls the wavelet's time position

$\psi$: Mother Wavelet

$1/\sqrt{|a|}$: Energy normalization factor

Admissibility Condition

The mother wavelet must satisfy:

$$C_\psi = \int_0^{\infty} \frac{|\hat{\Psi}(\omega)|^2}{\omega}\, d\omega < \infty$$

This condition requires $\hat{\Psi}(0) = 0$, meaning the mother wavelet must have zero mean: $\int \psi(t)\, dt = 0$.

Physical intuition: the wavelet must be oscillatory (alternating positive and negative), with no DC component. This guarantees the invertibility of the CWT.

Show full derivation: CWT inverse transform formula

The inverse CWT (reconstruction formula):

$$x(t) = \frac{1}{C_\psi}\int_0^{\infty}\int_{-\infty}^{\infty} W_x(a,b)\, \frac{1}{\sqrt{a}}\,\psi\!\left(\frac{t-b}{a}\right)\, db\, \frac{da}{a^2}$$

The key step in the proof uses Parseval's theorem and the admissibility condition, carried out in the frequency domain:

$$\hat{W}_x(a,\omega) = \sqrt{a}\, \hat{X}(\omega)\, \hat{\Psi}^*(a\omega)$$

Substituting into the inverse transform and integrating over $a$:

$$\int_0^{\infty} |\hat{\Psi}(a\omega)|^2\, \frac{da}{a} = C_\psi \quad \text{(independent of $\omega$)}$$

Therefore $x(t)$ is perfectly reconstructed. $\blacksquare$

Multi-Resolution Property: CWT vs STFT Time-Frequency Tiling

STFTCWT
Time-frequency tile shapeFixed-size rectanglesVaries with scale: wide and tall at low frequencies, narrow and short at high frequencies
Low-frequency behavior$\Delta f$ fixed (may not be small enough)$\Delta f$ small (long window $\to$ high frequency resolution)
High-frequency behavior$\Delta t$ fixed (may not be small enough)$\Delta t$ small (short window $\to$ high time resolution)
Area $\Delta t \cdot \Delta f$Fixed (= determined by window function)Fixed (= determined by mother wavelet)
Basis functionsTranslation + modulation (windowed $e^{j\omega t}$)Translation + dilation (scaled $\psi$)

Key understanding: CWT does not "violate" the Heisenberg uncertainty principle — each tile's area $\Delta t \cdot \Delta f$ is still bounded. The difference is that the tile shape automatically adjusts with frequency, giving each frequency band the most appropriate time/frequency resolution ratio.

Common Mother Wavelets

Morlet Wavelet (most commonly used for time-frequency analysis)

$$\psi(t) = \pi^{-1/4}\, e^{j\omega_0 t}\, e^{-t^2/2}$$

$\omega_0 \approx 5$~$6$ (center frequency, commonly $\omega_0 = 6$)

Complex exponential with a Gaussian envelope $\to$ approximately Gaussian in both time and frequency domains $\to$ minimum time-frequency area (near the Heisenberg limit). The top choice for time-frequency analysis.

Mexican Hat (DOG-2, commonly used for singularity detection)

$$\psi(t) = \frac{2}{\sqrt{3}\,\pi^{1/4}}\,(1-t^2)\,e^{-t^2/2}$$

Negative second derivative of a Gaussian. A real-valued wavelet $\to$ no complex phase information. Shaped like a Mexican hat. Suitable for detecting peaks and singularities in signals.

Scale-to-Frequency Correspondence

$$f \approx \frac{f_\psi}{a \cdot \Delta t}$$

$f_\psi$: center frequency of the mother wavelet (Morlet with $\omega_0 = 6$: $f_\psi \approx 0.955$ Hz); $\Delta t$: sampling interval

Note: The correspondence between scale and frequency is not exact and depends on the spectral shape of the mother wavelet. Different mother wavelets have different $f_\psi$ values. Do not equate scale with frequency.

How to Use: Step-by-Step

  1. Step 1: Choose the mother wavelet
    • Time-frequency analysis $\to$ Morlet (complex-valued, has phase, best time-frequency resolution)
    • Singularity / edge detection $\to$ Mexican Hat (real-valued, sensitive to peaks)
    • Need consistency with DWT $\to$ Daubechies (compact support, for validating DWT results)
  2. Step 2: Determine the scale range $[a_{\min}, a_{\max}]$
    • Corresponding frequency range $[f_{\min}, f_{\max}]$: $a_{\min} = f_\psi / (f_{\max} \cdot \Delta t)$, $a_{\max} = f_\psi / (f_{\min} \cdot \Delta t)$
    • Scales are usually sampled geometrically (logarithmic spacing): $a_k = a_{\min} \cdot 2^{k \cdot dj}$, with $dj$ typically $1/12$~$1/4$
  3. Step 3: Compute CWT coefficients
    % MATLAB example scales = 2.^(0:0.1:7); % scale range coefs = cwtft(x, 'wavelet','morl','scales',scales); % or use the built-in function [wt, f] = cwt(x, fs, 'amor'); % 'amor' = analytic Morlet
  4. Step 4: Plot the Scalogram

    $|W_x(a,b)|^2$ — energy distribution as a function of scale (or corresponding frequency) and time. The y-axis is usually logarithmic.

Concrete Example: ECG QRS Complex Detection

ParameterValueRationale
SignalECG, $f_s = 360$ HzMIT-BIH database standard
Mother waveletMorlet ($\omega_0 = 6$)Need both time localization and frequency analysis
Frequency range10-40 HzMain energy band of the QRS complex
Scale range$a \approx 8.6$~$34.4$$a = f_\psi / (f \cdot \Delta t)$, $\Delta t = 1/360$
ResultScalogram peaks in the 10-40 Hz bandPrecisely correspond to the R-peak location of each heartbeat

Interactive: CWT Scalogram

Morlet CWT scalogram of a chirp signal. Notice that the low-frequency region has high frequency resolution but low time resolution (wide and thin tiles), while the high-frequency region has high time resolution but low frequency resolution (narrow and thick tiles).

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. Seismology: CWT is the standard tool for seismic wave analysis. Low-frequency surface waves (0.01-0.1 Hz) use large scales for precise frequency measurement (determining crustal thickness), while high-frequency body waves (1-20 Hz) use small scales for precise arrival time localization (locating the epicenter). Morlet CWT is widely used at the IRIS Seismological Data Center
  2. Financial time series: Morlet CWT is used to analyze multi-scale volatility of stock indices. Large scales (months to years) reveal business cycles, while small scales (days to weeks) show short-term fluctuations. It can discover how dominant periodicities shift across different time periods
  3. Neuroscience (brainwave analysis): Analyzing event-related spectral perturbations (ERSP) in EEG. CWT automatically provides appropriate time/frequency resolution across the delta (1-4 Hz), theta (4-8 Hz), alpha (8-12 Hz), beta (13-30 Hz), and gamma (30-100 Hz) bands
  4. Mechanical fault transient detection: Periodic impact pulses from bearing damage (high-frequency transients) — CWT can precisely localize the time of each impact while analyzing its frequency characteristics (determining whether the fault is on the inner race, outer race, or rolling element)

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Scale-to-frequency correspondence is imprecise: Different mother wavelets have different $f_\psi$ values, and the spectral width of the mother wavelet means each scale corresponds to a range of frequencies rather than a single frequency
  • Boundary effects — Cone of Influence (COI): At the beginning and end of the signal, large-scale wavelets "extend beyond" the signal boundary. Regions outside the COI are unreliable — typically marked as shaded areas on the scalogram
  • Greater computational cost than STFT: CWT computes over all scales and translations $\to$ $O(N \cdot N_{\text{scales}})$. Using frequency-domain convolution for acceleration $\to$ $O(N \log N \cdot N_{\text{scales}})$
  • Highly redundant: CWT produces far more coefficients than the original signal length (continuous $a$ and $b$), so it is not suitable for compression
  • Reconstruction formula requires the admissibility condition: If the mother wavelet does not strictly satisfy the admissibility condition (e.g., Morlet wavelet when $\omega_0 < 5$), reconstruction will have errors

When Not to Use CWT? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Only need octave band decomposition (compression, denoising)CWT is too redundant and too slowDWT ($O(N)$, non-redundant) $\to$ Section 5.5
Need fixed frequency resolutionCWT's frequency resolution varies with scaleSTFT (fixed $\Delta f$) $\to$ Section 5.1
Need the sharpest time-frequency focusCWT is blurred due to mother wavelet widthSST (sharpens CWT) $\to$ Section 5.7
Nonlinear signal, don't want a predefined basisWavelets are still a predefined basis functionEMD / HHT $\to$ Section 5.6

References: [1] Morlet, J. et al., Wave Propagation and Sampling Theory, Geophysics, 47(2):203-236, 1982. [2] Grossmann, A. & Morlet, J., Decomposition of Hardy Functions into Square Integrable Wavelets of Constant Shape, SIAM J. Math. Anal., 15:723-736, 1984. [3] Daubechies, I., Ten Lectures on Wavelets, SIAM, 1992. [4] Torrence, C. & Compo, G.P., A Practical Guide to Wavelet Analysis, Bull. Amer. Meteorol. Soc., 1998.

Quick Check

Q1: What is the fundamental difference between CWT and STFT?

Show answer

STFT has a fixed window width. CWT adaptively adjusts the window width with frequency: long windows at low frequencies (high frequency resolution), short windows at high frequencies (high time resolution).

Q2: Why must the mother wavelet satisfy the zero-mean condition?

Show answer

This is the admissibility condition, which guarantees that the CWT is invertible. Physical meaning: the wavelet must be oscillatory (both positive and negative), not purely positive or purely negative.

← 5.3 Cohen's Class5.5 Discrete Wavelet Transform (DWT) →

5.5 Discrete Wavelet Transform (DWT)

Mallat's filter bank architecture — peeling apart frequency bands layer by layer, like an onion

Why does this matter? Because CWT has high computational cost and high redundancy. DWT achieves the same multi-resolution analysis with O(N) computation, and is the industry standard for JPEG 2000 image compression and ECG denoising.

Previously... The CWT from Section 5.4 has high computational cost (continuous scales and translations). DWT is a discrete version implemented with filter banks at O(N) complexity, and is the industry standard for image compression and denoising.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand that DWT is equivalent to an iterated two-channel filter bank (Mallat algorithm)
  • Grasp the octave decomposition structure in the frequency domain
  • Understand the intuitive meaning and practical impact of vanishing moments
  • Learn to select wavelet families and decomposition levels, and apply them to denoising and compression
  • Recognize the shift-invariance problem of DWT and its solutions

One-Sentence Summary

DWT uses a set of recursive lowpass + highpass filters to decompose a signal layer by layer — like peeling an onion, each layer reveals a different frequency band. With each layer, the frequency range is halved and the data size is also halved, so the entire computation requires only $O(N)$ time.

Pain Point: CWT Is Too Redundant and Too Slow

CWT computes over continuous scales $a$ and continuous translations $b$, producing a large amount of redundant coefficients (far more than the original signal data). This works well for theoretical analysis, but creates three problems in engineering applications:

  • High computational cost: $O(N \cdot N_{\text{scales}} \cdot \log N)$, impractical for long signals
  • High redundancy: Not suitable for compression (the goal is to represent the signal with as few coefficients as possible)
  • No orthogonality: Continuous-scale wavelets do not form an orthogonal basis, which is unfavorable for mathematical analysis

Solution: Compute only at dyadic sampling scales $a = 2^j$ and translations $b = k \cdot 2^j$ $\to$ DWT.

Origin

Stephane Mallat (1989) made the critical contribution connecting wavelet theory to engineering practice: he proved that DWT is equivalent to an iterated two-channel filter bank, computable in $O(N)$ time. This algorithm, known as the Mallat algorithm (or Pyramid Algorithm), brought wavelets from theory to practice.

Ingrid Daubechies (1988) constructed orthogonal wavelet bases with compact support — meaning the filters have finite length and can be exactly implemented digitally. The Daubechies wavelet family she constructed (db1=Haar, db2, db3, ..., dbN) remains the most widely used wavelets today.

Daubechies later received the Fudan-Zhongzhi Science Award in 2019 and the Wolf Prize in Mathematics in 2023, and is one of the most important founders of wavelet theory.

Principle: Mallat Fast Wavelet Transform

Intuition: Each decomposition level passes the signal through two filters — lowpass (keeping the low-frequency "rough outline") and highpass (keeping the high-frequency "details"). Then the low-frequency part is downsampled (since the bandwidth is halved, the sampling rate can be halved), and the same process continues. Like peeling an onion: each layer reveals deeper structure.

Decomposition (Analysis)

$$c_{A}^{(j+1)}[k] = \sum_{n} h[n - 2k]\, c_{A}^{(j)}[n] \quad \text{(Lowpass filter + downsample by 2 $\to$ Approximation coefficients)}$$ $$c_{D}^{(j+1)}[k] = \sum_{n} g[n - 2k]\, c_{A}^{(j)}[n] \quad \text{(Highpass filter + downsample by 2 $\to$ Detail coefficients)}$$

$h[n]$: lowpass decomposition filter (corresponding to scaling function); $g[n]$: highpass decomposition filter (corresponding to wavelet function)

$g[n] = (-1)^n h[L-1-n]$ (QMF relation)

Reconstruction (Synthesis)

$$c_{A}^{(j)}[n] = \sum_{k} \tilde{h}[n - 2k]\, c_{A}^{(j+1)}[k] + \sum_{k} \tilde{g}[n - 2k]\, c_{D}^{(j+1)}[k]$$

$\tilde{h}, \tilde{g}$: reconstruction filters. The Perfect Reconstruction (PR) condition guarantees $x = \text{IDWT}(\text{DWT}(x))$.

Octave Decomposition in the Frequency Domain

Assuming sampling rate $f_s$, the effective frequency range is $[0, f_s/2]$:

Decomposition LevelDetail Coefficient BandApproximation Coefficient BandNumber of Coefficients
Level 1$[f_s/4,\; f_s/2]$$[0,\; f_s/4]$$N/2$ + $N/2$
Level 2$[f_s/8,\; f_s/4]$$[0,\; f_s/8]$$N/4$ + $N/4$
Level 3$[f_s/16,\; f_s/8]$$[0,\; f_s/16]$$N/8$ + $N/8$
Level $J$$[f_s/2^{J+1},\; f_s/2^J]$$[0,\; f_s/2^{J+1}]$$N/2^J$ + $N/2^J$

Total number of coefficients = $N$ (no redundancy). Computation = $N + N/2 + N/4 + \cdots < 2N = O(N)$.

Show full derivation: Perfect Reconstruction (PR) condition

In the $z$-domain, the analysis-synthesis output of a two-channel filter bank is:

$$\hat{X}(z) = \frac{1}{2}\left[\tilde{H}(z)H(z) + \tilde{G}(z)G(z)\right]X(z) + \frac{1}{2}\left[\tilde{H}(z)H(-z) + \tilde{G}(z)G(-z)\right]X(-z)$$

Perfect reconstruction requires:

  1. Aliasing Cancellation: $\tilde{H}(z)H(-z) + \tilde{G}(z)G(-z) = 0$
  2. No Distortion: $\tilde{H}(z)H(z) + \tilde{G}(z)G(z) = 2z^{-d}$ (perfect pass-through with delay $d$)

For orthogonal wavelets ($\tilde{H} = H$, $\tilde{G} = G$), only $H(z)$ needs to satisfy:

$$|H(e^{j\omega})|^2 + |H(e^{j(\omega+\pi)})|^2 = 2$$

This is the key equation Daubechies used to construct orthogonal wavelets. $\blacksquare$

Vanishing Moments: The Intuition

A wavelet $\psi(t)$ has $p$ vanishing moments, meaning:

$$\int t^k\, \psi(t)\, dt = 0 \quad \text{for } k = 0, 1, \ldots, p-1$$

Intuitive explanation: $p$ vanishing moments = the wavelet is "blind to" polynomials of degree $p-1$.

  • 1 vanishing moment (Haar): Blind to constants (degree-0 polynomials) $\to$ only captures what "differs from a constant"
  • 2 vanishing moments (db2): Blind to both constants and linear trends $\to$ only captures what "differs from a straight line"
  • 4 vanishing moments (db4): Blind to polynomials of degree $\leq 3$ $\to$ only captures higher-order details

Practical impact: More vanishing moments $\to$ smaller wavelet coefficients in smooth regions (approaching zero) $\to$ higher compression efficiency (more coefficients can be discarded). But the cost is: longer filters (db$p$ filter length = $2p$) $\to$ greater computational delay, more severe boundary effects.

How to Use: Step-by-Step

  1. Step 1: Choose a wavelet family
    WaveletFilter LengthVanishing MomentsPropertiesSuitable For
    Haar (db1)21Simplest, discontinuousBinary signals, step detection
    db484Compact support, asymmetricGeneral-purpose first choice
    sym8168Nearly symmetricWhen near-linear phase is needed
    coif3186 (wavelet) + 6 (scaling)Both wavelet and scaling function have vanishing momentsNumerical analysis, approximation
    CDF 9/79+7 (biorthogonal)4+4Symmetric, floating-pointJPEG 2000 image compression
  2. Step 2: Choose the number of decomposition levels $J$

    $J$ = how many octave frequency bands you need. Maximum $J_{\max} = \lfloor\log_2(N/L)\rfloor$ ($L$ = filter length).

    Rule of thumb: $J = \lfloor\log_2(N)\rfloor - 1$ or determine by frequency band requirements.

  3. Step 3: Decompose
    % MATLAB [C, L] = wavedec(x, J, 'db4'); % C = [cA_J | cD_J | cD_{J-1} | ... | cD_1] % L = coefficient lengths at each level # Python (PyWavelets) import pywt coeffs = pywt.wavedec(x, 'db4', level=J) # coeffs = [cA_J, cD_J, cD_{J-1}, ..., cD_1]
  4. Step 4: Process coefficients (depending on purpose)
    • Denoising: Apply thresholding to detail coefficients
      • Soft Thresholding: $\hat{d} = \text{sign}(d) \cdot \max(|d| - \lambda, 0)$ — smooth, low bias
      • Hard Thresholding: $\hat{d} = d \cdot \mathbf{1}(|d| > \lambda)$ — preserves large coefficients, but discontinuous
      • Universal Threshold: $\lambda = \sigma \sqrt{2\ln N}$, where $\sigma$ is estimated from Level 1 detail coefficients via MAD: $\hat{\sigma} = \text{MAD}(cD_1) / 0.6745$
    • Compression: Keep only the $K$ largest coefficients, set the rest to zero $\to$ reconstruct. Compression ratio = $N/K$
    • Feature extraction: Compute energy at each level $E_j = \sum_k |cD_j[k]|^2$, used as classification features
  5. Step 5: Reconstruct
    % MATLAB x_rec = waverec(C_modified, L, 'db4'); # Python x_rec = pywt.waverec(coeffs_modified, 'db4')

Concrete Example: ECG Denoising

Signal: ECG, $f_s = 360$ Hz, contaminated with 60 Hz powerline interference and high-frequency EMG noise

Method: db4, 4-level decomposition

LevelFrequency Band (Hz)Corresponding ComponentProcessing
cD190-180High-frequency EMG noiseSoft threshold (nearly all removed)
cD245-9060 Hz powerline + some EMGSoft threshold
cD322.5-45Main QRS complex energyKeep
cD411.25-22.5P-wave, T-waveKeep
cA40-11.25Baseline wanderRemove or keep (depending on need)

Result: The reconstructed ECG clearly preserves QRS, P, and T-wave morphology, with high-frequency noise and baseline wander effectively removed.

Applications

  1. Image compression — JPEG 2000: Uses CDF 9/7 biorthogonal wavelets to perform 2D DWT on images, followed by quantization and entropy coding of wavelet coefficients. JPEG 2000 achieves 20-30% better compression than JPEG (which uses DCT) at the same quality. Smooth regions in images $\to$ wavelet coefficients approach zero (thanks to vanishing moments) $\to$ high compression ratio
  2. ECG/EEG denoising: As in the example above. Soft thresholding + universal threshold criterion is the standard method in medical signal processing
  3. Seismic data compression: Seismic surveys produce TB-scale data. DWT compression can reduce storage by 10-50x while preserving key geological structure features
  4. Edge detection: Edges in images = abrupt brightness changes $\to$ appear as large values in DWT high-frequency (detail) coefficients. Haar or db2 wavelets are particularly suitable for detecting step-like edges

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Not shift-invariant: Shifting the same signal by one sample can completely change the DWT coefficients. This is caused by the downsampling operation. Consequences: (a) denoising results may differ depending on signal alignment; (b) reconstructed signals may exhibit Gibbs-like ringing at edges
  • Solution — Stationary Wavelet Transform (SWT): No downsampling $\to$ shift-invariant, but higher redundancy ($J \times N$ coefficients vs. DWT's $N$)
  • Wrong wavelet choice can introduce artifacts: For example, using the discontinuous Haar wavelet on smooth signals $\to$ blocky artifacts in reconstruction
  • Only octave band decomposition: Frequency resolution decreases exponentially with level. Level 1 covers $[f_s/4, f_s/2]$, Level 5 covers $[f_s/64, f_s/32]$ — there is no "in-between" bandwidth option
  • Boundary handling: Each decomposition level requires signal extension at boundaries (symmetric, periodic, or zero-padding), and different extension methods affect coefficients near the boundaries

When Not to Use DWT? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Need a continuous time-frequency representationDWT only has discrete octave bandsCWT $\to$ Section 5.4
Need shift invarianceDownsampling destroys shift invarianceSWT (Stationary WT, no downsampling) or DTCWT (Dual-Tree CWT)
Need uniform frequency band splittingOctave decomposition is not uniformWavelet Packet (can split bands arbitrarily)
Need data-driven decompositionDWT basis is predefinedEMD $\to$ Section 5.6 or VMD

References: [1] Mallat, S., A Theory for Multiresolution Signal Decomposition: The Wavelet Representation, IEEE Trans. PAMI, 11(7):674-693, 1989. [2] Daubechies, I., Orthonormal Bases of Compactly Supported Wavelets, Comm. Pure Appl. Math., 41(7):909-996, 1988. [3] Donoho, D.L. & Johnstone, I.M., Ideal Spatial Adaptation by Wavelet Shrinkage, Biometrika, 81(3):425-455, 1994. [4] Mallat, S., A Wavelet Tour of Signal Processing, 3rd ed., Academic Press, 2008.

← 5.4 CWT5.6 EMD / HHT →

5.6 Empirical Mode Decomposition (EMD) / Hilbert-Huang Transform (HHT)

Data-driven adaptive decomposition — let the data tell you the basis

Why does this matter? Because both Fourier and wavelet analysis use predefined basis functions, but some signals (ocean waves, seismic data, biological rhythms) have shapes that resemble neither sinusoids nor any known wavelet. EMD lets the data determine its own basis — this is a unique tool for processing nonlinear non-stationary signals.

Previously... All methods so far use predefined bases (sinusoids, wavelets). But what if the signal's shape does not resemble any known basis? EMD lets the data determine the basis by itself.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand every step of the EMD Sifting Process
  • Grasp the definition and physical meaning of IMF (Intrinsic Mode Function)
  • Understand how the Hilbert-Huang Transform derives a time-frequency representation from IMFs
  • Recognize the mode mixing problem and the improvements of EEMD/CEEMDAN

One-Sentence Summary

EMD makes absolutely no assumption about basis functions — it lets the data tell you what components it should be decomposed into. No sinusoids, no wavelets — the decomposed components take whatever shape the signal has.

Pain Point: Predefined Bases Are Not Flexible Enough

Fourier analysis assumes the signal is composed of sinusoids; wavelet analysis uses a pre-selected mother wavelet as the basis. For nonlinear, non-stationary signals, these predefined basis functions may be fundamentally unsuitable:

  • Ocean waves: Real ocean waves are not sinusoidal — crests are sharp and troughs are flat (Stokes waves). Fourier analysis produces numerous spurious harmonics (2nd, 3rd harmonics...) that are not real physical components
  • Biological signals: Heart rate variability (HRV) has nonlinear, non-stationary characteristics. The widely used LF/HF ratio in Fourier spectra relies on assumptions (stationarity) that are frequently violated
  • Mechanical faults: Impact responses from bearing damage are modulated by nonlinear spring-damper systems, producing waveforms that are neither sinusoidal nor resemble any standard wavelet

Fundamental problem: Real-world signals do not "owe" us a sinusoidal decomposition. Why not let the data decide how to decompose itself?

Origin

Norden E. Huang et al. (1998) proposed EMD and HHT at NASA Goddard Space Flight Center. This paper, published in Proceedings of the Royal Society A, is one of the most cited papers in signal processing over the past 25 years (over 15,000 citations).

Inspiration: Huang is an oceanographer whose core motivation came from ocean wave analysis — ocean waves are highly nonlinear and non-stationary. The harmonics produced by Fourier analysis are mathematical artifacts that do not represent real physical processes. He wanted a method where the decomposed components directly reflect physical phenomena.

Huang once said: "EMD is like a surgeon's scalpel — you don't need it for healthy tissue, but when you need it, nothing else will do."

Principle: Sifting Process

Intuition: Like panning for gold — shake the signal on a sieve, the topmost oscillations (highest-frequency components) are sifted out first, then the next layer, and so on until only a slow trend remains.

Complete Sifting Steps

  1. Find local extrema: Identify all local maxima and local minima of the signal $x(t)$
  2. Construct the upper envelope: Apply cubic spline interpolation to all local maxima $\to$ upper envelope $e_{\max}(t)$
  3. Construct the lower envelope: Apply cubic spline interpolation to all local minima $\to$ lower envelope $e_{\min}(t)$
  4. Compute the mean envelope: $m(t) = \frac{e_{\max}(t) + e_{\min}(t)}{2}$
  5. Remove the trend: $h(t) = x(t) - m(t)$
  6. Check IMF conditions:
    • Condition 1: The difference between the number of extrema (maxima + minima) and zero crossings is $\leq 1$
    • Condition 2: The mean envelope is approximately zero at all times
  7. If satisfied: $h(t)$ is the first IMF $\to$ $\text{IMF}_1(t) = h(t)$
  8. If not satisfied: Replace $x(t)$ with $h(t)$, go back to Step 1 and continue sifting
  9. Remove the first IMF: $r_1(t) = x(t) - \text{IMF}_1(t)$ (residual)
  10. Use the residual as new input: $x(t) \leftarrow r_1(t)$, go back to Step 1 to extract $\text{IMF}_2$
  11. Repeat until the residual $r_K(t)$ is monotonic (no more oscillations), or has $\leq 1$ extremum

EMD Decomposition Result

$$x(t) = \sum_{i=1}^{K} \text{IMF}_i(t) + r_K(t)$$

$\text{IMF}_i$: the $i$-th Intrinsic Mode Function (ordered from high to low frequency); $r_K$: final residual (trend)

Intuition for IMFs: Each IMF can be thought of as an "approximately narrowband" oscillatory component of the signal. Its amplitude and frequency can both slowly vary over time (something Fourier analysis cannot achieve), but there is only one dominant frequency at each instant.

Show full derivation: Sifting stopping criteria details

When should sifting iterations stop (when to declare $h$ as an IMF)? Common criteria include:

Cauchy-type criterion (Huang 1998):

$$\text{SD} = \frac{\sum_t |h_{k-1}(t) - h_k(t)|^2}{\sum_t h_{k-1}^2(t)} < \epsilon$$

$\epsilon$ is typically 0.2~0.3. Drawback: purely based on numerical convergence, may lead to over-sifting.

Rilling three-criterion rule (2003):

  • $|m(t)/a(t)| < \theta_1$ holds for at least a fraction $(1-\alpha)$ of all time points ($a(t) = (e_{\max} - e_{\min})/2$ is the local amplitude)
  • $|m(t)/a(t)| < \theta_2$ holds for all time points
  • Typical values: $\theta_1 = 0.05$, $\theta_2 = 0.5$, $\alpha = 0.05$

This criterion is more robust, avoiding over-sifting that distorts the IMF.

Hilbert-Huang Transform (HHT)

After obtaining the IMFs, apply the Hilbert transform to each IMF to extract instantaneous frequency and amplitude:

  1. Apply the Hilbert transform to $\text{IMF}_i(t)$ $\to$ analytic signal $z_i(t) = \text{IMF}_i(t) + j\,\hat{H}[\text{IMF}_i](t)$
  2. Instantaneous amplitude: $a_i(t) = |z_i(t)|$
  3. Instantaneous phase: $\phi_i(t) = \arg[z_i(t)]$
  4. Instantaneous frequency: $\omega_i(t) = d\phi_i/dt$

Hilbert Spectrum

$$H(\omega, t) = \sum_{i=1}^{K} a_i(t)\, \delta(\omega - \omega_i(t))$$

Each IMF contributes an energy value at each instant to the position of its instantaneous frequency $\to$ time-frequency representation

Unlike STFT/CWT, the Hilbert spectrum's frequencies are not on a fixed grid but are continuously varying curves over time. This is a more natural representation for non-stationary signals.

How to Use

% MATLAB conceptual code % Step 1: EMD decomposition imf = emd(x); % returns IMF matrix (one IMF per row) % Step 2: Apply Hilbert transform to each IMF [hs, f_inst, t_inst] = hht(imf, fs); % Step 3: Plot the Hilbert spectrum hht(imf, fs); % direct plotting # Python (emd-signal package) import emd imfs = emd.sift.sift(x) IP, IF, IA = emd.spectra.frequency_transform(imfs, fs, 'hilbert') hht = emd.spectra.hilbert_huang(IF, IA, freq_range=(0, fs/2))

Concrete Example: Ocean Wave Data Analysis

Signal: 20-minute wave height time series from a certain sea area, $f_s = 2$ Hz (0.5-second sampling interval)

EMD Result: Decomposed into 8 IMFs + residual

IMFTypical PeriodPhysical Correspondence
IMF 1-21-3 secondsWind waves — short waves generated by local wind
IMF 33-5 secondsTransition zone — wind waves converting to swell
IMF 4-65-15 secondsSwell — long waves from distant storms
IMF 7-830 seconds to minutesLong-period waves / Infragravity waves
Residual $r$$> 10$ minutesTidal and mean water level variations

Advantage: EMD automatically separates waves from different physical causes without presetting frequency boundaries. Each IMF's waveform reflects the true wave shape (non-sinusoidal), and the Hilbert spectrum reveals time-varying instantaneous frequency and amplitude characteristics.

Applications

  1. Ocean engineering (wave analysis): EMD's original application domain. Analyzing typhoon waves, rogue waves, and wave-structure interactions. Recommended by the IEEE Oceanic Engineering Society for nonlinear wave analysis
  2. Structural health monitoring: Vibration responses of bridges and buildings under earthquakes or strong winds are nonlinear and non-stationary. EMD can separate different modal vibrations and track natural frequency changes as a function of damage severity
  3. Biomedical signals (HRV analysis): In HRV analysis, EMD can extract respiratory-related components (high-frequency IMFs) and blood pressure regulation-related components (low-frequency IMFs) without assuming stationarity
  4. Financial time series: Trend extraction from stock price indices. Low-frequency IMFs + residual = long-term trend; high-frequency IMFs = short-term fluctuations and noise. More adaptive than moving averages

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Mode mixing: The most serious problem. A single IMF contains components of clearly different frequencies, or a single physical component is scattered across multiple IMFs.

    Cause: Intermittent signals (a frequency component that comes and goes) cause problems in envelope interpolation during the sifting process.

    Solutions:

    • EEMD (Ensemble EMD, Wu & Huang 2009): Add white noise $\to$ EMD $\to$ average multiple results. The noise helps "break" the mixing. Typical settings: noise amplitude = 0.1~0.2 standard deviations, ensemble size = 100~500
    • CEEMDAN (Complete EEMD with Adaptive Noise, Torres et al. 2011): Add noise to each step's residual rather than the original signal $\to$ cleaner decomposition, less residual noise
  • No rigorous mathematical theory: Unlike Fourier with its completeness and Parseval's theorem, and unlike wavelets with frame theory, EMD lacks rigorous mathematical proofs of convergence, uniqueness, or stability
  • End effects: Cubic spline interpolation needs extrapolation at signal boundaries $\to$ envelopes may diverge or become unreasonable. Common solutions: mirror extension, extrema extrapolation
  • Non-unique results: Different sifting stopping criteria, different envelope interpolation methods, different boundary handling $\to$ different results. This is a problem for scientific research requiring reproducibility
  • Computational cost: Each sifting iteration requires finding extrema, interpolation, and subtraction $\to$ overall cost is not small, especially since EEMD requires hundreds of repetitions

When Not to Use EMD? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Need rigorous mathematical framework and reproducibilityEMD lacks theoretical guaranteesCWT or DWT (have complete mathematical theory)
Need stable, deterministic decompositionEMD results are parameter-sensitiveVMD (Variational Mode Decomposition) — uses optimization instead of sifting, results are unique and stable
Signal is linear and stationaryEMD's advantages are not apparent, adds unnecessary complexityFFT / Welch (simple and direct)
Need efficient real-time processingEMD (especially EEMD) is too slowSTFT or DWT

VMD (Variational Mode Decomposition, Dragomiretskiy & Zosso 2014): Reformulates mode decomposition as a constrained variational optimization problem. Advantages: unique results, not sensitive to initialization, can specify the number of modes. Disadvantage: requires presetting the number of modes $K$ (but residual criteria can be used for automatic selection). VMD is increasingly replacing EMD in many applications.

References: [1] Huang, N.E. et al., The Empirical Mode Decomposition and the Hilbert Spectrum for Nonlinear and Non-Stationary Time Series Analysis, Proc. R. Soc. Lond. A, 454:903-995, 1998. [2] Wu, Z. & Huang, N.E., Ensemble Empirical Mode Decomposition, Adv. Adaptive Data Analysis, 1(1):1-41, 2009. [3] Torres, M.E. et al., A Complete Ensemble Empirical Mode Decomposition with Adaptive Noise, IEEE ICASSP, 2011. [4] Dragomiretskiy, K. & Zosso, D., Variational Mode Decomposition, IEEE Trans. Signal Process., 62(3):531-544, 2014.

← 5.5 DWT5.7 Synchrosqueezing →

5.7 Synchrosqueezing Transform (SST)

Sharpening CWT — combining the robustness of wavelets with near-WVD resolution

Why does this matter? Because CWT time-frequency plots are too blurry, and WVD has cross-terms. SST combines the advantages of both — the stability of wavelets plus the sharpness approaching WVD — and is one of the most important advances in time-frequency analysis in the past decade.

Previously... EMD from Section 5.6 lacks mathematical theory and produces non-unique results. SST combines the stability of CWT with the sharpness of WVD, and is an important advance of the past decade.

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the core idea of SST: using instantaneous frequency estimates to "squeeze" CWT energy to its correct position
  • Recognize the historical context of Frequency Reassignment and how SST improves upon it
  • Compare the resolution and cross-term characteristics of SST vs. CWT vs. WVD
  • Understand SST's sensitivity to noise and the extension to 2nd-order SST

One-Sentence Summary

SST "squeezes" the blurry CWT time-frequency plot into sharp lines — maintaining the robustness and cross-term-free nature of wavelets, while achieving time-frequency focus approaching WVD. Like refocusing a blurry photograph.

Pain Point: CWT Is Blurry, WVD Has Ghost Images

So far, we face a trilemma:

MethodResolutionCross-termsRobustness
STFT / SpectrogramHeisenberg-limited ✗None ✓High ✓
WVDPerfect ✓Severe ✗Low ✗
CWT ScalogramMulti-resolution (but still blurry)None ✓High ✓

Is there a way to have the best of both worlds — cross-term-free like CWT and sharp like WVD? SST provides a near-perfect answer.

Origin

Frequency Reassignment (Auger & Flandrin, 1995): The earliest "sharpening" idea. They observed that STFT/CWT energy gets "smeared" across the window/wavelet width, but one can use the instantaneous frequency of each coefficient to "relocate" the energy to the correct position. The problem was that the result after reassignment is not invertible.

Ingrid Daubechies, Jianfeng Lu & Hau-Tieng Wu (2011): Proposed SST in Applied and Computational Harmonic Analysis. Their key contribution was designing an invertible reassignment method — squeezing only in the frequency (scale) direction, leaving the time direction unchanged. This guarantees invertibility while achieving a sharp time-frequency representation.

Daubechies made foundational contributions to both wavelet theory (CWT/DWT, Sections 5.4-5.5) and SST — she is one of the most influential scholars in time-frequency analysis.

Principle

Intuition: Imagine the CWT scalogram as a blurry photograph — energy spreads out like watercolor across a range of scales. What SST does is: for each coefficient, ask "what is your true frequency?" (using instantaneous frequency estimation), then push the energy from its current scale to the corresponding true frequency. Like pushing each drop of watercolor back to where it belongs.

Step 1: Compute CWT

$$W_x(a, b) = \frac{1}{\sqrt{a}}\int x(t)\, \psi^*\!\left(\frac{t-b}{a}\right)\, dt$$

Step 2: Estimate instantaneous frequency for each coefficient

$$\omega(a, b) = -\text{Im}\left[\frac{\partial_b W_x(a, b)}{W_x(a, b)}\right] = -\text{Im}\left[\frac{W_x^{(\psi')}(a, b)}{W_x(a, b)}\right]$$

$W_x^{(\psi')}$: CWT computed with $\psi'(t)$ (the derivative of the mother wavelet)

Intuition: $\omega(a,b)$ is the true frequency "perceived" by the CWT coefficient at scale $a$, time $b$. If the signal's true frequency at that point is $\omega_0$, then regardless of what value $a$ takes (as long as $W_x(a,b)$ is not too small), $\omega(a,b) \approx \omega_0$.

Step 3: Synchrosqueezing

Reassign CWT energy from scale $a$ to instantaneous frequency $\omega(a,b)$:

$$T_x(\omega_l, b) = \sum_{\substack{a_k:\; |\omega(a_k, b) - \omega_l| < \Delta\omega/2}} W_x(a_k, b)\, a_k^{-3/2}\, \Delta a_k$$

For each frequency bin $\omega_l$, sum up the CWT coefficients from all scales whose instantaneous frequency points to it

Show full derivation: Why SST is invertible but Reassignment is not

Frequency Reassignment (Auger-Flandrin): Reassigns energy in both time and frequency directions simultaneously. This is a many-to-one mapping (multiple time-frequency points can map to the same point) $\to$ not invertible.

SST: Reassigns only in the frequency (scale) direction, keeping time position $b$ unchanged. Therefore, at each fixed $b$, the squeezing operation is one-dimensional.

Invertibility theorem (Daubechies et al. 2011): For a signal composed of $K$ components with separated instantaneous frequencies, the SST result $T_x(\omega, b)$ can be uniquely inverted back to the original signal:

$$x(t) = \text{Re}\left[\frac{1}{C_\psi}\int T_x(\omega, t)\, d\omega\right]$$

The prerequisite is that the instantaneous frequencies of the components do not overlap at any time (separation condition). $\blacksquare$

Show full derivation: SST effect analysis for a single-component chirp

Consider $x(t) = A(t)\, e^{j\phi(t)}$, instantaneous frequency $\omega_0(t) = \phi'(t)$.

CWT response at scale $a$: $W_x(a, b) \approx A(b)\sqrt{a}\, \hat{\Psi}^*(a\omega_0(b))\, e^{j\phi(b)}$

Instantaneous frequency estimate: $\omega(a, b) = \omega_0(b)$ (independent of $a$!)

Therefore SST squeezes all scales' energy to $\omega_0(b)$ $\to$ forming a sharp line on the frequency axis, perfectly tracking the instantaneous frequency.

CWT scalogram: Energy in a band-shaped region around $\omega_0(b)$ (because the mother wavelet has width).

SST: Concentrated into a line at $\omega_0(b)$.

The sharpening level is equivalent to WVD, but without cross-terms. $\blacksquare$

SST vs CWT vs WVD: Visual Comparison

PropertyCWT ScalogramWVDSST
Single-component appearanceBlurry bandSharp lineSharp line
Multi-component cross-termsNoneSevereNone
InvertibilityInvertibleInvertibleInvertible (under separation condition)
Can take negative valuesNo ($|W|^2 \geq 0$)YesYes (complex-valued)
Computational cost$O(N \cdot J \log N)$$O(N^2)$$O(N \cdot J \log N)$ (same order as CWT)

How to Use

  1. Choose the mother wavelet: Typically Morlet (analytic wavelet), because SST needs precise instantaneous frequency estimation and Morlet's narrowband properties are most suitable
  2. Compute CWT: Same as Section 5.4
  3. Compute the instantaneous frequency map: $\omega(a,b)$ — requires computing both $W_x$ and $W_x^{(\psi')}$
  4. Perform Synchrosqueezing: Reassign CWT coefficients to the corresponding frequency bins
  5. Visualize: Plot $|T_x(\omega, b)|^2$
% MATLAB (Signal Processing Toolbox R2016b+) [sst_result, f] = wsst(x, fs, 'amor'); % 'amor' = analytic Morlet % sst_result: frequency x time complex matrix % f: frequency vector pcolor(t, f, abs(sst_result)); shading interp; colormap(jet); # Python (ssqueezepy package) import ssqueezepy Tx, Wx, ssq_freqs, scales = ssqueezepy.ssq_cwt(x, wavelet='morlet') plt.pcolormesh(t, ssq_freqs, np.abs(Tx), shading='auto')

Applications

  1. Component separation of multi-component non-stationary signals: For example, two chirp signals crossing in the time-frequency plane — STFT shows a blurry blob, WVD shows cross-term interference, but SST reveals two sharp crossing lines. Combined with Ridge Extraction algorithms, each component can be automatically separated and reconstructed via inversion
  2. Geology (seismic wave mode separation): Different modes of seismic surface waves (fundamental mode, higher-order modes) appear as distinct $f$-$v$ relationships on dispersion curves. SST's sharp time-frequency plot makes mode separation more precise, aiding in subsurface structure inversion
  3. Heart rate variability (HRV) analysis: SST can precisely track time-varying respiratory frequency characteristics (respiratory frequency varies with activity, sleep stage, etc.), localizing the instantaneous respiratory frequency more sharply than CWT
  4. Mechanical fault diagnosis: Tracking gear meshing frequencies under variable-speed conditions. SST sharpens the blurry frequency trajectories in CWT into precise lines, aiding in RPM computation and anomaly detection

Pitfalls and Limitations

  • Sensitive to noise: The instantaneous frequency estimate $\omega(a,b) = -\text{Im}[\partial_b W / W]$ becomes unstable in regions where $|W|$ is small (low SNR). Noise causes $\omega$ estimates to jump erratically $\to$ scattered dots appear in SST results. In practice, an energy threshold must be set to ignore regions where $|W|$ is too small
  • Not suitable for rapid frequency jumps: SST assumes that instantaneous frequency varies slowly locally. For sudden frequency jumps (e.g., FSK modulation), SST's sharpening effect deteriorates at the jump points
  • 2nd-order SST (Oberlin et al. 2015): An improved version. Uses second-order instantaneous frequency estimation (accounting for the rate of change of $\omega$) $\to$ better results for chirps and other rapidly varying frequency signals. But higher computational cost and more complex implementation
  • Separation condition: When components' instantaneous frequencies coincide at certain times, SST cannot separate them (unlike WVD, which theoretically can, but with cross-terms)
  • Slightly higher computational cost than CWT: Requires additional computation of $W_x^{(\psi')}$ (one CWT pass with the derivative wavelet) and instantaneous frequency mapping. Approximately 2-3x the cost of CWT

When Not to Use SST? Alternatives

ScenarioProblemAlternative
Only need a rough time-frequency overviewSST is much more expensive than STFTSTFT Spectrogram (much faster, usually sufficient)
Very low SNR (< 0 dB)Instantaneous frequency estimation is unstableCWT Scalogram (blurry but stable) or Multitaper Spectrogram
Frequency has sudden jumps1st-order SST assumption does not hold2nd-order SST or STFT (short windows better track rapid changes)
Nonlinear signal, don't want a predefined waveletSST still depends on mother wavelet choiceEMD / HHT $\to$ Section 5.6
Need precise energy distributionSST results are complex-valued, not positive energyReassigned Spectrogram (Auger-Flandrin)

Part V: Time-Frequency Analysis Methods Overview

Positioning and use cases for all seven methods:

MethodCore PropertyBest Suited ForGreatest Limitation
5.1 STFTFixed window, fast computationFirst choice for general time-frequency analysisFixed resolution
5.2 WVDHighest resolutionPrecise single-component analysisMulti-component cross-terms
5.3 Cohen'sUnified framework, tunable kernelTheoretical analysis and comparisonHigh computational cost
5.4 CWTMulti-resolutionMulti-scale time-frequency analysisRedundant, blurry
5.5 DWT$O(N)$, non-redundantCompression, denoising, feature extractionOnly octave bands
5.6 EMDData-driven, adaptiveNonlinear non-stationary signalsLacks theory, mode mixing
5.7 SSTSharpened CWT, no cross-termsMulti-component non-stationary separationNoise-sensitive

Selection guide: Start with STFT for quick observation $\to$ if multi-resolution is needed, use CWT $\to$ if sharper results are needed, use SST $\to$ if cross-terms are a problem, avoid WVD $\to$ if the signal is highly nonlinear, try EMD $\to$ if compression/denoising is needed, use DWT.

References: [1] Daubechies, I., Lu, J. & Wu, H.-T., Synchrosqueezed Wavelet Transforms: An Empirical Mode Decomposition-like Tool, Appl. Comp. Harm. Anal., 30(2):243-261, 2011. [2] Auger, F. & Flandrin, P., Improving the Readability of Time-Frequency and Time-Scale Representations by the Reassignment Method, IEEE Trans. Signal Process., 43(5):1068-1089, 1995. [3] Oberlin, T., Meignen, S. & Perrier, V., Second-Order Synchrosqueezing Transform or Invertible Reassignment?, IEEE Trans. Signal Process., 63(5):1335-1344, 2015. [4] Thakur, G. et al., The Synchrosqueezing Algorithm for Time-Varying Spectral Analysis, Signal Processing, 93(5):1079-1094, 2013.

← 5.6 EMD / HHT6.1 Advanced FFT →

6.1 Advanced FFT Algorithms

Learning Objectives

  • Distinguish the use cases for Split-Radix, Bluestein, and Goertzel algorithms
