  • Determine when Goertzel is more efficient than FFT and when it is not
  • Use the Bluestein identity to understand how arbitrary-length DFTs can be converted to convolution

Why does this matter? Because real-world data lengths are not always powers of 2, and sometimes you only need the result at a single frequency -- computing a full FFT would be wasteful.

Previously... Part V established the theory of time-frequency analysis. Part VI returns to engineering practice -- starting with advanced FFT implementation techniques.

Pain point: What do you do when the data length is not a power of 2? When you only need to detect one specific frequency (e.g., a DTMF dial tone), computing a full FFT is wasteful.
Historical context:
  • Goertzel Algorithm -- Gerald Goertzel, 1958. Designed to compute the DFT value at a single frequency bin.
  • Bluestein Algorithm (Chirp-Z Transform) -- Leo Bluestein, 1970. Cleverly converts an arbitrary-length DFT into a convolution problem.
  • Split-Radix FFT -- Duhamel & Hollmann, 1984. Combines the advantages of Radix-2 and Radix-4, saving approximately 20% of complex multiplications.

Principles

Intuition: Radix-2 FFT is a "general-purpose tool," but more efficient alternatives exist for special cases:

  • Split-Radix: Simultaneously uses length N/2 (even part) and two N/4 (odd part) DFTs, requiring ~20% fewer multiplications than pure Radix-2.
  • Bluestein: Works for any N, using an algebraic identity to convert the DFT into convolution.
  • Goertzel: Computes only one bin using a second-order recursion (IIR), with O(N) complexity.

Formulas:

Bluestein Identity:

kn = \tfrac{1}{2}\bigl[k^2 + n^2 - (k-n)^2\bigr]

Substituting into the DFT twiddle factor $W_N^{kn}$:

X[k] = W_N^{k^2/2} \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} \bigl(x[n]\,W_N^{n^2/2}\bigr)\,W_N^{-(k-n)^2/2}

The term in parentheses is $x[n]$ multiplied by a known sequence (chirp), and the outer operation is convolution with another known sequence. Three FFTs (zero-padded to a power-of-2 length) can complete a DFT of any length N.

Goertzel Recursion:

s[n] = x[n] + 2\cos(2\pi k/N)\,s[n-1] - s[n-2], \quad s[-1]=s[-2]=0

X[k] = s[N-1] - W_N^k\,s[N-2]

Show full derivation: Bluestein Identity

DFT definition: $X[k] = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} x[n]\,W_N^{kn}$, where $W_N = e^{-j2\pi/N}$.

Using the identity $kn = \tfrac{1}{2}[k^2 + n^2 - (k-n)^2]$ (verify by expanding the right side):

W_N^{kn} = W_N^{k^2/2}\,W_N^{n^2/2}\,W_N^{-(k-n)^2/2}

Substituting into the DFT:

X[k] = W_N^{k^2/2} \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} \bigl[x[n]\,W_N^{n^2/2}\bigr]\,W_N^{-(k-n)^2/2}

Let $a[n] = x[n]\,W_N^{n^2/2}$ and $b[n] = W_N^{-n^2/2}$. The summation is the convolution of $a$ and $b$ evaluated at the $k$-th point. Zero-padding $a$ and $b$ to length $\geq 2N-1$ (rounded to a power of 2) allows the convolution to be computed with three FFTs.

Show full derivation: Goertzel Algorithm

DFT bin k: $X[k] = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} x[n]\,W_N^{kn}$.

Observe that $W_N^{-kN} = 1$, so:

X[k] = W_N^{-kN}\sum_{n=0}^{N-1} x[n]\,W_N^{kn} = \sum_{n=0}^{N-1} x[n]\,W_N^{-k(N-n)}

This is equivalent to passing $x[n]$ through a filter with transfer function $H(z)=\frac{1}{1-W_N^{-k}z^{-1}}$ and taking the output at $n=N$.

However, this is a first-order complex recursion. To avoid complex multiplications, multiply both numerator and denominator by $(1-W_N^{k}z^{-1})$:

H(z) = \frac{1 - W_N^{k}z^{-1}}{1 - 2\cos(2\pi k/N)z^{-1} + z^{-2}}

The denominator is a real-coefficient second-order recursion (requiring only real multiplications). After running N steps, the numerator (one complex multiplication) yields $X[k]$.

Total: N real multiplications + 1 complex multiplication = O(N) per bin.

How to Use

Algorithm Selection Decision Tree:
  1. N is a power of 2 -- Use Radix-2 or Split-Radix FFT directly.
  2. N is not a power of 2 -- Zero-pad to the next power of 2 (simplest), or use Bluestein (when exact N is strictly required).
  3. Only K bins needed (K << N) -- Use K Goertzel computations. More efficient than a full FFT when $K < \log_2 N$.

Concrete Example: DTMF Dial Tone Detection

DTMF (Dual-Tone Multi-Frequency) only requires detecting 8 frequencies: 697, 770, 852, 941, 1209, 1336, 1477, 1633 Hz.

// Sampling rate 8000Hz, 205 samples per segment (~25.6ms)
// Full FFT (N=256): 256 x log2(256) = 256 x 8 = 2048 complex multiplications
// 8 Goertzel runs:   8 x 205 = 1640 real multiplications + 8 complex multiplications
// Goertzel saves ~90% of computation (accounting for complex vs. real difference)

for each freq in [697, 770, 852, 941, 1209, 1336, 1477, 1633]:
    k = round(freq * N / fs)
    coeff = 2 * cos(2 * pi * k / N)
    s1, s2 = 0, 0
    for sample in segment:
        s0 = sample + coeff * s1 - s2
        s2 = s1
        s1 = s0
    power = s1*s1 + s2*s2 - coeff*s1*s2
    if power > threshold:
        tone_detected(freq)

Applications

  1. DTMF telephone dial tone detection: Telephone switches use Goertzel to detect 8 frequencies. 205 samples per segment @8kHz, processing ~39 segments per second.
  2. Power quality measurement: Smart meters only need to measure the 50Hz fundamental and a few harmonics (100, 150, 200... Hz). Goertzel saves >10x power compared to a full FFT, extending battery life.
  3. Non-standard data lengths: Some communication protocol frame lengths are not powers of 2 (e.g., LTE SC-FDMA uses multiples of 12). Bluestein/CZT can handle them directly.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Zero-padding to a power of 2 is usually the simplest and most effective approach. Unless there are strict N constraints (memory, latency), Bluestein is unnecessary.
  • Goertzel's efficiency advantage disappears when $K > \log_2 N$; in that case, a full FFT is faster.
  • Split-Radix implementation is more complex than Radix-2. Modern CPUs have SIMD-optimized FFT libraries (FFTW, MKL) that are hard to beat with manual implementations.
When Not to Use:
  • If you can freely choose the data length, simply use a power of 2 -- no need for Bluestein.
  • If you need the full spectrum (all N bins), Goertzel offers no advantage.
  • Alternative: Modern FFT libraries (FFTW) have built-in Mixed-Radix algorithms that efficiently handle any length (as long as N has small prime factors). Bluestein is only truly needed when N is prime.

✅ Quick Check

Q1: DTMF detection only requires 8 frequencies. Comparing Goertzel with a full FFT (N=256), which is more efficient? By how much?

Show answer

Goertzel: 8xN=2048 operations. FFT: (N/2)log2N=1024 operations. In this case, FFT is actually faster! Goertzel only saves when K < log2N.

Q2: Data length N=300 (not a power of 2) -- what is the simplest approach?

Show answer

Zero-pad to 512 (the next power of 2) and use standard Radix-2 FFT. Much simpler than the Bluestein algorithm.

← 5.7 SST6.2 Numerical Precision →

6.2 Numerical Precision

Learning Objectives

  • Calculate the relationship between floating-point FFT SQNR and N and precision
  • Explain fixed-point FFT overflow issues and two scaling strategies
  • Choose floating-point/fixed-point precision based on SQNR requirements

Why does this matter? Because when implementing FFT on embedded systems and FPGAs, numerical precision directly determines system performance -- if you don't understand fixed-point overflow and rounding errors, your spectral results will be garbage.

Previously... Section 6.1 introduced different FFT algorithms. But on real hardware (FPGAs, embedded systems), floating-point numbers are not true real numbers -- numerical precision is an engineering problem you must face.

Pain point: Running a 16-bit fixed-point FFT on an FPGA yields only 60dB SNR (Signal-to-Noise Ratio), but the specification requires 80dB -- why such a large gap?
Historical context: Peter Welch systematically analyzed the finite wordlength effects of FFT in 1969, establishing the theoretical framework for floating-point and fixed-point FFT errors. Later, Oppenheim & Weinstein and others further refined scaling strategies for fixed-point FFT.

Principles

Intuition: FFT is a cascade of butterfly operations. Each butterfly involves multiplication and addition, and each step introduces rounding error. $\log_2 N$ butterfly stages = $\log_2 N$ rounds of error accumulation.

Floating-point FFT error bound:

\|X_{\text{computed}} - X_{\text{exact}}\|_{\text{rms}} \;\leq\; C\,\varepsilon_m\,\sqrt{\log_2 N}\;\|x\|_{\text{rms}}

where $\varepsilon_m$ is the machine epsilon: $\approx 1.19 \times 10^{-7}$ for float32, $\approx 2.22 \times 10^{-16}$ for float64.

The Fixed-Point FFT Problem -- Overflow:

The butterfly operation $a + b$ may exceed the representable range of fixed-point numbers. Two resolution strategies:

  • Right-shift 1 bit per stage (Convergent Scaling): After each butterfly stage, right-shift all values by 1 bit (divide by 2). Guarantees no overflow, but loses 1 bit of precision per stage. After $\log_2 N$ stages, $\log_2 N$ bits are lost.
  • Block Floating Point (BFP): Check the maximum value at each stage and only scale when necessary. Average loss is less than per-stage right-shifting.

Concrete numbers:

ConfigurationNSQNR (dB)Notes
Float641024≈ 281Sufficient for virtually any application
Float321024≈ 131Adequate for most consumer applications
Fixed 32-bit1024≈ 132Mainstream FPGA choice
Fixed 16-bit (per-stage scaling)1024≈ 7210 butterfly stages lose 10 bits, leaving 6 effective bits
Fixed 16-bit (BFP)1024≈ 84~12dB better than fixed scaling
Show full derivation: SQNR for per-stage scaling in fixed-point FFT

Quantization noise power for 16-bit fixed-point: $\sigma_q^2 = 2^{-2B}/12$, with B=16 giving $\sigma_q^2 \approx 3.2 \times 10^{-11}$.

Right-shifting 1 bit per stage is equivalent to introducing one quantization event. $\log_2 N = 10$ stages, each introducing independent quantization noise.

Total quantization noise power $\approx 10 \times \sigma_q^2$ (but note that per-stage scaling progressively reduces effective bits).

More precisely: after right-shifting at each stage, effective bits decrease from B to B-1, ultimately leaving only B - log2N = 16 - 10 = 6 effective bits.

SQNR ≈ 6.02 × 6 + 1.76 + 10log10(N/2) ≈ 36.1 + 1.76 + 27.1 ≈ 65-72 dB (depending on signal statistics).

How to Use

Precision Selection Guide:
  1. Float64 (double): Scientific computing, offline analysis with no precision concerns. SQNR > 280dB.
  2. Float32 (single): GPU acceleration, most consumer electronics, sufficient for N ≤ 220. SQNR ≈ 130dB.
  3. Fixed 32-bit: FPGA radar/communications, equivalent to float32 in most cases.
  4. Fixed 16-bit: Low-power embedded. Must use BFP or per-stage scaling. SQNR 70-85dB.

Concrete Example: FPGA Radar FFT Design

// Spec: 1024-point FFT, 14-bit ADC input, SQNR > 75dB required
// Solution: 18-bit internal word width + Block Floating Point

// Check maximum value after each butterfly stage:
if (max_value > 0.5 * FULL_SCALE):
    right_shift_all_by_1()
    block_exponent += 1

// 10 butterfly stages, on average only 5-6 scaling operations needed (signal-dependent)
// Effective bits: 18 - 6 = 12 bit -> SQNR ~ 6.02x12 + 1.76 ~ 74 dB
// Conclusion: 18-bit BFP barely meets spec; recommend 20-bit for margin

Applications

  1. FPGA radar processor: Xilinx/AMD FPGA DSP48 slices natively support 18×25 bit multiplication. 1024-point FFT with 18-bit BFP, throughput 500MHz.
  2. Embedded audio DSP: Audio DSPs in mobile SoCs (e.g., Qualcomm Hexagon) typically use 16-bit fixed-point processing. 256-point FFT with per-stage scaling, SQNR ≈ 80dB -- sufficient for audio (human ear dynamic range ~96dB).
  3. 5G baseband processor: 4096-point FFT @30.72MHz sampling rate. Uses 16-bit fixed-point + BFP, processing tens of thousands of OFDM symbols per second.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Float32 for large N: When N > 220, accumulated errors can become significant. Astronomical observations, large seismic arrays, etc. should use float64.
  • Forgetting scaling in fixed-point: The most common bug. After overflow, values wraparound, producing completely wrong results that are hard to debug.
  • Twiddle factor precision: In fixed-point FFT, the precision of sin/cos lookup tables also affects results. Typically need 2-4 more bits than the data.
  • Parseval's theorem check: Time-domain energy should equal frequency-domain energy. If the discrepancy exceeds expected precision, there is a precision problem.
When Not to Use (Fixed-Point FFT):
  • If your platform has a floating-point unit (FPU), use floating-point directly. Fixed-point is only worthwhile when there is no FPU or power is extremely constrained.
  • If precision requirement > 90dB, 16-bit fixed-point is insufficient -- use 24-bit or 32-bit.
  • Alternative: Floating-point IP cores are available for FPGAs (Xilinx FFT IP supports float32), at the cost of more resources.

✅ Quick Check

Q1: What is the approximate SQNR of a 1024-point float32 FFT in dB?

Show answer

float32 epsilon ~ 1.2x10^-7, SQNR ~ 20log10(1/(epsilon*sqrt(log2(1024)))) ~ 20log10(1/(1.2x10^-7*sqrt(10))) ~ 131 dB.

Q2: A 16-bit fixed-point FFT right-shifts 1 bit per stage. After 10 stages (1024-point), how many effective bits remain?

Show answer

16 - 10 = 6 effective bits, SQNR ~ 6x6 = 36 dB. This is usually insufficient -- block floating point or 32-bit fixed-point is needed.

← 6.1 Advanced FFT6.3 OLA/OLS →

6.3 Overlap-Add / Overlap-Save (OLA/OLS)

Learning Objectives

  • Derive the correctness conditions for OLA and OLS (FFT length >= L+M-1)
  • Calculate the latency and computation cost of segmented convolution
  • Choose between OLA and OLS based on implementation requirements

Why does this matter? Because real-time audio processing, streaming filtering, and voice call noise reduction all require "process as you receive" -- OLA/OLS is the standard approach for applying FFT to infinitely long streams.

Previously... Section 6.2 addressed precision issues. But there is another implementation problem: real signals are streams (infinitely long) -- you cannot FFT everything at once. How do you process in segments yet get results identical to processing all at once?

Pain point: Real-time audio effects (reverb, EQ) need to convolve the input with an impulse response, but audio is an infinitely long stream -- you cannot wait to receive it all before computing the FFT.
Historical context: Thomas Stockham proposed the concept of Fast Convolution in 1966 -- using FFT to accelerate convolution. OLA (Overlap-Add) and OLS (Overlap-Save) are two strategies for segmented fast convolution of long sequences; both have the same efficiency but different implementations.

Principles

Intuition: The length of a linear convolution = sum of both sequence lengths - 1. After splitting a long sequence into segments, each segment's convolution result is longer than the original segment -- the tail "spills over" into the next segment's range. OLA and OLS handle this spillover problem in different ways.

Overlap-Add (OLA)

  1. Split input $x[n]$ into non-overlapping segments, each of length L.
  2. Zero-pad each segment to $N = L + M - 1$ (M = filter length).
  3. FFT convolution: $Y_i = \text{IFFT}\{\text{FFT}\{x_i, N\} \cdot H[k]\}$.
  4. Each segment's result has length N > L; the tail M-1 points are overlap-added with the head of the next segment.

Overlap-Save (OLS)

  1. Split input $x[n]$ into overlapping segments, each of length N, with adjacent segments overlapping by M-1 points.
  2. Directly compute length-N FFT convolution (no zero-padding needed).
  3. The first M-1 points of each segment's result are the erroneous circular convolution portion -- discard them.
  4. Keep the remaining L = N - M + 1 points, which are identical to the linear convolution result.
Show full derivation: Why discarding the first M-1 points in OLS gives correct results

Length-N FFT convolution computes circular convolution, but what we need is linear convolution.

The difference between circular and linear convolution only appears in the "tail wrapping around to the head" portion. Specifically:

Linear convolution $y_{\text{lin}}[n] = \sum_{m=0}^{M-1} h[m]\,x_i[n-m]$: when $n < M-1$, $x_i[n-m]$ may access samples before the current segment.

In circular convolution, $x_i[n-m]$ "wraps around" to the end of the segment (modulo N), producing incorrect results.

However, if we let each segment overlap by M-1 points, then the first M-1 samples of the current segment are the last M-1 samples of the previous segment. For $n \geq M-1$, the circular convolution does not need to access data outside the segment, so it is identical to the linear convolution.

Therefore: discard the first M-1 points (the erroneous circular convolution portion), keep the remaining L = N - M + 1 points (the correct portion).

How to Use

Design Steps:
  1. Determine filter length M (the length of the impulse response).
  2. Choose segment length L: Latency = L/fs seconds. Larger L means higher latency but better efficiency (FFT overhead is amortized).
  3. FFT length N = L + M - 1, rounded up to the next power of 2.
  4. Pre-compute $H[k] = \text{FFT}\{h[n], N\}$ (only done once).
  5. Per-segment processing:
    • OLA: Take L points -> zero-pad to N -> FFT -> multiply by H[k] -> IFFT -> overlap-add.
    • OLS: Take N points (including M-1 overlap) -> FFT -> multiply by H[k] -> IFFT -> discard first M-1 points.

Concrete Example: Audio Reverb Effect

// Impulse response: concert hall IR, M = 4096 points (@ 48kHz = 85.3ms reverb tail)
// Segment length: L = 4096
// FFT length: N = L + M - 1 = 8191 -> round to 8192 (2^13)
// Latency = L / fs = 4096 / 48000 = 85.3 ms

// Computation comparison:
// Direct convolution: L x M = 4096 x 4096 = 16,777,216 multiplications/segment
// FFT convolution: 3 x (N/2)log2N = 3 x 4096 x 13 = 159,744 multiplications/segment
//                  (2 FFTs + 1 IFFT, each (N/2)log2N complex multiplications)
// Speedup: 16.8M / 160K ~ 105x

// To reduce latency:
// L = 512 -> latency = 10.7ms, N = 4608 -> 8192
// Per-segment computation is the same (same N), but more segments per second
// Segments per second = 48000 / 512 = 93.75 segments
// FFTs per second = 93.75 x 3 = 281 8192-point FFTs

Applications

  1. Audio effects: Reverb (IR up to several seconds = hundreds of thousands of points), EQ (FIR with hundreds to thousands of taps). DAW software like Pro Tools uses OLA for real-time multi-track audio processing.
  2. Acoustic Echo Cancellation (AEC): Room impulse response ~100ms = 1600 points @16kHz. Process every 10ms segment using OLS fast convolution.
  3. Radar Pulse Compression: Long pulse correlated with matched filter, implemented in real-time using OLA/OLS.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • First segment of OLS: Requires prepending M-1 zeros to the input (since there is no "previous segment tail" available).
  • Insufficient FFT length: If N < L + M - 1, the circular convolution produces errors -- results will have aliasing. This is the most common bug.
  • Latency-efficiency tradeoff: Small L means low latency but poor efficiency (large FFT overhead). Live music performance requires <10ms latency, limiting L to 256-512.
  • Numerical precision: Very long IRs (several seconds) may require large N, where float32 precision may be insufficient. Partitioned IR techniques can help (small N for early reflections with low latency, large N for late reflections with high efficiency).
OLA vs OLS Comparison:
  • OLS has a simpler implementation (no accumulation buffer needed), with more regular memory access patterns.
  • OLA is more intuitive conceptually, and input segments don't need to overlap (saving a little memory).
  • Both have identical efficiency (same number and size of FFTs).

When OLA/OLS is unnecessary:

  • Short filter (M < 64) -- direct time-domain convolution may be faster (no FFT overhead).
  • Offline processing with ample memory -- do a single full-length FFT convolution.
  • Alternative: Partitioned Convolution -- split a long IR into multiple segments with different FFT sizes, achieving both low latency and high efficiency.

Interactive: Overlap-Add Segmented Convolution

Convolution of a long signal (4096 points) with a short filter (64 points). Compare direct convolution and OLA results -- they are identical.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot

✅ Quick Check

Q1: An audio reverb impulse response is 4096 points. Using OLA, what is the minimum FFT length?

Show answer

N >= L + M - 1. With segment length L=4096, N >= 4096+4096-1=8191, rounded to 8192 (power of 2).

Q2: Which is simpler to implement, OLA or OLS? Why?

Show answer

OLS is simpler -- no extra overlap-add step needed; just discard the first M-1 points of each segment's IFFT result.

← 6.2 Numerical Precision6.4 Filter Design →

6.4 Filter Design

Learning Objectives

  • Design an FIR low-pass filter using the window method (from specifications to h[n])
  • Use the Kaiser formula to calculate the required filter order and beta parameter
  • Compare FIR vs IIR tradeoffs and choose based on requirements

Why does this matter? Because the first step in almost every DSP system is filtering -- denoising, anti-aliasing, channel selection. Without filter design skills, you cannot do DSP.

Previously... Section 6.3 solved the long-sequence convolution problem. Now let's design that convolution kernel -- the filter itself.

Pain point: "I need a low-pass filter with cutoff frequency 100Hz, transition bandwidth within 20Hz, and stopband attenuation of 60dB -- what does h[n] look like? How many taps do I need?"
Historical context:
  • Window Method: The most intuitive FIR design approach -- directly truncate the ideal impulse response and multiply by a window.
  • Kaiser Window: James Kaiser, 1974. Proposed empirical formulas for precisely controlling the relationship between transition bandwidth and stopband attenuation.
  • Parks-McClellan Algorithm (Equiripple Design): James McClellan & Thomas Parks, 1972. An optimal design based on Chebyshev approximation that achieves the minimum maximum error for a given filter order.

Principles

Intuition: The ideal low-pass filter has a rectangular frequency response (passband=1, stopband=0). Taking the inverse Fourier transform yields a sinc function -- but sinc has infinite length! We must truncate it. Truncation = multiplication by a rectangular window = frequency-domain convolution with sinc, leading to the Gibbs phenomenon (ripples at the edges). Using a better window reduces the ripples.

Window Method Steps:

  1. Ideal impulse response: $h_{\text{ideal}}[n] = \frac{\sin(\omega_c n)}{\pi n}$ (sinc function)
  2. Truncate to 2M+1 points (M determines the transition bandwidth)
  3. Multiply by window function $w[n]$: $h[n] = h_{\text{ideal}}[n] \cdot w[n]$

Kaiser Window Design Formulas:

Given stopband attenuation $A_s$ (dB) and transition bandwidth $\Delta\omega$ (rad/s):

M \approx \frac{A_s - 8}{2.285 \cdot \Delta\omega}

\beta = \begin{cases} 0.1102(A_s - 8.7) & \text{if } A_s > 50 \\ 0.5842(A_s - 21)^{0.4} + 0.07886(A_s - 21) & \text{if } 21 \leq A_s \leq 50 \\ 0 & \text{if } A_s < 21 \end{cases}

Show full derivation: Origin of the Kaiser Window Design Formulas

The Kaiser window is an approximation to the DPSS (Discrete Prolate Spheroidal Sequence), using the zeroth-order modified Bessel function of the first kind:

w[n] = \frac{I_0\!\left(\beta\sqrt{1-(2n/M)^2}\right)}{I_0(\beta)}, \quad |n| \leq M/2

Through extensive numerical experiments, Kaiser discovered the above empirical formulas relating $\beta$ and $M$ to the stopband attenuation $A_s$ and transition bandwidth $\Delta\omega$, with accuracy within 10%.

$\beta$ controls the window shape (larger means wider main lobe, lower sidelobes). $M$ controls the window length (longer means narrower main lobe, thus narrower transition band).

How to Use

Kaiser Window FIR Design Steps:

Concrete Example: Design an FIR low-pass filter with $f_c = 100$Hz, $f_s = 1000$Hz, transition band 50Hz (passband to 100Hz, stopband starting at 150Hz), stopband attenuation 60dB.

// Step 1: Convert specs to normalized frequency
ωp = 2π × 100 / 1000 = 0.2π  (passband edge)
ωs = 2π × 150 / 1000 = 0.3π  (stopband edge)
Δω = ωs - ωp = 0.1π ≈ 0.3142 rad
ωc = (ωp + ωs) / 2 = 0.25π   (cutoff frequency, midpoint of passband and stopband)
As = 60 dB

// Step 2: Compute β
As > 50, so β = 0.1102 × (60 - 8.7) = 0.1102 × 51.3 = 5.653

// Step 3: Compute filter order M
M = (As - 8) / (2.285 × Δω) = 52 / (2.285 × 0.3142) = 52 / 0.718 ≈ 72.4
Round up M = 73 (odd, ensuring Type I FIR)

// Step 4: Generate h[n]
for n in range(-36, 37):  // -M/2 to M/2
    h_ideal = sin(0.25π × n) / (π × n)  // limit at n=0 = ωc/π = 0.25
    w = kaiser(n, β, M)
    h[n] = h_ideal × w

// Step 5: Verify
// Passband ripple < 0.01dB (Kaiser window at As=60dB gives nearly flat passband)
// Stopband attenuation ~ 60dB ✓
// Transition bandwidth ~ 50Hz ✓
// Delay = M/2 = 36 samples = 36ms (@1kHz)

FIR vs IIR Comparison

PropertyFIR (Finite Impulse Response)IIR (Infinite Impulse Response)
StabilityAlways stable (no feedback)May be unstable (poles must be inside unit circle)
PhaseCan achieve perfect linear phaseNonlinear phase (unless allpass compensation is used)
OrderTypically requires higher orderMuch lower order for same specifications (~1/10)
DesignWindow method / Parks-McClellanButterworth / Chebyshev / Elliptic
Use casesWhen linear phase is needed (audio, communications)Real-time control, low-latency requirements
100
37
Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. Audio EQ (Equalizer): A 32-band EQ uses 32 FIR bandpass filters, each ~128 taps, processed in real-time using OLA.
  2. Communications baseband filtering: 5G NR channel filters use FIR, with passband flatness requirement <0.1dB and stopband attenuation >50dB.
  3. Biomedical signal preprocessing: ECG 50/60Hz notch filter can be implemented with a 2nd-order IIR (FIR would require hundreds of taps).
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Kaiser formulas are empirical: Results may differ by 5-10%; always verify the frequency response after design.
  • Linear phase = group delay = M/2 samples: The delay of a high-order FIR may be unacceptable (e.g., 1000 taps @1kHz = 500ms delay).
  • Parks-McClellan may not converge: Extremely narrow transition bands or very high stopband attenuation may require parameter adjustment.
When Not to Use FIR / Window Method:
  • Very narrow transition band + low latency needed -- use IIR (Butterworth/Chebyshev/Elliptic).
  • Optimal design needed (minimum order meeting specs) -- use Parks-McClellan instead of window method.
  • Alternative: Multirate Filtering -- decimating before filtering can greatly reduce computation. CIC + FIR combination is the standard SDR architecture.

📝 Worked Example

Design a low-pass FIR: passband cutoff 200Hz, stopband start 300Hz, stopband attenuation 50dB, fs=2000Hz. (a) Normalized frequencies? (b) Kaiser beta? (c) Filter order? (d) Center value of h[n]?

Show solution

(a) ωp=200/1000·π=0.2π, ωs=300/1000·π=0.3π, Δω=0.1π

(b) As=50 → β = 0.5842(50−21)0.4 + 0.07886(50−21) = 3.39

(c) M ≈ (50−8)/(2.285×0.1π) = 58.5 → round to 59 (odd) → order 59 = 60 points

(d) h[M/2] = ωc/π = 0.25π/π = 0.25

✅ Quick Check

Q1: Designing a low-pass FIR with 60dB stopband attenuation and 50Hz transition band -- how many taps are needed using a Kaiser window?

Show answer

M ~ (A_s - 8)/(2.285*dw) = (60-8)/(2.285*2pi*50/fs). For fs=1kHz, dw=0.1pi, M ~ (52)/(2.285*0.314) ~ 72 taps.

Q2: What is FIR's main advantage over IIR?

Show answer

FIR is inherently stable (no risk of poles outside the unit circle) and easily achieves exact linear phase (= pure delay, no distortion).

✅ Quick Check

Q1: Designing a low-pass FIR with 60dB stopband attenuation and transition band 0.1pi -- how many taps using a Kaiser window?

Show answer

M ~ (A_s - 8)/(2.285*dw) = (60-8)/(2.285x0.314) ~ 72 taps.

Q2: What is FIR's greatest advantage? And IIR's?

Show answer

FIR: inherently stable + exact linear phase. IIR: same performance with much lower order (= less computation and latency).

← 6.3 OLA/OLS6.5 2D FFT →

6.5 2D FFT & Image Processing

Learning Objectives

  • Understand the separability of 2D DFT (row FFT + column FFT)
  • Implement image low-pass/high-pass filtering using frequency-domain masks
  • Explain the relationship between MRI k-space and 2D FFT

Why does this matter? Because medical imaging (MRI), satellite remote sensing, and computer vision all perform denoising and feature extraction in the frequency domain -- 2D FFT is a fundamental skill in image processing.

Previously... Section 6.4 designed one-dimensional filters. Images are two-dimensional signals -- 2D FFT lets you analyze and process images in the spatial frequency domain.

Pain point: "The image has a periodic moire pattern -- how do I remove it?" "MRI scanning is too slow -- can we acquire only partial k-space to speed things up?"
Historical context: The Cooley-Tukey FFT algorithm of 1965 simultaneously gave rise to 2D FFT applications in image processing. The k-space concept for MRI was proposed by Paul Lauterbur in 1973 (for which he received the 2003 Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine). Satellite and astronomical image processing made extensive use of 2D FFT to remove periodic interference in the 1970-80s.

Principles

Intuition: In 1D, low frequency = slow variation, high frequency = rapid variation. The 2D case is completely analogous: low spatial frequency = slowly varying regions in the image (smooth areas, overall brightness), high spatial frequency = rapidly varying regions (edges, texture, noise).

2D DFT Formula:

F[u,v] = \sum_{m=0}^{M-1}\sum_{n=0}^{N-1} f[m,n]\,e^{-j2\pi(um/M + vn/N)}

Separability: The 2D DFT can be decomposed into row FFTs followed by column FFTs:

F[u,v] = \sum_{m=0}^{M-1}\left(\sum_{n=0}^{N-1} f[m,n]\,e^{-j2\pi vn/N}\right)e^{-j2\pi um/M}

Computation cost: 2D FFT of an M×N image = M row FFTs of length N + N column FFTs of length M = O(MN log(MN)).

Meaning of the frequency-domain center:

  • Center (u=0, v=0) = DC component = average image brightness
  • Near center = low frequency = overall structure and smooth regions
  • Far from center = high frequency = edges, details, noise
  • Bright lines in a specific direction = periodic structure in that direction within the image
Show full derivation: Separability of the 2D DFT

The 2D DFT kernel $e^{-j2\pi(um/M + vn/N)}$ can be factored into a product of two 1D kernels:

e^{-j2\pi(um/M + vn/N)} = e^{-j2\pi um/M} \cdot e^{-j2\pi vn/N}

Therefore:

F[u,v] = \sum_m e^{-j2\pi um/M} \underbrace{\left(\sum_n f[m,n]\,e^{-j2\pi vn/N}\right)}_{G[m,v] = \text{1D DFT of row m}}

First compute the 1D DFT along each row to get $G[m,v]$, then compute the 1D DFT along each column of $G[m,v]$ to get $F[u,v]$. The order can be swapped (columns first, then rows).

How to Use

2D Frequency-Domain Image Processing Steps:
  1. Load grayscale image $f[m,n]$ (M×N pixels).
  2. (Optional) Multiply by $(-1)^{m+n}$ to center the spectrum (fftshift).
  3. Compute 2D FFT -> $F[u,v]$.
  4. Design frequency-domain mask $H[u,v]$ (low-pass/high-pass/band-pass/notch).
  5. Multiply $G[u,v] = F[u,v] \cdot H[u,v]$.
  6. 2D IFFT -> processed image $g[m,n]$.

Concrete Example: Removing Power Line Interference from Satellite Images

// Satellite image 512x512 pixels, with horizontal stripe interference from 60Hz power lines
// Step 1: Compute 2D FFT and examine the spectrum
F = fft2(image)
F_shifted = fftshift(F)
magnitude = log(1 + abs(F_shifted))  // log scale display

// Step 2: Find bright spots in the spectrum corresponding to the interference
// Horizontal stripes -> bright spots on the vertical axis (at (0, +/-v0))
// v0 corresponds to the spatial frequency of the interference

// Step 3: Design a Notch Filter
// Place a small circular zero-gain region at each bright spot, radius r=5 pixels
H = ones(512, 512)
for each notch_point (u0, v0):
    for u, v in circle(u0, v0, r=5):
        H[u, v] = 0  // notch

// Step 4: Frequency-domain filtering + IFFT
G = F_shifted * H
result = real(ifft2(ifftshift(G)))
// Result: stripe interference removed, image details preserved

MRI Connection -- k-space Is the 2D Frequency Domain

The MRI scanner's RF coil directly acquires data in k-space (= the 2D Fourier space). Each scan trajectory fills one line of k-space. Image reconstruction = 2D IFFT.

  • Scan all of k-space -> complete image, but long scan time (several minutes).
  • Scan only k-space center (low frequency) -> blurry image but fast (used for localizer scans).
  • Scan only k-space periphery (high frequency) -> only edge information remains.
  • Compressed Sensing MRI: Randomly sample partial k-space (~25%), reconstruct the full image with sparse reconstruction algorithms. Scan time reduced by 4x.

Applications

  1. MRI image reconstruction: 256×256 k-space data -> 2D IFFT -> anatomical image. Compressed sensing achieves 4-8x acceleration.
  2. Astronomical image processing: Telescope images affected by atmospheric turbulence (PSF blurring); Wiener deconvolution in the frequency domain recovers details.
  3. Industrial X-ray inspection: Periodic structures in PCB X-ray images (via arrays) produce bright spots in the frequency domain; notch filtering highlights defects.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Boundary effects: 2D FFT assumes the image is periodically extended. If the image boundaries are discontinuous, cross-shaped spectral leakage will appear. Solution: mirror padding or edge tapering.
  • Ringing artifacts: Sharp frequency-domain masks (e.g., ideal low-pass) cause ringing in the spatial domain. Use Gaussian or Butterworth-type masks to mitigate.
  • Phase is important: Structural information in images is primarily in the phase (not the magnitude). Do not destroy the phase during processing.
When Not to Use 2D FFT:
  • Structures in the image are non-periodic -> frequency-domain methods perform poorly; use spatial-domain methods (e.g., bilateral filter, non-local means).
  • Local processing needed (different filtering for different regions) -> use 2D STFT or wavelet.
  • Alternative: Wavelet transforms are more powerful than 2D FFT for multi-scale analysis and are the mainstream approach for modern image compression (JPEG 2000) and denoising.
← 6.4 Filter Design6.6 OFDM →

6.6 OFDM Communication Systems

Learning Objectives

  • Describe the complete OFDM symbol transmit/receive flow (IFFT -> +CP -> channel -> -CP -> FFT)
  • Derive how CP converts linear convolution to circular convolution
  • Perform frequency-domain channel estimation using LS/MMSE

Why does this matter? Because your phone, Wi-Fi, and digital TV all use OFDM -- it is the absolute core of modern wireless communications.

Previously... Section 6.5 demonstrated FFT applications in imaging. Now let's look at communications -- OFDM uses IFFT/FFT to power every connection your phone makes.

Pain point: Wireless channels exhibit multipath effects -- after reflection, different copies of the same symbol arrive at the receiver at different times, causing Inter-Symbol Interference (ISI). Traditional single-carrier systems require complex equalizers to counteract ISI.
Historical context:
  • Robert Chang, 1966: Proposed the basic concept of OFDM at Bell Labs.
  • Weinstein & Ebert, 1971: First implemented OFDM modulation/demodulation using DFT/IDFT, making OFDM practically feasible.
  • Peled & Ruiz, 1980: Introduced Cyclic Prefix (CP) to solve the ISI problem.
  • Widespread adoption from the 1990s: DVB-T (digital TV), 802.11a (Wi-Fi), ADSL.
  • 4G LTE (2009), 5G NR (2018), Wi-Fi 6/7 are all based on OFDM.

Principles

Intuition: Instead of using one high-speed carrier to transmit large amounts of data (each symbol very short, easily destroyed by ISI), spread the data across N low-speed subcarriers for parallel transmission. Each subcarrier's symbol period = N times the original, far exceeding the channel delay spread, making ISI negligible.

Transmitter:

  1. N QAM (Quadrature Amplitude Modulation) symbols $D[0], D[1], \ldots, D[N-1]$
  2. N-point IFFT: $d[n] = \text{IFFT}\{D[k]\}$ -> time-domain OFDM symbol
  3. Add CP (Cyclic Prefix): copy the last $N_{CP}$ points of $d[n]$ to the front
  4. Through DAC and RF front-end for transmission

Receiver:

  1. Remove CP
  2. N-point FFT: $Y[k] = \text{FFT}\{y[n]\}$
  3. Per-subcarrier equalization: $\hat{D}[k] = Y[k] / \hat{H}[k]$ (only one complex division needed!)

Why Does CP Work?

CP converts linear convolution into circular convolution -- exactly what FFT multiplication assumes. As long as the CP length ≥ channel delay spread, the FFT at the receiver yields $Y[k] = H[k] \cdot D[k] + W[k]$ for each subcarrier -- a perfect frequency-domain multiplication relationship.

Show full derivation: How CP Eliminates ISI

Channel impulse response $h[n]$, length L (delay spread = L-1 samples).

Transmitted time-domain signal $d[n]$ has length N; after adding CP, length becomes $N + N_{CP}$.

Received signal: $r[n] = \sum_{l=0}^{L-1} h[l]\,d_{\text{CP}}[n-l] + w[n]$

After removing CP (taking n = N_CP to N_CP+N-1), if $N_{CP} \geq L-1$:

  • The "tail" of the previous OFDM symbol falls entirely within the CP interval -> discarded -> no ISI.
  • The convolution of the current symbol has fully "ramped up" within the CP interval; after CP removal, it is equivalent to circular convolution of $d[n]$.

Circular convolution in the frequency domain = pointwise multiplication:

Y[k] = H[k] \cdot D[k] + W[k]

Therefore each subcarrier can be equalized independently: $\hat{D}[k] = Y[k] / H[k]$. By contrast, a single-carrier system requires matrix inversion (O(N^3) or equalizer approximation).

How to Use

5G NR Specific Parameters:

Numerology (μ)Subcarrier Spacing (SCS)FFT Size (max)CP LengthSymbol TimeUse Case
015 kHz4096288 / 352 samples71.4 μs<3GHz wide-area coverage
130 kHz4096288 / 352 samples35.7 μs3.5GHz mainstream deployment
260 kHz4096288 / 352 samples17.8 μsmmWave
3120 kHz4096288 / 352 samples8.9 μsmmWave high-speed

Concrete Example: 5G NR μ=1 (30kHz SCS)

// FFT size: 4096
// Sampling rate: 4096 x 30kHz = 122.88 MHz
// Active subcarriers: 3276 (100MHz bandwidth)
// OFDM symbol time: 1/30kHz + CP = 33.33us + 2.34us = 35.67us
// Per slot (14 symbols): 0.5 ms
// OFDM symbols per second: 14 x 2000 = 28,000
// FFTs per second (TX+RX): 28,000 x 2 = 56,000 4096-point FFTs
// Complex multiplications per second: 56,000 x 4096 x log2(4096)/2 = 56,000 x 24,576 ~ 1.38 x 10^9

Channel Estimation

  • Pilots (Reference Signals): Insert known symbols at known subcarrier positions.
  • LS Estimation (Least Squares): $\hat{H}[k_p] = Y[k_p] / D_{\text{pilot}}[k_p]$ (only at pilot positions)
  • Interpolation: Interpolate from LS-estimated pilot points to obtain $\hat{H}[k]$ for all subcarriers.
  • MMSE Estimation: Uses channel delay profile statistics for optimal interpolation, 3-5dB better than LS.
  • Frequency-domain equalization: Zero-Forcing: $\hat{D}[k] = Y[k]/\hat{H}[k]$. MMSE: $\hat{D}[k] = \frac{\hat{H}^*[k]}{|\hat{H}[k]|^2 + \sigma_w^2/\sigma_D^2} Y[k]$

Applications

  1. 5G NR: Both downlink and uplink use CP-OFDM (uplink can also use DFT-s-OFDM to reduce PAPR). Over 2 million base stations deployed globally (2025).
  2. Wi-Fi 6/7 (802.11ax/be): OFDMA (multi-user OFDM), 2048-point FFT, supporting 160/320MHz bandwidth.
  3. DVB-T2 digital TV: 32K FFT (32768 points), handling long delay spreads (mountain reflections).
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Carrier Frequency Offset (CFO): Transmitter and receiver oscillator frequencies are not perfectly matched -> subcarriers are no longer orthogonal -> Inter-Carrier Interference (ICI). For example, 5G @28GHz with 0.1ppm oscillator accuracy = 2.8kHz offset, which is 9.3% of the 30kHz SCS -- frequency synchronization is essential.
  • PAPR (Peak-to-Average Power Ratio): When N subcarrier phases align, peak power can reach N times the average power. PAPR ~10-12dB -> the power amplifier needs a very large linear range (expensive and power-hungry). PAPR reduction methods: clipping, DFT-s-OFDM, tone reservation.
  • CP is overhead: CP carries no new information, consuming ~7% of time and spectral resources.
When Not to Use OFDM:
  • Channel has almost no multipath (e.g., satellite communications) -> single carrier is sufficient, with no PAPR problem.
  • Ultra-low latency requirements (CP adds latency) -> FBMC (Filter Bank Multi-Carrier) does not need CP.
  • Alternative: SC-FDMA (Single-Carrier FDMA) is used for LTE uplink, with 2-3dB lower PAPR than OFDM. FBMC, UFMC are beyond-5G candidate technologies.

✅ Quick Check

Q1: What happens if the OFDM Cyclic Prefix (CP) is too short?

Show answer

Channel delay exceeds CP -> adjacent OFDM symbols overlap -> ISI. Also, the effective convolution within the FFT interval is not circular -> subcarrier orthogonality is destroyed -> ICI.

Q2: 5G NR with 30kHz subcarrier spacing and 4096 FFT -- what is the approximate bandwidth?

Show answer

30kHz x 4096 ~ 122.88 MHz.

Interactive: OFDM Symbol Generation (IFFT)

64 subcarriers, each carrying a QPSK symbol. IFFT converts frequency-domain data to a time-domain OFDM symbol.

Adjust parameters above to see the plot
Adjust parameters above to see the plot
← 6.5 2D FFT6.7 Radar →

6.7 Radar Signal Processing

Learning Objectives

  • Implement pulse compression using matched filtering
  • Explain the dual-FFT architecture of Range-Doppler processing
  • Assess range-velocity resolution capability from the ambiguity function

Why does this matter? Because autonomous driving, weather forecasting, and military detection all rely on radar, and radar signal processing is essentially FFT at its core.

Previously... Section 6.6 covered FFT in communications. Radar is also a heavy user of FFT -- range uses fast-time FFT, velocity uses slow-time FFT.

Pain point: Radar must simultaneously measure a target's range and velocity. Range relies on pulse delay -> fast-time FFT; velocity relies on Doppler shift -> slow-time FFT. Both dimensions require FFT.
Historical context:
  • Pulse Compression: P.M. Woodward, 1953. Uses matched filtering to improve range resolution without increasing peak power.
  • Pulse-Doppler Processing: 1960s, led by the U.S. Air Force, using FFT to extract Doppler frequencies from multiple pulse echoes.
  • Synthetic Aperture Radar (SAR): Developed from the 1950s, synthesizing a large antenna from the flight path to achieve high-resolution imaging.

Principles

Intuition: Transmit a known waveform $s(t)$; the target's reflected signal is a delayed and frequency-shifted version. To determine the delay (= range) and frequency shift (= velocity), the best approach is "matched filtering" -- correlating the known waveform with the echo. FFT enables this correlation to be computed efficiently.

Matched Filter

The optimal filter that maximizes SNR = time-reversed conjugate of the transmitted signal:

h_{\text{MF}}(t) = s^*(-t)

Frequency-domain implementation: $Y(\omega) = S^*(\omega) \cdot X(\omega)$ -> IFFT -> compressed pulse

Range-Doppler Processing

Received data arranged in a 2D matrix: [fast-time × slow-time]

  1. Fast-time FFT (each row): Range compression, compressing the chirp pulse into a narrow peak.
  2. Slow-time FFT (each column): Doppler processing, extracting velocity from phase changes across multiple pulses.
  3. Result: Range-Doppler Map -- each bright spot = one target, horizontal position = range, vertical position = velocity.

Resolution:

\Delta R = \frac{c}{2B} \quad (\text{range resolution, B = bandwidth})

\Delta v = \frac{\lambda}{2T_{\text{CPI}}} \quad (\text{velocity resolution, }T_{\text{CPI}} = \text{coherent processing interval})

Ambiguity Function

Definition:

\chi(\tau, f_d) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} s(t)\,s^*(t-\tau)\,e^{j2\pi f_d t}\,dt

Intuitive interpretation: The ambiguity function describes the waveform's resolution capability in the "range ($\tau$) -- Doppler ($f_d$)" plane. $|\chi(0,0)|$ = peak (perfect match), $|\chi(\tau, f_d)|$ at other positions = sidelobes (degree of ambiguity).

Woodward's theorem: The total volume of the ambiguity function is conserved -- if you suppress sidelobes in one area, they rise elsewhere. Waveform design is about "sculpting" the sidelobe shape across the range-Doppler plane.

  • LFM Chirp: Diagonal ridge-shaped ambiguity function (range-Doppler coupling, but narrow main lobe).
  • Phase-Coded Pulse: Thumbtack-shaped (low sidelobes but Doppler-sensitive).
Show full derivation: Range Resolution of LFM Chirp

LFM (Linear Frequency Modulated) chirp: $s(t) = \text{rect}(t/T)\,e^{j\pi \mu t^2}$，其中 $\mu = B/T$ (chirp rate)。

Matched filter output (at zero Doppler):

\chi(\tau, 0) = \int s(t)\,s^*(t-\tau)\,dt

After derivation (expanding and simplifying the quadratic phase terms), the result approximates to:

|\chi(\tau, 0)| \approx T\,\text{sinc}(B\tau)

The first null of the sinc function is at $\tau = 1/B$, corresponding to range:

\Delta R = \frac{c\tau}{2} = \frac{c}{2B}

Key observation: range resolution depends only on bandwidth B, independent of pulse duration T. This is the power of pulse compression -- long pulse (high energy) + large bandwidth (high resolution) can be achieved simultaneously.

How to Use

Concrete Example: Automotive 77GHz FMCW Radar

// System parameters
f_c = 77 GHz           // carrier frequency
B = 1 GHz              // chirp bandwidth
T_chirp = 50 μs        // single chirp duration
N_chirps = 128         // chirps per CPI
λ = c / f_c = 3.896 mm // wavelength

// Range resolution
ΔR = c / (2B) = 3×10⁸ / (2×10⁹) = 0.15 m = 15 cm

// Maximum unambiguous range
R_max = c × T_chirp / 2 = 3×10⁸ × 50×10⁻⁶ / 2 = 7500 m
// (In practice limited by ADC sampling rate and SNR, typically ~200m)

// Velocity resolution
Δv = λ / (2 × N_chirps × T_chirp)
   = 3.896×10⁻³ / (2 × 128 × 50×10⁻⁶) = 0.304 m/s ≈ 1.1 km/h

// Maximum unambiguous velocity
v_max = λ / (4 × T_chirp) = 3.896×10⁻³ / (4 × 50×10⁻⁶) = 19.5 m/s ≈ 70 km/h

// FFT processing
// Fast-time: 256-point FFT -> 256 range bins, each bin = R_max/256 ~ 29 m
//            (actual ADC samples, e.g., 256 @10MHz -> covers 3840m)
// Slow-time: 128-point FFT -> 128 velocity bins
// Range-Doppler Map: 256 x 128 matrix, each bright spot = one target
Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. Automotive FMCW radar: 77GHz, B=1-4GHz, range resolution 3.75-15cm. 3-5 radar units per vehicle, global annual production exceeding 500 million units (2025).
  2. Weather radar: S-band (2.7-3.0GHz), using Doppler FFT to measure radial velocity of rain particles (wind field). WSR-88D Doppler radar uses 1024-point FFT, velocity resolution ~0.5 m/s.
  3. SAR satellite imagery: Azimuth direction uses FFT for focusing, achieving ~1m spatial resolution. Sentinel-1 satellite processes TB-scale data per orbit.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • LFM range-Doppler coupling: A moving target's Doppler shift is misinterpreted as a range offset. Requires Doppler compensation or dual-slope chirp.
  • Sidelobe masking: Sidelobes of strong targets may obscure weak targets. Requires windowing to reduce sidelobes + CFAR (Constant False Alarm Rate) adaptive threshold detection.
  • Blind Speed: When the target velocity is exactly an integer multiple of $v_{\text{max}}$, the Doppler shift wraps around to zero -> undetectable. Solved by staggered PRF (Pulse Repetition Frequency).
When Not to Use FFT-based Radar Processing:
  • Very few known targets -> parametric estimation methods (e.g., MUSIC, ESPRIT) achieve higher resolution than FFT.
  • Nonlinear FM (NLFM) waveforms -> matched filtering still applies, but standard FFT-based processing needs modification.
  • Alternative: Compressed Sensing radar can reconstruct the Range-Doppler map with fewer samples (suitable for sparse scenarios). MIMO radar uses multiple transmit/receive antennas to increase virtual aperture.

📝 Worked Example

77GHz FMCW automotive radar: bandwidth B=1GHz, chirp time 50us, 128 chirps. (a) Range resolution? (b) Maximum unambiguous range (256-point FFT)? (c) Velocity resolution?

Show solution

(a) ΔR = c/(2B) = 3×10⁸/(2×10⁹) = 0.15m = 15cm

(b) Rmax = N·ΔR = 256×0.15 = 38.4m

(c) λ = c/f = 3.9mm, Δv = λ/(2·128·50μs) = 3.9×10⁻³/(2×128×50×10⁻⁶) = 0.30 m/s

✅ Quick Check

Q1: Radar range resolution 15cm -- how much bandwidth is needed?

Show answer

dR = c/(2B) -> B = c/(2*0.15) = 3x10^8/(0.3) = 1 GHz.

Q2: Why is the LFM chirp ambiguity function a diagonal ridge?

Show answer

Because LFM frequency increases linearly with time, coupling occurs between range and Doppler -- a stationary target's range estimate shifts due to Doppler offset.

← 6.6 OFDM6.8 Array Processing →

6.8 Array Signal Processing

Learning Objectives

  • Write the ULA steering vector and explain the spatial Nyquist condition
  • Compare Delay-and-Sum, Capon, and MUSIC beamforming methods
  • Calculate the array's angular resolution from the beamwidth formula

Why does this matter? Because 5G massive MIMO, phased array radar, and sonar localization all rely on antenna arrays -- spatial filtering is the spatial-dimension equivalent of FFT.

Previously... Section 6.7's radar used FFT for range-velocity estimation. Antenna arrays extend the same concept to space -- using "spatial FFT" to estimate signal direction of arrival.

Pain point: A base station simultaneously receives signals from multiple users -- how does it distinguish signals from different directions? How does a radar know the target's angle? A single antenna cannot do this -- an antenna array is needed.
Historical context:
  • Phased Array: Used in WWII radar from the 1940s.
  • Capon (MVDR) Beamforming: Jack Capon, 1969. Minimum Variance Distortionless Response.
  • MUSIC Algorithm: Ralph Schmidt, 1986. Exploits the orthogonality between signal subspace and noise subspace for high-resolution DOA (Direction of Arrival) estimation.
  • Massive MIMO: Thomas Marzetta, 2010. Proposed using massive antenna arrays (64-256) to simultaneously serve multiple users. Became a core 5G technology.

Principles

Intuition: A set of uniformly spaced antennas (Uniform Linear Array, ULA) receives the same plane wave, but each antenna receives it at a slightly different time (depending on the wave's angle of incidence). This time difference = phase difference. Analyzing these phase differences reveals the signal's direction of arrival. This is perfectly analogous to time-domain sampling: antenna spacing = spatial sampling interval, angle of incidence = spatial frequency.

ULA Model

M antennas equally spaced at distance d. A plane wave arrives from angle $\theta$, with adjacent antenna phase difference:

\Delta\phi = \frac{2\pi d \sin\theta}{\lambda}

Steering Vector:

\mathbf{a}(\theta) = \begin{bmatrix} 1 \\ e^{j\frac{2\pi d\sin\theta}{\lambda}} \\ e^{j\frac{2\pi \cdot 2d\sin\theta}{\lambda}} \\ \vdots \\ e^{j\frac{2\pi(M-1)d\sin\theta}{\lambda}} \end{bmatrix}

Beamforming = Spatial Filtering

y = \mathbf{w}^H \mathbf{x}

where $\mathbf{x}$ is the received vector from M antennas, $\mathbf{w}$ is the weight vector.

Conventional Beamforming (Delay-and-Sum, DAS): $\mathbf{w} = \mathbf{a}(\theta_0)/M$. This is essentially a spatial DFT -- scanning all $\theta$ is equivalent to computing the DFT of spatial samples.

Spatial Nyquist Theorem

d ≤ λ/2, otherwise grating lobes appear (spatial aliasing! Perfectly analogous to aliasing in the time-domain Nyquist theorem).

When d > λ/2, signals from different directions produce identical phase differences on the antenna array -> indistinguishable -> spatial aliasing.

Show full derivation: Spatial DFT and Angular Resolution

Spatial power spectrum of the DAS beamformer:

P_{\text{DAS}}(\theta) = \frac{1}{M^2}\left|\sum_{m=0}^{M-1} e^{j\frac{2\pi md}{\lambda}(\sin\theta - \sin\theta_0)}\right|^2

Let spatial frequency $u = d\sin\theta/\lambda$ -- this is a discrete Fourier sum!

Main beam width (3dB beamwidth):

\Delta\theta_{3\text{dB}} \approx \frac{0.886\lambda}{Md\cos\theta_0}

At broadside ($\theta_0 = 0$), this simplifies to $\Delta\theta \approx 0.886\lambda/(Md)$.

More antennas (larger M) and larger spacing (larger d) -> narrower beam -> higher angular resolution. But d > λ/2 produces grating lobes.

How to Use

Concrete Example: 5G Massive MIMO Base Station

// Parameters
M = 64 antennas (8x8 planar array)
f_c = 28 GHz (mmWave)
λ = c / f_c = 3×10⁸ / 28×10⁹ = 10.71 mm
d = λ/2 = 5.36 mm (antenna spacing)

// Beamwidth
Δθ ≈ 0.886 × λ / (M_row × d) = 0.886 × 10.71 / (8 × 5.36) = 0.221 rad ≈ 12.7°
// (8x8 array has 8 antennas in both horizontal and vertical)
// Using all 64 antennas for 2D beamforming:
// Effective aperture = 8 x 5.36mm = 42.9mm
// Beamwidth ~ 12.7° x 12.7° (both dimensions)

// Spatial multiplexing capability
// 64 antennas can form multiple independent beams simultaneously
// Maximum ~M/2 = 32 users (theoretical limit)
// Practically 8-16 concurrent users (limited by channel correlation)

// Spectral efficiency improvement
// Single user: ~5 bps/Hz
// 16-user MU-MIMO: ~80 bps/Hz (ideal case)

Advanced Methods

Capon (MVDR) Beamformer:

\mathbf{w}_{\text{Capon}} = \frac{\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{a}(\theta_0)}{\mathbf{a}^H(\theta_0)\mathbf{R}^{-1}\mathbf{a}(\theta_0)}

Minimizes output power subject to unity gain in the target direction. Result: a much narrower beam than DAS, effectively suppressing interference.

MUSIC DOA Estimation:

Completely analogous to frequency-domain MUSIC (see Section 3.4), just replacing "frequency" with "angle":

P_{\text{MUSIC}}(\theta) = \frac{1}{\mathbf{a}^H(\theta)\,\mathbf{E}_n\mathbf{E}_n^H\,\mathbf{a}(\theta)}

where $\mathbf{E}_n$ is the noise subspace. Peak positions = signal directions of arrival. Can resolve sources separated by much less than the beamwidth.

16
0.5
Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. 5G Massive MIMO: 64-256 antennas, mmWave beam tracking, beam direction updated every millisecond.
  2. Radar Electronic Scanning (AESA): Fighter jet radars use thousands of antenna elements for electronic scanning, switching beam direction in milliseconds (mechanical rotation takes seconds).
  3. Hearing aid beamforming: 2-4 microphone arrays for speech enhancement. In noisy environments, the beam is steered toward the speaker (5-10dB SNR improvement).
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Calibration errors: Inconsistencies in antenna position, gain, and phase severely degrade performance. Massive MIMO requires periodic Over-the-Air Calibration.
  • Capon/MUSIC require sufficient snapshots: The covariance matrix $\mathbf{R}$ estimation needs ≥2M snapshots to be stable. Rapidly changing environments may not allow enough accumulation.
  • Wideband signals: The steering vector $\mathbf{a}(\theta)$ depends on frequency. Wideband signals require space-time processing (wideband beamforming), such as DFT beamforming + sub-band processing.
  • Near-field effects: When the target distance < $2D^2/\lambda$ (D = array aperture), the plane wave assumption breaks down and near-field beamforming is needed.
When Not to Use Array Processing:
  • Only one signal source and direction is not needed -> a single antenna suffices.
  • Signal sources are concentrated in a narrow angular range (e.g., satellite communications) -> a fixed-pointing dish antenna is cheaper than an array.
  • Alternative: If you need to separate co-frequency signals but don't care about direction, use CDMA (Code Division Multiple Access) or NOMA (Non-Orthogonal Multiple Access).

✅ Quick Check

Q1: What happens when ULA antenna spacing d > λ/2?

Show answer

Grating lobes -- spatial-domain aliasing. Analogous to aliasing from insufficient sampling rate in the time domain.

Q2: 5G massive MIMO with 64 antennas @28GHz (λ≈10.7mm) -- approximate beamwidth?

Show answer

~ λ/(M*d) ~ 10.7/(64x5.35) ~ 0.03 rad ~ 1.8° (assuming d=λ/2=5.35mm).

← 6.7 Radar6.9 Biomedical →

6.9 Biomedical Signal Analysis

Learning Objectives

  • List the five major EEG frequency bands and explain their physiological significance
  • Use the Welch method to estimate EEG band power ratios
  • Describe the standard HRV frequency-domain analysis workflow (R-R intervals -> resampling -> PSD -> LF/HF)

Why does this matter? Because clinical interpretation of EEG/ECG increasingly relies on frequency-domain quantitative metrics -- an essential skill for entering biomedical engineering.

Previously... Section 6.8 dealt with man-made signals. Biomedical signals (brainwaves, ECG) are also important application areas for frequency-domain analysis.

Pain point: Clinicians need objective quantitative metrics to diagnose epilepsy (abnormal brainwaves), sleep disorders (band power changes), and autonomic dysfunction (HRV abnormalities). Examining time-domain waveforms alone is too subjective and difficult -- frequency-domain tools are needed.
Historical context:
  • Hans Berger, 1929: First recorded human EEG (Electroencephalography), discovering the 8-13Hz α rhythm (Alpha rhythm) when eyes are closed.
  • HRV frequency-domain analysis standardization: The European Society of Cardiology (ESC) and North American Society of Pacing and Electrophysiology (NASPE) published the Task Force report in 1996, defining the HRV LF/HF bands and analysis methods.

Principles

Five Major EEG Frequency Bands

BandFrequency RangePhysiological SignificanceClinical Application
δ (Delta)0.5 – 4 HzDeep sleep (N3 stage)Sleep depth assessment, brain injury detection
θ (Theta)4 – 8 HzLight sleep, meditation, memory encodingSleep staging (N1/N2), attention metrics
α (Alpha)8 – 13 HzRelaxed wakefulness, eyes closedAlertness assessment, BCI control
β (Beta)13 – 30 HzFocus, alertness, active thinkingAttention monitoring, anxiety assessment
γ (Gamma)30 – 100 HzHigher cognitive functions, cross-regional integrationEpileptic high-frequency oscillation detection

Analysis Methods:

  • Welch PSD (see Section 3.2) -> integrate power in each band -> power ratios
  • For example, α/θ ratio = attention metric (higher = more focused)
  • Relative power: $P_\alpha^{\text{rel}} = P_\alpha / P_{\text{total}}$

HRV Frequency-Domain Analysis

Heart Rate Variability (HRV) reflects the activity of the Autonomic Nervous System.

BandFrequency RangePrimary Regulation
VLF (Very Low Frequency)0.003 – 0.04 HzThermoregulation, renin-angiotensin system
LF (Low Frequency)0.04 – 0.15 HzSympathetic + parasympathetic (not purely sympathetic!)
HF (High Frequency)0.15 – 0.4 HzParasympathetic (respiratory sinus arrhythmia)

Analysis workflow: R-R interval series (Tachogram) -> resampling (non-uniform -> uniform, typically cubic spline @4Hz) -> PSD -> band power integration.

Show full derivation: Why does HRV need resampling?

The R-R interval series is inherently non-uniformly sampled (because heartbeat intervals are not fixed). Fourier analysis requires uniform sampling.

Solution:

  1. Place each R-R interval value at its corresponding time point (non-uniform sequence).
  2. Use cubic spline interpolation to generate a uniform sequence (typically 4Hz = one point per 0.25 seconds).
  3. The Nyquist frequency at 4Hz sampling rate = 2Hz, sufficient to cover the HF band (0.4Hz).

Alternative: The Lomb-Scargle periodogram can directly process non-uniformly sampled data, but Welch PSD is more prevalent in clinical practice.

How to Use

EEG Analysis Steps

// Step 1: Signal acquisition
// Sampling rate: 250-1000 Hz (clinically common: 256 or 512 Hz)
// Electrodes: International 10-20 system (19-64 channels)
// ADC resolution: 24-bit (EEG amplitude is only ~10-100 μV)

// Step 2: Preprocessing
band_pass_filter(0.5, 100)  // Remove DC drift and high-frequency noise
notch_filter(60)            // Remove power line interference (60Hz)
ICA_artifact_removal()      // Independent Component Analysis to remove EOG/EMG artifacts

// Step 3: Spectral analysis (Welch method)
segment_length = 2 * fs     // 2-second segments (512 points @256Hz)
overlap = 0.5               // 50% overlap
window = 'hann'
PSD = welch(eeg_channel, segment_length, overlap, window)

// Step 4: Band power calculation
P_delta = integrate(PSD, 0.5, 4)    // delta power
P_theta = integrate(PSD, 4, 8)      // theta power
P_alpha = integrate(PSD, 8, 13)     // alpha power
P_beta  = integrate(PSD, 13, 30)    // beta power
P_gamma = integrate(PSD, 30, 100)   // gamma power
P_total = P_delta + P_theta + P_alpha + P_beta + P_gamma

// Step 5: Compute metrics
attention_index = P_alpha / P_theta  // alpha/theta ratio
relative_alpha = P_alpha / P_total   // relative alpha power

// Sleep staging examples:
// Awake (eyes closed): alpha dominant (relative_alpha > 0.4)
// N1 light sleep:      theta increases, alpha decreases
// N2:                  Sleep spindles (12-14Hz bursts) + K-complex
// N3 deep sleep:       delta dominant (relative_delta > 0.5, amplitude > 75uV)
// REM:                 Low-amplitude fast waves (similar to awake, but with rapid eye movements)

HRV Analysis Steps

// Step 1: R-wave detection (Pan-Tompkins Algorithm)
ecg_filtered = bandpass(ecg, 5, 15)  // Focus on QRS complex
ecg_diff = differentiate(ecg_filtered)
ecg_squared = ecg_diff ** 2
ecg_integrated = moving_average(ecg_squared, 150ms)
R_peaks = adaptive_threshold(ecg_integrated)

// Step 2: Compute R-R intervals (Tachogram)
RR_intervals = diff(R_peaks) / fs  // units: seconds
RR_times = cumsum(RR_intervals)    // corresponding time points

// Step 3: Remove ectopic beats
for i in range(len(RR)):
    if abs(RR[i] - RR[i-1]) / RR[i-1] > 0.20:
        RR[i] = interpolate(neighbors)  // interpolate replacement

// Step 4: Resample to uniform spacing
fs_resample = 4  // 4 Hz
RR_resampled = cubic_spline_interpolate(RR_times, RR_intervals, fs_resample)
RR_resampled -= mean(RR_resampled)  // remove DC

// Step 5: Welch PSD
segment_length = 256  // 256 點 @4Hz = 64 秒
overlap = 0.5
PSD_hrv = welch(RR_resampled, segment_length, overlap, 'hann')

// Step 6: Band power
LF_power = integrate(PSD_hrv, 0.04, 0.15)  // ms²
HF_power = integrate(PSD_hrv, 0.15, 0.40)  // ms²
LF_HF_ratio = LF_power / HF_power

// Normal reference values:
// Healthy young adult: LF/HF ~ 1.0-2.0
// Stress/anxiety:      LF/HF up (sympathetic activation)
// Athlete at rest:     HF up, LF/HF down (parasympathetic dominant)
// Heart failure:       Total power greatly reduced, LF/HF may be abnormally high or low
Adjust parameters above to see the plot

Applications

  1. Brain-Computer Interface (BCI): Detects μ (8-12Hz) / β (18-26Hz) rhythm changes caused by motor imagery to control wheelchairs or robotic arms. Classification accuracy 70-90%.
  2. Sleep monitoring wearables: Single-channel forehead EEG (e.g., Muse headband), computing δ/θ/α power for automatic sleep staging. 256Hz sampling rate, Welch PSD on 30-second epochs.
  3. Cardiac autonomic assessment: 5-minute short-term HRV analysis for stress assessment, exercise training monitoring, and diabetic autonomic neuropathy screening. Apple Watch/Garmin watches have this feature built in.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • EEG artifacts dominate the spectrum: Eye movements (EOG) produce large-amplitude 0-4Hz interference -> misidentified as δ activity. EMG contaminates >20Hz. 50/60Hz power line interference. Artifacts must be removed first.
  • LF ≠ sympathetic activity: This is the most common misconception! The LF band is regulated by both sympathetic and parasympathetic nerves (baroreflex mechanism). Only HF more purely reflects parasympathetic activity. LF/HF ratio can only serve as a rough indicator of "sympathetic-parasympathetic balance."
  • Short HRV segments are unreliable: VLF requires at least 5 minutes of data (otherwise frequency resolution is insufficient). LF requires at least 2 minutes. 24-hour long-segment analysis is more stable.
  • Breathing rate affects HF: If the subject breathes very slowly (<0.15Hz, e.g., during meditation), the respiratory component falls into the LF band rather than HF -> LF/HF ratio is distorted. Breathing rate must be recorded simultaneously.
When Not to Use Frequency-Domain Analysis:
  • Transient events in EEG (e.g., epileptic spikes, lasting <200ms) -> frequency-domain methods cannot localize in time. Use time-frequency analysis (STFT, wavelet).
  • HRV during arrhythmia (e.g., atrial fibrillation) -> RR intervals are completely irregular, frequency-domain analysis is meaningless. Use nonlinear methods (approximate entropy, Poincare plot).
  • Alternative: Multiscale Entropy analysis, Recurrence Plot, deep learning automatic feature extraction.

✅ Quick Check

Q1: Which EEG frequency band is enhanced when eyes are closed?

Show answer

Alpha waves (8-13 Hz) -- this is the Berger effect.

Q2: What does the LF/HF ratio in HRV frequency-domain analysis represent?

Show answer

Often interpreted as a sympathetic/parasympathetic balance indicator, but this is actually an oversimplification -- LF is regulated by both sympathetic and parasympathetic systems.

← 6.8 Array Processing6.10 Vibration →

6.10 Complete Vibration Analysis Workflow

Learning Objectives

  • Describe the complete 6-step vibration analysis workflow from sensor to fault diagnosis
  • Calculate BPFO/BPFI characteristic frequencies from given bearing parameters
  • Distinguish spectral signatures of imbalance (1X), misalignment (2X), looseness (harmonics), and bearing faults (BPFO)

Why does this matter? Because Predictive Maintenance saves industry billions of dollars annually, and its core is vibration spectrum analysis.

Previously... Section 6.9 analyzed human body signals. This final chapter ties all tools together, demonstrating the complete end-to-end vibration analysis workflow from sensor to fault diagnosis.

Pain point: A factory has 200 rotating machines (pumps, motors, compressors) -- how do you know which one is about to fail? Disassembly inspection is too expensive, but unplanned downtime is even costlier (a single unplanned shutdown can cost hundreds of thousands to millions of dollars). Vibration spectrum analysis = a non-invasive "stethoscope."
Historical context:
  • ISO 10816 / ISO 20816: Defines vibration severity levels (A: Good to D: Dangerous), the international standard for industrial vibration monitoring.
  • Robert Randall and Jérôme Antoni: Systematized the Envelope Spectrum Analysis method, particularly the band selection strategy (Spectral Kurtosis) for bearing fault diagnosis.

Complete Vibration Analysis Workflow

Step 1: Signal Acquisition

  • Sensor: Accelerometer, typically IEPE/ICP type (built-in amplifier, single coaxial cable output).
  • Sampling rate: At least 2.56× the highest frequency of interest (ISO recommends including the anti-aliasing filter roll-off).
    • General rotating machinery: fs = 25.6 kHz (covering up to 10 kHz)
    • Bearing diagnostics: fs = 51.2 kHz (high-frequency resonance band needed)
    • Gearbox analysis: fs > 50 kHz (meshing frequency can be very high)
  • Anti-Aliasing Filter: Analog low-pass filter before the ADC, cutoff frequency set at 0.4×fs.
  • Mounting: Stud mount > magnet > adhesive > handheld probe. Mounting quality directly affects high-frequency response.

Step 2: Preprocessing

// DC offset removal
signal -= mean(signal)

// High-pass filter: remove < 5Hz low-frequency interference
// (inertial base vibration, sensor mounting looseness, temperature drift)
signal = highpass(signal, fc=5, order=4)

// Band-pass filter when necessary
// (focus on frequency range of interest, e.g., near gear meshing frequency)
signal_bp = bandpass(signal, f_low, f_high, order=6)

Step 3: Basic Spectrum Analysis

// Windowed FFT + Welch averaging
nfft = 8192          // Frequency resolution = fs/nfft = 25600/8192 = 3.125 Hz
window = 'hann'
n_averages = 16      // 16-segment average, reduces random variability
overlap = 0.5        // 50% overlap
PSD = welch(signal, nfft, overlap, window, n_averages)

Identifying Characteristic Frequencies:

Fault TypeCharacteristic FrequencySpectral Signature
Imbalance1X = shaft speedHigh 1X amplitude, primarily radial
Misalignment2X = 2× shaft speedHigh 2X amplitude, possible axial vibration
Mechanical LoosenessMultiple harmonics (1X, 2X, 3X...)Multiple shaft speed harmonics, possible 0.5X sub-harmonic
Gear FaultGear mesh frequency = tooth count × shaft speedSidebands at mesh frequency ±1X
Blade/Vane IssueBlade pass frequency = blade count × shaft speedFluid pulsation in pumps/fans
Bearing FaultBPFO/BPFI/BSF/FTFRequires envelope spectrum analysis (see Step 4)

Step 4: Envelope Spectrum Analysis (Bearing Fault Diagnosis)

Intuition: Each time a bearing defect passes through the load zone, it produces a brief impact that excites the high-frequency structural resonance of the bearing housing (2-10 kHz). These impacts repeat at the bearing fault frequency. Looking directly at the spectrum only shows the high-frequency resonance, not the periodicity of the fault frequency. Envelope analysis = bandpass to extract resonance band -> Hilbert envelope -> FFT of envelope -> search for fault frequencies in the envelope spectrum.

// Step 4a: Bandpass filtering (focus on high-frequency resonance band)
// Band selection: use Spectral Kurtosis (SK) to automatically find optimal band
// or select 2-10 kHz based on experience
signal_bp = bandpass(signal, 2000, 10000, order=6)

// Step 4b: Hilbert envelope
analytic = hilbert(signal_bp)
envelope = abs(analytic)

// Step 4c: Envelope FFT
envelope -= mean(envelope)  // remove DC
envelope_spectrum = fft(envelope * hann_window)

// Step 4d: Search for bearing characteristic frequencies in envelope spectrum
// If peaks at BPFO and its harmonics (2xBPFO, 3xBPFO...) -> outer race fault
// If peaks at BPFI and its harmonics, modulated by 1X -> inner race fault
// If peaks at BSF and its harmonics -> rolling element fault
// If peaks at FTF and its harmonics -> cage fault

Step 5: Time-Frequency Analysis (Variable Speed Conditions)

If the speed is not constant (run-up, coast-down, load changes), characteristic frequencies vary with time. STFT or Order Tracking is needed.

  • STFT: Observe how frequencies change over time (color waterfall plot).
  • Order Tracking: Use a tachometer for synchronized sampling, converting the time axis to "angle" so that characteristic frequencies become fixed "orders."

Step 6: Trend Monitoring

// Acquire vibration data daily (or weekly)
// Track key metrics over time:
// - Overall RMS vibration level (mm/s)
// - RMS in specific frequency bands (e.g., 1X, BPFO band)
// - Peak and Crest Factor

// Alarm threshold settings:
// Method 1: ISO 20816 absolute thresholds
//   Zone A (< 2.8 mm/s): Good
//   Zone B (2.8-7.1 mm/s): Acceptable
//   Zone C (7.1-18 mm/s): Requires attention
//   Zone D (> 18 mm/s): Dangerous, shut down immediately
// Method 2: Baseline relative thresholds
//   Warning: Baseline + 6 dB (2x)
//   Alarm:   Baseline + 12 dB (4x)

// Sudden upward trend -> schedule maintenance (planned shutdown)
// 10-100x cheaper than unplanned downtime

Bearing Characteristic Frequencies

\text{BPFO} = \frac{n}{2} f_r \left(1 - \frac{d}{D}\cos\alpha\right) \quad \text{(outer race fault frequency)}

\text{BPFI} = \frac{n}{2} f_r \left(1 + \frac{d}{D}\cos\alpha\right) \quad \text{(inner race fault frequency)}

\text{BSF} = \frac{D}{2d} f_r \left[1 - \left(\frac{d}{D}\cos\alpha\right)^2\right] \quad \text{(rolling element fault frequency)}

\text{FTF} = \frac{1}{2} f_r \left(1 - \frac{d}{D}\cos\alpha\right) \quad \text{(cage fault frequency)}

where: n = number of rolling elements, fr = shaft speed (Hz), d = rolling element diameter, D = pitch diameter, α = contact angle.

Concrete Example: SKF 6205 Bearing

// SKF 6205 deep groove ball bearing parameters
n = 9           // number of balls
d = 7.94 mm     // ball diameter
D = 38.5 mm     // pitch diameter
α = 0°          // contact angle (deep groove ball bearing)
cos(α) = 1

// Shaft speed: 1800 RPM = 30 Hz
f_r = 30 Hz

// BPFO (outer race fault frequency)
BPFO = (9/2) × 30 × (1 - 7.94/38.5) = 4.5 × 30 × 0.7938
     = 4.5 × 30 × 0.7938 = 107.2 Hz

// BPFI (inner race fault frequency)
BPFI = (9/2) × 30 × (1 + 7.94/38.5) = 4.5 × 30 × 1.2062
     = 4.5 × 30 × 1.2062 = 162.8 Hz

// BSF (rolling element fault frequency)
BSF = (38.5 / (2×7.94)) × 30 × [1 - (7.94/38.5)²]
    = 2.424 × 30 × [1 - 0.04253]
    = 2.424 × 30 × 0.9575 = 69.6 Hz

// FTF (cage fault frequency)
FTF = (1/2) × 30 × (1 - 7.94/38.5) = 15 × 0.7938
    = 11.9 Hz

// Diagnostic logic:
// Envelope spectrum peaks at 107.2, 214.4, 321.6 Hz -> outer race fault
// Envelope spectrum peaks at 162.8, 325.6 Hz with +/-30Hz sidebands -> inner race fault
// Envelope spectrum peaks at 69.6, 139.2 Hz -> ball fault
// Envelope spectrum peaks at 11.9, 23.8 Hz -> cage fault

Applications

  1. Petrochemical plant pump monitoring: Centrifugal pumps are the most common rotating machinery in petrochemical plants. Vibration monitoring provides early warning for imbalance, misalignment, and bearing wear. Each pump has 2-3 accelerometers (horizontal, vertical, axial) with online monitoring systems acquiring data every second.
  2. Wind turbine main bearing: A single main bearing can cost millions of dollars, and replacement requires a large crane (even more costly). Vibration + AE (Acoustic Emission) monitoring detects early damage 3-6 months in advance.
  3. CNC spindle: High-speed spindle (30,000-60,000 RPM) bearing faults directly affect machining accuracy. Vibration monitoring is used for cutting force monitoring, tool wear detection, and spindle health management.
Pitfalls and Limitations:
  • Poor sensor mounting: Handheld probe high-frequency response only reaches 1-2 kHz, magnet reaches 3-5 kHz, stud mount reaches 10-20 kHz. Envelope analysis requires high frequencies -- mounting quality is critical.
  • Unstable speed: Speed variations cause spectral peak smearing, reducing frequency resolution. Variable frequency drive motors are particularly problematic. Solution: speed-synchronized sampling (order tracking).
  • Looking only at overall RMS, not the spectrum: Overall RMS indicates "severity" but cannot distinguish fault types. High 1X may be imbalance, high 2X may be misalignment -- the corrective actions are completely different.
  • False positive: seeing BPFO doesn't necessarily mean bearing damage: Verify the presence of modulation (BPFO harmonics in envelope spectrum + shaft speed modulation). Some structural resonance frequencies may coincidentally be close to BPFO, causing misdiagnosis.
When Not to Use FFT Vibration Analysis:
  • Very low-speed machinery (<10 RPM) -- acceleration signal is too weak. Use displacement sensors (proximity probe) or AE instead.
  • Non-rotating machinery (e.g., pressure vessels, piping) -- no clear rotational frequency. Use AE or guided wave.
  • Transient events (e.g., gear tooth breakage) -- steady-state FFT cannot capture these. Use time-frequency analysis or statistical metrics (kurtosis, crest factor).
  • Alternative: Machine Learning (ML) automatic feature extraction is replacing some traditional rule-based diagnostics. However, ML model training still requires FFT features as input -- the two are complementary, not mutually exclusive.

📝 Worked Example

Pump speed 1500RPM, gear tooth count Z=23. (a) 1X frequency? (b) Gear mesh frequency? (c) 2-second measurement, fs=25.6kHz, FFT with 4096-point Hann window, frequency resolution? (d) Can you resolve the +/-fr modulation sidebands around 1X?

Show solution

(a) fr = 1500/60 = 25Hz

(b) GMF = 23x25 = 575Hz

(c) df = 25600/4096 = 6.25Hz

(d) Sidebands at 575+/-25Hz = 550Hz and 600Hz, spacing 25Hz > df=6.25Hz -> can be resolved

✅ Quick Check

Q1: SKF 6205 bearing (9 balls, d=7.94mm, D=38.5mm) at 1800 RPM -- what is the BPFO?

Show answer

fr=30Hz, BPFO = (9/2)×30×(1-7.94/38.5) ≈ 107.1 Hz。

Q2: Why can't vibration analysis rely solely on overall RMS?

Show answer

Because RMS only reflects total energy and cannot distinguish fault types. For example, imbalance (1X) and bearing defects (BPFO) may have similar RMS values but completely different spectra.
