Interactive Lab: Signal Generator + FFT Spectrum Analyzer

Adjust parameters hands-on and observe firsthand how frequency, amplitude, noise, and window functions affect FFT results.

Signal Parameters

Experiment Guide

Experiment 1: Frequency Resolution

Set f1=50Hz, f2=55Hz (only 5Hz apart). Observe with rectangular window -- can you resolve them? Switch to Hann window? Increase data length (more N points)?

Experiment 2: Spectral Leakage

Set f1=50Hz, A1=1, turn off f2 (A2=0). Sampling rate 1024Hz, N=1024. 50Hz falls exactly on an integer bin -- no leakage. Change to f1=50.5Hz -- energy leaks to adjacent bins. How much does the Hann window improve things?

Experiment 3: Effect of Noise

Set f1=100Hz, A1=1. Gradually increase the noise level. Observe how the noise floor rises. When does the signal peak get buried?

Experiment 4: Window Function Comparison

Set f1=100Hz, A1=1, f2=105Hz, A2=0.01 (40dB difference). The rectangular window's sidelobes will mask the weak signal. Switch to Blackman window -- can you see f2 now?

Adjust parameters above to see the plot
Adjust parameters above to see the plot
Technical Details:
  • Sampling rate: 1024 Hz
  • Number of samples: 1024 points
  • Frequency resolution: Δf = fs/N = 1024/1024 = 1 Hz
  • Maximum analyzable frequency: fs/2 = 512 Hz (Nyquist)
  • Signal model: x[n] = A1sin(2πf1n/fs) + A2sin(2πf2n/fs) + noise

🧪 Guided Experiments

Experiment 1: Spectrum of a Single Sine Wave

Setup: f1=100Hz, A1=1.0, turn off f2 (A2=0), noise=0, window=Hann

Expected result: Time domain shows a clean sine wave. Spectrum has a sharp peak at 100Hz with almost no leakage nearby.

Think about: Switch to Rectangular window and observe the sidelobes (leakage) appearing around 100Hz.

Experiment 2: Two Closely Spaced Frequencies

Setup: f1=100Hz, f2=108Hz, A1=A2=1.0, noise=0

Expected result: With Hann window, you should see two separate peaks. Switch to Rectangular window -- the peaks may merge (main lobes too wide, interfering with each other).

Think about: Move f2 closer to 104Hz and observe when the two peaks become indistinguishable.

Experiment 3: Weak Signal in Noise

Setup: f1=100Hz A1=1.0, f2=200Hz A2=0.1, noise=0.5

Expected result: The 100Hz peak is clearly visible, but the 200Hz peak may be buried in noise. Switch to Blackman window (low sidelobes) -- the 200Hz peak should become easier to see.

Experiment 4: Harmonic Structure of a Square Wave

Setup: (requires changing waveform to square in the code) f1=50Hz square wave, A1=1.0

Expected result: Spectrum shows peaks at 50, 150, 250, 350... Hz (odd harmonics), with amplitudes decaying as 1/n. This is the practical verification of the Fourier series.

Self-Assessment Quiz: 20 Questions on Fourier Analysis

Covers core concepts from all six parts. Each question tests understanding, not formula memorization.

Question 1: In the Hilbert space L2[0, 2π], why can {ejnω} serve as a basis?




Explanation: A Hilbert space basis requires two conditions: (1) Orthogonality -- $\langle e^{jm\omega}, e^{jn\omega}\rangle = 2\pi\delta_{mn}$; (2) Completeness -- no nonzero function is orthogonal to all basis elements. {ejnω} satisfies both conditions, forming an orthogonal basis. Unit amplitude is just normalization, not the fundamental reason. There are many orthogonal function sets (Legendre, Hermite...), so this is not the only one.

Question 2: The Dirac delta function δ(t) is not a function in the traditional sense. What mathematical framework does it belong to?




Explanation: Laurent Schwartz's Theory of Distributions, created in the 1940s, gave δ(t) a rigorous mathematical definition. δ is a linear functional acting on test functions: $\langle \delta, \varphi \rangle = \varphi(0)$. It is not an ordinary function that is "infinite at the origin."

Question 3: What does the Uncertainty Principle tell us?




Explanation: Gabor-Heisenberg uncertainty principle: $\Delta t \cdot \Delta f \geq \frac{1}{4\pi}$. The more concentrated a signal is in time (short pulse), the more spread out its spectrum (wide bandwidth), and vice versa. This is a mathematical property of the Fourier transform, not a technical limitation. Gaussian signals achieve equality (minimum uncertainty).

Question 4: What is the fundamental difference between Fourier Series (FS) and Fourier Transform (FT)?




Explanation: A periodic signal's spectrum is discrete (values only at integer multiples of the fundamental frequency), represented by Fourier series (summation). An aperiodic signal's spectrum is continuous, represented by Fourier transform (integration). FS is not an approximation of FT -- they are different mathematical tools for different types of signals. Under the distribution theory framework, FS can be viewed as a special case of FT (the FT of a periodic signal is a train of delta functions).

Question 5: The sampling theorem requires fs > 2fmax. If a signal has a bandwidth of 100-200 Hz (bandpass signal), what is the minimum sampling rate?




Explanation: The Bandpass Sampling Theorem states that a bandpass signal only requires fs ≥ 2B (B = bandwidth), not 2fmax. Here B = 200-100 = 100Hz, so fs ≥ 200Hz suffices. However, fs must be carefully chosen so that the downconverted spectra do not overlap. For example, fs = 250Hz works, but fs = 300Hz may cause aliasing.

Question 6: DFT length N = 1024, sampling rate fs = 10 kHz. What is the frequency resolution? To resolve two frequencies 1Hz apart, what is the minimum N?




Explanation: Δf = fs/N = 10000/1024 = 9.77 Hz. To achieve 1Hz resolution: N = fs/Δf = 10000/1 = 10000. Note: zero-padding can make the spectrum appear smoother (interpolation), but does not truly improve resolution -- resolution depends only on the actual data length.

Question 7: Regarding the relationship between the Z-transform and DTFT, which is correct?




Explanation: The Z-transform $X(z) = \sum x[n]z^{-n}$ is defined on the entire z-plane (converges within the ROC). Setting z = ejω (unit circle) yields the DTFT. The prerequisite is that the ROC must include the unit circle; otherwise the DTFT does not exist (e.g., unstable systems). The Z-transform is more general than the DTFT because it can analyze unstable systems.

Question 8: You use a rectangular window for FFT analysis of a single-frequency signal. If the signal frequency falls exactly between two FFT bins (non-integer bin), what happens?




Explanation: This is the classic spectral leakage problem. A finite-length FFT essentially multiplies the signal by a rectangular window. In the frequency domain, this equals the signal spectrum convolved with a sinc function. The sinc sidelobes (-13dB) "leak" energy to bins far from the true frequency. Using a Hann window (-31.5dB sidelobes) or Blackman window (-58dB) greatly improves this.

Question 9: What is Welch's method's main advantage over direct periodogram computation? What is the cost?




Explanation: Welch's method splits long data into short segments, applies windows and FFTs to each, then averages. Averaging K segments reduces variance to 1/K. But shorter segments mean reduced frequency resolution (larger Δf). This is a tradeoff between frequency resolution and statistical stability -- not a technical limitation, but an inevitable consequence of the uncertainty principle.

Question 10: Why can the MUSIC algorithm achieve higher frequency resolution than FFT?




Explanation: MUSIC is a parametric method -- it assumes the signal consists of p sinusoids plus noise. Using eigendecomposition of the correlation matrix, it separates the signal subspace from the noise subspace. It searches for steering vectors orthogonal to the noise subspace to locate frequencies. Because it exploits the additional information from the model assumption, it can break through the Fourier resolution limit (Δf = 1/T). The cost: the number of signals p must be correctly estimated; incorrect model leads to poor results.

Question 11: What is the main use of the Hilbert transform?




Explanation: The Hilbert transform converts a real signal x(t) into an analytic signal z(t) = x(t) + jH{x(t)}, where H{} is the Hilbert transform. The analytic signal's magnitude |z(t)| = instantaneous amplitude (envelope), and the derivative of its phase arg(z(t)) = instantaneous frequency. This is extremely important in vibration analysis envelope spectra, communications AM demodulation, and speech analysis.

Question 12: In Cepstrum analysis, what does "liftering" mean?




Explanation: Cepstrum is named by reversing the first four letters of Spectrum. Similarly, liftering = reversed letters of filtering. In the quefrency domain, low quefrency corresponds to the overall spectral shape (e.g., formant envelope), while high quefrency corresponds to fine spectral structure (e.g., harmonic spacing). Liftering = truncation or windowing in the quefrency domain, selecting specific cepstral components.

Question 13: You need to analyze a 100ms chirp signal (frequency sweeping linearly from 1kHz to 5kHz). How long should the STFT window be?




Explanation: STFT window length is a tradeoff between time and frequency resolution. The chirp changes 4kHz over 100ms, rate = 40kHz/s. To track frequency changes, the window must be much shorter than the chirp length. 5-10ms window: time resolution 5-10ms (sufficient to see frequency changes), frequency resolution 100-200Hz (acceptable). 1ms window is too short: 1000Hz frequency resolution cannot discern frequency. 100ms window is equivalent to not doing STFT.

Question 14: What is CWT's (Continuous Wavelet Transform) greatest advantage over STFT?




Explanation: STFT uses a fixed window length, giving the same time-frequency resolution at all frequencies. CWT uses a scalable wavelet: when analyzing low frequencies, the wavelet automatically stretches (good frequency resolution, poor time resolution); when analyzing high frequencies, the wavelet automatically compresses (good time resolution, poor frequency resolution). This "multi-resolution" property is ideal for signals containing both slow low-frequency variations and high-frequency transients.

Question 15: In an OFDM system, what happens if the CP length is shorter than the channel delay spread?




Explanation: CP serves two functions: (1) absorb residual from the previous symbol (prevent ISI); (2) make the channel convolution equivalent to circular convolution (enabling pointwise multiplication in the FFT domain). CP too short means both functions fail, causing both ISI (inter-symbol interference) and ICI (inter-carrier interference). All subcarriers are affected, not just edge ones. This is one of the most critical parameters in OFDM system design.

Question 16: In FMCW radar, what does increasing chirp bandwidth B improve?




Explanation: Range resolution ΔR = c/(2B). B = 1GHz gives ΔR = 15cm. B = 4GHz gives ΔR = 3.75cm. Velocity resolution is determined by coherent processing interval T_CPI (Δv = λ/(2T_CPI)), independent of B. Maximum range is determined by power and chirp duration. Angular resolution is determined by the antenna array.

Question 17: An engineer sees clear 1st, 2nd, 3rd, and 4th harmonics of BPFO in the vibration envelope spectrum. What does this indicate?




Explanation: BPFO = Ball Pass Frequency, Outer race. BPFO and its harmonics appearing in the envelope spectrum indicate a localized defect on the outer race (e.g., pitting). Each time a rolling element passes over the defect, it produces an impact at the BPFO frequency. The presence of harmonics indicates the impact waveform is not a perfect sinusoid but contains harmonic content. If it were an inner race defect, BPFI would be visible, typically modulated by shaft speed (1X) since the inner race rotates with the shaft, moving the defect in and out of the load zone.

Question 18: In array signal processing, what problem does antenna spacing d > λ/2 cause? What time-domain phenomenon is it analogous to?




Explanation: The spatial Nyquist theorem requires d ≤ λ/2. Antenna spacing is the spatial sampling interval, and λ/2 is the spatial Nyquist interval. When d > λ/2, plane waves from different directions produce identical phase differences on the antenna array (just as different frequencies produce the same discrete sequence after sampling in the time domain), resulting in grating lobes = spatial aliasing. The beamformer cannot distinguish true targets from false targets in the grating lobe directions.

Question 19: Regarding HRV frequency-domain analysis, which of the following is a common misconception?




Explanation: This is the most widespread misconception in HRV analysis. The LF (0.04-0.15Hz) band is regulated by both sympathetic and parasympathetic nervous systems -- the baroreflex mechanism makes both active in this band. The 1996 Task Force report explicitly states that LF cannot be simply equated with sympathetic activity. Only HF (0.15-0.4Hz) more purely reflects parasympathetic activity (respiratory sinus arrhythmia). The LF/HF ratio can at best serve as a rough estimate of sympathetic-parasympathetic "balance."

Question 20: In OLA (Overlap-Add) fast convolution, what condition must the FFT length N satisfy? What happens if it is not met?




Explanation: OLA/OLS uses FFT to compute circular convolution. For two sequences of length L and M, their linear convolution has length L+M-1. If FFT length N < L+M-1, the circular convolution result wraps around, causing the tail and head to overlap -- this is time-domain aliasing. N ≥ L+M-1 guarantees circular convolution = linear convolution. Additionally, choosing N as a power of 2 is for efficiency (Radix-2 FFT), not a correctness requirement.

About

This learning platform is a comprehensive, graduate-level Fourier Analysis online textbook covering six major parts from mathematical foundations to engineering practice.

Course Structure

PartTopicSections
Part IMathematical Foundations4 sections
Part IICore Transforms (Four Fourier Transforms + Z Transform)6 sections
Part IIISpectral Estimation4 sections
Part IVAnalytic Signals & Cepstrum3 sections
Part VTime-Frequency Analysis5 sections
Part VIEngineering Practice10 sections

Design Philosophy

  • Intuition First: Each concept is first explained in plain language ("why"), then the formula is given, and finally a rigorous derivation is provided in expandable <details> blocks.
  • Problem-Driven: Starting from real problems -- "Why isn't the FFT on my FPGA accurate enough?" "Why does OFDM's CP need to be so long?" -- rather than from abstract definitions.
  • Engineering Connection: Every theory is accompanied by concrete industrial application examples and real-world numerical parameters.
  • Interactive Exploration: Built-in interactive charts and a lab where learners can adjust parameters and observe results firsthand.

Target Audience

  • EE / ECE / Communications / CS graduate students
  • Signal processing / DSP engineers
  • Vibration analysis / predictive maintenance engineers
  • Biomedical engineering / neuroscience researchers
  • Radar / communications system design engineers

Technical Information

  • Language: English.
  • Math typesetting: Uses the CSS class fm to mark math formulas, supporting MathJax/KaTeX rendering.
  • Interactive charts: Uses div containers with the plot class, rendered by JavaScript plotting libraries (e.g., Plotly.js, Chart.js).
